Assessed marketplace expansion scope and identified critical UX challenges
Assessed marketplace expansion scope and identified critical UX challenges
Got it. Now I have real context. Before I lay out the full plan, I need to be straight with you about the actual scope of what you're asking for — because your request is bigger than it sounds.

The honest framing
You're asking for two things at once:

A UX redesign of the existing cleaning marketplace
A pivot from single-vertical (cleaning) to multi-vertical (all home services)
Those are different problems. Doing them simultaneously is where most marketplace expansions break. I'll plan for both, but I'm going to push back on parts of it.

Critique of what's there now
Strengths:

The trust stack (background check + GPS + photos + escrow) is genuinely differentiated for cleaning
Specific personas (Airbnb hosts, retirees, professionals) is good positioning
24-hour escrow window is buyer-friendly
Weaknesses that will bite during expansion:

The trust stack is over-fitted to cleaning. Before/after photos work great for cleaning. They're useless for AC repair (internal work), awkward for plumbing, and meaningless for lawn care unless redefined. Each service type needs its own proof-of-work model.
Hourly pricing won't survive most trades. Plumbers, electricians, HVAC techs, roofers, and most painters quote per job — often after an in-person inspection. Forcing "hours worked" into those categories alienates the contractors you need.
The credits abstraction is unnecessary friction. "1 credit = $1" is a learning tax. Thumbtack, Angi, TaskRabbit, Handy — none use this pattern. It exists usually because of refund mechanics, but you can keep escrow without inventing a currency.
There's no quote/estimate flow. The current booking is "instant book." That works for cleaning. It does not work for most other home services, which need: request → contractor quotes → customer accepts → scheduled.
No visible license/insurance verification. Once you're in plumbing or electrical, this becomes a legal and safety must-have, not a nice-to-have.
Discovery is built around "browse cleaners." Won't scale to multiple categories. Needs hierarchy.
Platform recommendation
Mobile-first responsive web for customers. Native or installable PWA for contractors.

Customers book occasionally, sometimes on desktop, sometimes on phone — responsive web covers this without app-install friction
Contractors use the product daily and need GPS, camera, push notifications, offline tolerance — that's native-or-PWA territory, not regular web
Cheapest path: PWA for both, with the contractor side wrapped in a native shell later (Capacitor or Expo)
If you want a deeper dive on platform tradeoffs, I can do that separately.

Phased build plan
Phase 1 — MVP redesign (cleaning only, but architected for expansion)
The trap is to redesign for "all home services" before any of it exists. Don't. Redesign cleaning, but rebuild the data model and information architecture so multi-service drops in cleanly later.

What changes in Phase 1:

"Service Category" becomes a top-level concept in the IA. Cleaning is one of N.
New booking flow that supports two patterns: instant book (current behavior) AND request a quote (groundwork)
Drop credits. Use dollars. Keep escrow.
Redesign discover/browse to use category cards (even with one category, for now)
Redesign contractor profile to support per-service-type credentials (license, insurance, certifications, work portfolio) — even if cleaning doesn't need them all yet
What stays the same:

The current trust stack
The existing cleaning booking flow keeps working for current users
Phase 2 — Add ONE second category (proof of expansion)
Pick handyman. Reasons:

No licensing required for most handyman tasks
Hourly pricing still applies
Photos still document work
Same customer trust profile as cleaning
Do not pick plumbing or electrical first. Those need licensure flows you don't have.

Phase 3 — Quote-based services
Painting, lawn care, larger handyman jobs. Build the full request-quote-accept flow.

Phase 4 — Licensed trades
Plumbing, electrical, HVAC, roofing. Add license/insurance verification system, surety, possibly state-specific compliance.

Core user flows to design (Phase 1)
Customer flows
First-time onboarding (3 screens)
Browse / discover services (1–3 screens depending on entry point)
Book a service — instant path (4 screens)
Request a quote — new path (4 screens)
In-progress job tracking (1 live-status screen)
Approve & pay (1 screen, with photo review)
Dispute flow (2 screens)
History & reviews (1–2 screens)
Contractor flows
Onboarding (5+ screens — identity, background check, payouts, services offered, credentials)
Job inbox / available work (1 screen)
Accept job / submit quote (2 screens)

Earnings & payouts (1 screen)
Profile management (multi-tab)
Shared / system

Notifications center
Profile & settings

New homepage / category landing

Contractor onboarding (multi-step, the credentials collection screen specifically)
Everything else is a variant of these.

Open questions before I start wireframing
These actually matter for the design:

Existing users? Is there a live user base I shouldn't disrupt, or is the product still early enough for an aggressive redesign?
Are you the designer? This affects how polished I make the wireframes vs. leaving them as scaffolding for someone else to refine.
Tech stack? Affects PWA vs. native cost, and whether I should design with a specific component library in mind.

Architected cleaning-focused redesign strategy and identified key UX priorities
Architected cleaning-focused redesign strategy and identified key UX priorities
Quick note: my fetcher returned the homepage content for /book too. That means either the actual booking UI is rendered in JavaScript (which the fetcher can't see) or the product is still partially marketing-only. Refocused critique (cleaning only)
The cleaning category has a brutal truth: it's a trust purchase first, a price purchase second. People are letting a stranger into their home. Every design decision must serve that.

What the current site gets wrong for that goal:

Trust is described, not shown. "Background-checked" and "GPS-verified" are listed as bullets. They should be felt — visualized, demonstrated, proven with real elements (the actual GPS pin, the actual photo gallery, real cleaner faces with real ratings).
No social proof on the front door. No review count, no testimonials, no "10,000 cleanings completed," no city coverage badge. Cleaning marketplaces live and die by social proof.
Booking isn't visible. A homepage for a service marketplace should let me start booking from the hero — at minimum, ZIP code + date + cleaning type. Three fields. Right there. Today the user has to navigate to "Book a Cleaning."
The cleaner is invisible on the homepage. No faces. No names. No ratings. The product is people, but the homepage shows none of them.
"Credits" is still friction. Even staying in cleaning-only, this teaches users a system they don't need. Use dollars, keep escrow.
The four cleaning types are hidden in a paragraph. They should be the primary visual on the page — four cards with photos, scope, time estimates, and starting prices.
No transparent pricing on the front. Saying "$25–60/hour" requires a click. People shopping for a cleaner want to see the dollar number on screen one.
Design principles for the redesign
These are the rules I'd hold every screen to:

Show, don't tell trust. Replace bullet lists with real evidence: maps, photos, reviews, names, badges with real meaning.
Booking starts on screen one. The hero CTA is a real input, not a "Get Started" button.
The cleaner is the product. Humanize them everywhere. Photo, name, rating, jobs completed, response time, neighborhoods served.
Pricing is always visible. Hourly rate, total estimate, fees if any — never hidden behind a click.
Speed is felt. The "60 seconds" promise needs to actually feel that fast in the flow. Each step should fit on one screen with minimal typing.
Photo proof is the hero feature. Design the post-cleaning approval screen like an Instagram-quality gallery with side-by-side before/after sliders. This is your differentiator — make it look like one.
Screens to redesign (cleaning-only)
This is the focused list. Roughly in priority order.

Customer-facing
Homepage / landing — booking starts here, social proof visible, cleaner faces shown
Cleaning type selection — four cards (Standard, Deep, Move-Out, Airbnb) with scope, time, photo, starting price
Booking flow — date/time, hours, address, special instructions, optional cleaner pick (4 steps, ideally one screen each, no scroll)
Cleaner profile / browse — photo, name, bio, ratings, recent jobs with photos, neighborhoods, badges
Live job tracker — "Cleaner is on the way" with map, ETA, check-in confirmation
Approve & pay — the before/after photo gallery review (this is your Instagram moment)
Customer dashboard — upcoming bookings, recent jobs, rebook with one tap, saved cleaners
Dispute flow — clean, calm, not buried (trust matters even when something goes wrong)
Cleaner-facing
Cleaner onboarding — identity, background check status, payouts, service area, rates, scheduling
Job inbox — incoming bookings, accept/decline, available jobs nearby
Active job screen — the GPS check-in, photo capture (room-by-room), check-out
Earnings dashboard — completed jobs, pending payout, instant payout option
Shared / system
In-app messaging — between customer and cleaner (currently missing, frequently needed: gate codes, parking, pets, supplies)
Notifications
Profile / settings
That's 15 screens. Roughly half are unique designs; the rest are variants or simpler views.

Architected mobile homepage wireframe with booking-first layout and trust signals
Architected mobile homepage wireframe with booking-first layout and trust signals
Good — clean answers. That unlocks real work. Quick context-set, then I'll get into it.

Setup confirmed. No users to migrate, solo founder/designer/dev means my wireframes need to double as implementation guides — clear enough that you can hand them to yourself and build without guessing. I'll annotate where it matters.

The credits decision (the one that actually matters)
Your cash-flow instinct is sound. Pre-paid credits = customer money on your books before service is performed = working capital. That's a real lever. But there's a path to the same float without making customers learn a virtual currency.

You already get most of the float from "pay at booking with escrow."

When a customer pays at booking time:

Money lands on the platform (or in a Stripe Connect escrow setup) immediately
It's released to the cleaner only when the customer approves the job (your existing 24-hour review)
Float window = booking time → approval time. For a cleaning booked Friday for next Tuesday, that's 4+ days of float per transaction. For weekly Airbnb turnovers, it's days × volume.
That's not as much float as bulk pre-purchase, but it's substantial — and it scales linearly with bookings, no virtual currency required.

What credits actually add over pay-at-booking:

Bulk pre-purchase before any specific job exists (true upfront cash injection)
Stickiness — people with a balance book more often
A built-in discount lever ("$200 buys $220 in credits")
What credits cost you:

First-booking friction. The user who's already nervous about letting a stranger into their home now also has to buy a currency before they can book. That's a conversion killer.
Looks like a gimmick at first impression. Airbnb, Thumbtack, TaskRabbit, Uber, DoorDash, Tidy, Handy — none use credits as the default purchase mechanism. There's a reason.
Regulatory load. Stored value can trigger money transmitter licensing in some states and escheat (unclaimed property) laws. Real legal/accounting overhead for a solo founder.
Refund psychology. "Store credit only" feels worse than a real refund when something goes wrong.
Accounting complexity — deferred revenue recognition, GAAP timing.
My recommendation: hybrid model.

Default flow → pay in dollars at booking, escrow until approval. Zero friction for the first-time customer (who is exactly the user you need to convert at launch). Stripe Connect supports this natively with manual capture or delayed transfer.
Power-user layer → "PureTask Credit Packs" as Phase 2. Airbnb hosts who book multiple cleanings per week, families on weekly schedules — they self-select into bulk pre-purchase with a discount. They get the stickiness benefit, you get the cash injection, and the average first-time customer never has to think about it.
This gets you 80% of the cash-flow upside, 100% of the conversion benefits, and lets you defer the regulatory and accounting complexity until you've validated demand.

I'm proceeding with the dollars-default model in the wireframes. Push back if you want me to wireframe the credits-required flow instead.

On to wireframes
Mobile-first, since cleaning bookings overwhelmingly happen on phone. I'll do desktop variations after we lock the mobile structure. Starting with the homepage — it's the highest-leverage screen since it's where conversion lives or dies.

Here's the mobile homepage. Wireframe-fidelity — structure and information hierarchy locked, visual polish deliberately bare so we can iterate on layout decisions without getting distracted by aesthetics.

V

Connecting to visualize...
What this changes vs. the current site
Booking starts on screen one. The hero isn't a wall of text — it's a working tool. Three fields, one CTA. Everything below supports the decision.

Trust is shown, not claimed. The verification stack moved from a paragraph list to four visual tiles right where the user makes the trust decision. Same content, ten times the visual weight.

Cleaners are visible from the homepage. Real faces, real names, real ratings, scrollable. This single change pulls the product out of "service description" territory and into "people you can hire." It also lets first-time customers preview supply density before they commit — which is critical for marketplace credibility at launch.

Service types are cards, not paragraphs. Photo + name + duration + starting price. The 60-second promise becomes plausible because the user can see what they're picking.

Photo proof gets its own section. Right now your single biggest differentiator is buried in a bullet list. In the redesign it becomes a visual section with sample before/afters. This is what makes you not look like every other cleaning service.

Implementation notes (since you're the dev too)
The hero booking widget is the conversion lever. Build it as a sticky/persistent component you can reuse on category pages and cleaner profiles. One source of truth for the booking-start state.
The "Top cleaners near you" row should pull from a featured_cleaners query — top-rated within a radius, geolocation-based if location permission granted, ZIP-based fallback. For launch with thin supply, hand-pick the top 3 nightly via an admin flag. Don't try to algorithmically sort 5 cleaners.
The cleaning type cards should link to a category detail page (next wireframe), not directly to checkout. Let users compare cleaners within a category before committing.
Real photos matter more than copy here. Budget for a small day-of-launch shoot — 2–3 actual cleaners and the four cleaning types in real homes. Stock photos on a homepage like this kill conversion in the first 5 seconds.
For social proof, the two review cards in the design need to be real, with real names and real cleanings. Fake reviews are easy to spot and torch your trust story instantly.
Things I deliberately left out
A pricing page link in the hero. Sending users away from the booking widget is a leak. Pricing lives in the cleaning type cards.
"As seen in" press logos. Premature for launch.
A FAQ accordion on the homepage. Adds vertical real estate without converting. FAQs live on /help until you have data showing what users actually ask.
A coverage map / cities list. Important, but better as its own page linked from footer until you have multi-city presence.
What's next
Three options for wireframe #2:

Cleaning type detail page — what happens when someone taps a cleaning type card. Shows what's included in that service, lists available cleaners, lets them pick. This is the natural click-through from the homepage.
Booking flow — the multi-step booking process (date/time confirmation, address details, special instructions, cleaner selection, payment, confirmation).
Cleaner profile page — what a customer sees when they tap a cleaner from the homepage row. Photo gallery, bio, ratings, recent jobs with before/after photos, calendar.
I'd recommend the cleaning type detail page next, since it's the natural click path from this homepage and locks the discovery → selection flow before we design the actual booking transaction.

Reaction? What's working, what's wrong, what should change before I move on?

Excavated design flaws and strategic gaps systematically
Excavated design flaws and strategic gaps systematically
Good push. Looking at it cold, the wireframe is a decent v0.5 but the plan has real problems I glossed over. Let me actually critique it instead of admiring it.

What's actually strong
The credits analysis and the hybrid recommendation hold up. That section is solid.
Putting booking on the hero is correct.
Surfacing real cleaners on the homepage is correct.
Photo proof getting its own section is correct.
That's the keepable spine. The rest needs work.

What's actually weak in the wireframe
1. The booking widget is incomplete. I have three fields — Address, Service, When — but "When" is silently doing two jobs (date AND time). Cleaning bookings need both because cleaners have hour-by-hour availability. Either I commit to four fields in the hero, or "When" gets pushed to the next page. My call: drop date/time from the hero entirely. Two fields (Address, Service) → "See cleaners and prices." Date/time pickers work better on the cleaner-list page where users can see who's actually free.

2. The select fields look like static labels. "Standard" and "Tomorrow" sitting in input boxes with no caret indicator look like they're filled in and locked. Real users will miss they're tappable. Need a clearer dropdown affordance or different visual treatment.

3. Trust strip is in the wrong place. I put it AFTER the booking widget. By the time someone fills the widget, they've already trusted you enough to type their address. Trust signals belong before or woven into the conversion ask, not after it. Fix: move two trust signals into the hero subhead area as micro-pills, drop the standalone strip.

4. "Top cleaners near you" is dishonest before location is given. The user hasn't entered a ZIP. I can't claim "near you." Fix: rename to "Top-rated cleaners on PureTask" with city tags on each card. Once ZIP is entered elsewhere, that section can update.

5. "How it works" is the weakest section in the whole design. Numbered circles with two-line descriptions is the default AI-landing-page pattern. Every SaaS site has this. Fix: replace with four mini-illustrations — a booking-widget snippet, a cleaner card, a map-plus-photo combo, a photo gallery with checkmark. Show the product in each step instead of describing it.

6. Reviews section is decoration, not proof. No star visuals, no aggregate rating count, no cleaner attribution, no service type filter. Fix: add a header strip with "4.9 average across 1,247 cleanings" plus a star-rating row, then show real reviews with the cleaner's name credited ("Cleaned by Maria L.") — that doubles as social proof for cleaner sign-ups.

7. Cleaning type cards in 2x2 grid are too small. At 360px wide each card photo is roughly 150x95. The category image is the visual anchor for the buying decision. Fix: stack as 1-column on mobile with bigger photos. 2x2 returns at desktop breakpoint.

8. No price preview after service selection. If the user picks "Deep clean" in the hero widget, the next CTA could read "See cleaners from $120" instead of generic "See cleaners and prices." Reduces surprise, sets price anchor early.

What's strategically missing from the plan
9. I never gave you an information architecture. I wireframed a screen before showing you the sitemap. That's backward. You need to see all the pages and how they connect before we keep building screens. Should fix this next, before any more wireframes.

10. I assumed mobile-first without checking the personas. Airbnb hosts manage on desktop. Seniors book on desktop. Busy professionals book during the workday on desktop. Mobile-first responsive is still probably right, but I should have shown you a desktop variant too — or at least flagged where the layout adapts.

11. I treated cleaners like a CTA tile. They're 50% of the marketplace. They need their own landing page (/cleaners or /apply) with cleaner-focused conversion design — earnings calculator, payout schedule, demo of the cleaner app, requirements checklist. The bottom-of-homepage tile is fine as a touchpoint, but it's not a strategy.

12. No empty / loading / error states. What does the homepage show in a city where you have zero cleaners? What does the cleaner row show when supply is thin? Designing only for the happy path is the most common pre-launch mistake — I made it.

13. No urgency or live-marketplace signals. Marketplaces should feel alive. Missing: aggregate counters ("12 cleanings booked today"), live availability badges on cleaner cards ("Available this afternoon"), recency indicators ("Booked 4 hours ago"). Without these, the page feels static.

14. No local SEO architecture. Service marketplaces live and die by city + service combo pages. "House cleaning in San Francisco." "Move-out cleaning Brooklyn." This affects information architecture, not just visual design — we should plan for it now, not retrofit later.

15. No placeholder for the future credit packs. I said "Phase 2" but didn't reserve a visual home. When credit packs ship, do they live in account settings? A "save with credits" upsell strip? Plan now, design later.

What I'd change first (priority order)
Stop wireframing screens and draw the IA. One sitemap diagram showing all pages, their hierarchy, and primary user flows. Lock this before building more screens.
Fix the homepage wireframe — drop date/time from hero, fix select affordances, move trust strip, rename cleaner row, redo "how it works," upgrade reviews. I'll redo it.
Add desktop variant — a parallel desktop wireframe so you can see how the design responds.
Plan the cleaner-side landing page — separate strategy doc + wireframe, not a CTA tile.
Plan the empty/loading/error states for each screen — checklist per page, not after-the-fact.
SEO architecture — what city / service-type pages exist, what URL patterns, what content each needs.
What I'd recommend doing right now
Two paths:

Path A — Fix and continue. I redo the homepage wireframe with the fixes above, then draw the IA, then continue with the cleaning type detail page. Slower but clean.

Path B — IA first, then re-wireframe. I draw the full information architecture (sitemap + key user flows) FIRST, then redo the homepage knowing where everything lives, then proceed. Faster long-term, less rework.

I'd recommend Path B. Drawing the IA usually surfaces 2–3 more problems with the homepage that I'd otherwise discover one wireframe at a time. Better to find them now.

Which path do you want? Or push back on any of these critiques — some of them might not matter for your launch.

Claude responded: Good call.

Orchestrated content structure and strategic planning framework
Orchestrated content structure and strategic planning framework
Good call. I'll build the IA, then come back to the homepage with that as scaffolding.

This response covers the architecture in three pieces: a customer-side sitemap, a cleaner-side sitemap, and the primary booking flow. Then SEO architecture in text. Then I'll surface decisions you need to lock before I redo the homepage.

Here's the full information architecture in three pieces — customer side, cleaner side, then the primary booking flow that ties them together. After all three, I'll get into SEO architecture and the decisions you need to lock before I redo the homepage.


Three decisions in this sitemap that matter for the homepage redesign:

Service category pages and city+service SEO pages are siblings, not parent/child. That's deliberate. A service category page (e.g., "Deep cleaning") is what you link to from the homepage — same content for every visitor. A city+service page (e.g., "Deep cleaning in San Francisco") is the SEO landing — same content but localized headline, local cleaners, local pricing. Both are public, both look similar, but they live at different URLs and serve different traffic. Build them from the same component with different data props.

Marketing pages is a single bucket (About, Pricing, Help, Reviews, Coverage). They live in the footer, not the main nav. Putting them in the main nav would dilute the conversion path. Pricing especially — keep it in the footer for the curious, but pricing transparency lives inside the cleaning type cards on the homepage where buyers actually look for it.

The authenticated dashboard is the anchor for everything post-signup. Not the booking flow, not the active job. The reason: a returning customer's most common action is rebooking a cleaner they liked. Dashboard → Saved cleaners → Rebook should be one tap. The booking flow is for first-time bookings; the dashboard is for everything after.

Now the cleaner side. This is where the product gets technically more demanding — these pages need GPS, camera, and offline tolerance.


Three things on the cleaner side that affect the homepage redesign:

The cleaner side is its own product. Five public pages and eight authenticated screens. The "For cleaners" link on the customer homepage isn't a CTA tile — it's the entry to a parallel funnel with its own conversion design. That's why it gets a dedicated landing page (/cleaners or /become-a-cleaner) with cleaner-focused content: earnings calculator, payout schedule, requirements, demo of the cleaner app. The cleaner-acquisition story is "make money on your own schedule with weekly payouts" — totally different from the customer-acquisition story.

The cleaner app is field software, not desktop software. GPS check-in, photo capture, push notifications, sometimes offline (basements, garages, weak signal). This is why I keep recommending PWA or native for the cleaner side. The customer side can be pure responsive web; the cleaner side needs more.

Background check status is its own page, not a settings field. Reason: a cleaner whose check expired or is pending sees a blocking screen with clear next steps, not a buried toggle. This screen also handles re-screening (annual renewal). Treat verification as a first-class part of the cleaner app.

Now the primary booking flow — the conversion path the homepage funnels into.


The blue boxes are pre-payment, the teal boxes are post-payment. The wrap arrow is "time passes" — from the cleaner accepting the booking to actually arriving on site. That gap could be hours (booked for tomorrow), minutes (instant book), or days (booked for next week).

Five flow decisions worth flagging before the homepage redesign:

The homepage hero collects only address + service. Date/time selection happens on the Cleaner list page where users can see who's actually available. This was the fix I called out in my self-critique — committing to it now means the homepage hero stays at two fields, not three or four.

Account creation is gated at "Pay and confirm," not earlier. The user can browse cleaners, build a booking, and get to the payment screen as a guest. Only at the moment of payment do we ask them to sign up (or offer guest checkout with email-only). This is the standard ecommerce pattern — every gate before payment is friction that bleeds conversion.

Cleaner profile (not in this flow diagram) is a side branch off Cleaner list. Some users will tap a cleaner to look closer, then return to the list. Others will book directly from the list. Both paths must work — the cleaner profile is optional, not mandatory.

The "Active job" screen is the customer's live tracker. Same data as the cleaner-side active-job screen, but presented for the customer's purpose: where is the cleaner now, did they check in, what photos have been uploaded so far. This is a real-time read-only view of what the cleaner is doing — and it's where the GPS/photo trust story actually plays out. This screen is the differentiator made tangible.

The "Review" step at the end is optional but prompted aggressively. Reviews are how you build the social proof your homepage depends on. Treat the review prompt as a first-class screen with a friction-free 1-to-5 tap, optional written review, and option to save the cleaner. Don't make it a buried email link.

SEO architecture
This is the part most marketplace founders skip and regret. Service marketplaces live or die on SEO traffic — people searching "house cleaning [city]" or "deep cleaning [neighborhood]" who land on your page and convert. You need to plan the URL structure now because it shapes the homepage component architecture.

Recommended URL patterns:

/ — homepage (national, broadly targeted)
/cleaning/[service] — service category page (e.g., /cleaning/standard, /cleaning/deep)
/cleaning/[city] — city landing page (e.g., /cleaning/san-francisco)
/cleaning/[service]/[city] — combo SEO page (e.g., /cleaning/deep/san-francisco)
/cleaner/[slug] — public cleaner profile (e.g., /cleaner/maria-l-sf)
/discover — browse cleaners (filterable list)
Why combo pages matter. The keyword "deep cleaning San Francisco" gets searched by people who already know they want deep cleaning in SF. They're closer to converting than someone searching just "house cleaning." A page that exactly matches their search intent — local cleaners, local prices, local headline, local reviews — converts better than a generic homepage.

How to build them efficiently. All combo pages are the SAME component as the homepage, with three things swapped:

Headline ("Deep cleaning in San Francisco" vs. "House cleaning you can trust")
Cleaner list filtered to the city
Reviews filtered to the city + service combo
Build the homepage as a composable component now and you get all combo pages for free later. Build the homepage as a one-off and you'll rewrite it in 6 months when you start ranking.

What to launch with vs. defer. At launch, start with / and 4 service category pages (/cleaning/standard, /cleaning/deep, /cleaning/move-out, /cleaning/airbnb-turnover). City and combo pages come once you have supply in 2+ cities. Don't generate hundreds of combo pages for cities you don't serve — that's spam to Google and gets you penalized.

Decisions you need to make before I redo the homepage
These affect structure, not just visuals. Lock these and I can re-wireframe with confidence.

What's next
Once you answer those four, I'll redo the homepage wireframe with:

The fixes from my self-critique (hero fields, trust placement, cleaner row name, "how it works" replaced with product peeks, reviews upgraded with star+aggregate, cleaning type cards stacked single-column on mobile, price preview after service selection)
The architecture lessons from this IA (pricing in cleaning type cards, marketing in footer, dashboard-anchored returning-user logic)
The platform decision baked in
The launch geography reflected in headline + cleaner row logic
A desktop variant alongside mobile so you can see how it adapts
After the homepage, the natural next screen is the Cleaner list page (since it's the next stop in the booking flow and where date/time selection happens). We'll get there step by step.

Push back on any of the IA above if something doesn't match how you want the product to work. The architecture is easier to argue with now than after we've built screens on top of it.

Claude responded: Good.

Platform direction (my call)
Mobile-first for customers, dedicated PWA for cleaners.

Reasoning:

Customer side as responsive web — cleaning bookings are infrequent enough that asking customers to install an app is a conversion killer. Responsive web with PWA "add to home screen" prompt for repeat customers covers the use case. No app store friction, instant SEO traffic from city/service combo pages, fast iteration.
Cleaner side as PWA — cleaners use the product daily. They need GPS check-in, camera capture, push notifications for new jobs, offline tolerance for basements/garages with weak signal. PWA gets you 90% of native capability without iOS/Android dev cost — critical when you're solo. Real native (Capacitor wrapper) becomes a Phase 2 upgrade once you have 50+ active cleaners and revenue to justify it.
Why not mobile-first responsive web for both? Cleaners need home screen presence, push notifications, and reliable camera/GPS access. Pure responsive web makes these clunky. PWA is a small step beyond responsive that buys real product capability.
Why not split with native cleaner app? You're solo. Building and maintaining an iOS app, Android app, and web frontend is a full team's worth of work. PWA is the same codebase as web with a manifest file, service worker, and a few API integrations. You can ship and iterate in the same sprint.
Tech stack implication: Build with Next.js or similar SSR framework. SSR matters for the SEO architecture (city/service combo pages need to render server-side for Google). Then layer PWA features on top of the cleaner-facing routes.

Launch geography lock-in
Northern California: San Francisco to Reno. That's a wide corridor — roughly the I-80 spine. Two strategic implications:

Headline gets more specific. Not "house cleaning you can trust" — too generic. Try: "Trusted house cleaning across Northern California." Names the geography, builds trust, anchors search intent.
City+service SEO pages launch with a defined list. Day-one combo pages worth building:
SF, Oakland, Berkeley, San Jose, Palo Alto, Walnut Creek
Sacramento, Davis, Roseville, Folsom
Reno, Sparks
Plus the four service types
That's roughly 50 combo pages from one component template. Don't write them all at launch — start with SF + Sacramento + Reno × all four services = 12 combo pages. Expand as you gain supply.

Hero confirmation
Two fields locked: address + service. Date/time moves to the cleaner list page.

Cleaner row strategy
"Top-rated cleaners on PureTask" with placeholder cards until launch supply exists. Showing the section structure now, hand-curating top 3 once you have real cleaners.

But I'm going to push back on something: an empty placeholder row at launch is worse than no row. It signals "no supply" to a first-time visitor. Two options that fix this:

Option A — soft launch beta. Hide the cleaner row until you have at least 3 verified cleaners on the platform. Replace with a "Joining soon: top-rated cleaners across NorCal" tile that links to a waitlist. Honest, doesn't fake supply.
Option B — recruit your seed cleaners before public launch. Get 5-10 cleaners verified and live before the homepage redesign goes public. The homepage is the demand-side hook; without cleaners, it generates traffic you can't convert.
I'd recommend Option B. The redesign matters less than having actual supply on day one. If you launch the homepage with no cleaners visible, customers bounce, you don't get reviews, the marketplace never starts.

For now I'll wireframe the cleaner row with realistic placeholder content (gray skeleton state with "Loading top-rated cleaners…" or hand-curated when ready) so the layout is correct either way.

May 2
Claude responded: Excellent — you're now thinking like a real product designer.
Excellent — you're now thinking like a real product designer. These are the right questions and you're catching nuances most founders miss. Let me address each piece honestly, including where I think your strategy is strong and where it has problems.

On the "I'm on the way" idea
This is a strong instinct and you're right to pull back from constant tracking. Constant tracking is a privacy problem (and a battery problem for the cleaner). Constant tracking also has zero customer value — nobody is watching a dot drive on a map for 45 minutes.

Better mental model: tracking is event-driven, not continuous.

Three GPS events that actually matter:

"On my way" — cleaner taps the button. Push notification + ETA appear for customer. Location ping every ~3 minutes from this point. Stops on arrival.
"Arrived" — cleaner taps to clock in. Geo-fence check confirms they're at the address (within ~50m). Single GPS verification, not ongoing.
"Job complete" — cleaner clocks out. Final geo-fence check. Tracking ends.
Between those events, no GPS. The cleaner's phone is just a phone. This is honest, respects the cleaner's autonomy, drains less battery, and avoids creeping out customers who'd rather not feel like they're surveilling someone.

There's a real risk here you should be aware of: GPS-spoofing. A bad-actor cleaner could fake location to clock in without arriving. Mitigations: cross-check IP geolocation against GPS, require a photo within 5 minutes of clock-in, flag patterns where check-in time and first-photo timestamp are far apart. None are perfect — your real defense is the rating system catching it after the fact.

On clock-in and clock-out
Solid. Couple of refinements:

Clock-in should be gated by geo-fence. If they tap "Arrived" but they're not within ~50m of the address, the button shouldn't work — show "You don't appear to be at the location yet" with manual override after 3 failed attempts (in case GPS is wonky in dense urban or rural areas). Manual overrides get logged for fraud review.

Clock-in should require a "before" photo of at least one room within 5 minutes. Otherwise you have cleaners who clock in, drive away, come back, and clock out — collecting time that isn't real work. The first before-photo is the proof of presence.

Clock-out should require all key-room "after" photos uploaded. Don't let them clock out until photos exist. This is a hard gate. Before vs. after is meaningless if half the after photos are missing.

Pause / break tracking is optional Phase 2. For long deep cleans, cleaners take legitimate breaks. Don't worry about this at launch.

On key rooms for photos
Suggested defaults by service type — these aren't arbitrary, they're the rooms with the highest dispute rate in cleaning marketplaces:

Standard cleaning: Kitchen (counters, sink, stovetop), Main bathroom (toilet, shower, sink), Living room (visible surfaces, floor), Master bedroom (made bed, floor)
Deep clean: All Standard rooms + Inside oven, Inside fridge, Baseboards in one room, Behind one major appliance
Move-out: All Standard rooms + Inside oven, Inside fridge, Empty closets in each bedroom, Inside cabinets (kitchen + bath)
Airbnb turnover: Kitchen, All bathrooms, All bedrooms (made beds), Living room, Trash status
Required vs. optional: Mark 4-5 rooms as required for each service type. Cleaner can add more rooms voluntarily — encourage this with a "Add another room" tappable card that grows the gallery. More photos = better dispute defense for the cleaner. Frame it as protective, not bureaucratic.

Photo guidance is a launch must-have. Cleaners are not photographers. Show a small "before" example image when they tap to capture a kitchen, with text like "Wide shot, counter visible." Generic phone-camera photos with bad lighting and weird angles undermine your trust story. A 30-second photo coaching screen during onboarding pays for itself in dispute reduction.

On tipping placement
This is a sharper question than you realize and you're already 70% of the way to the right answer. Let me sharpen it the rest of the way.

Your instinct is correct: tip after the review, not at approval. Here's why, and here's what to do exactly.

Three sub-points:

Approval is a financial decision. Tipping at approval pollutes it. When the customer is reviewing photos and deciding whether to release payment, asking for a tip in the same flow makes them anchor on "did I get my money's worth?" not "was this great?" Splitting the moments separates judgment from generosity.
Reviews are the proxy for satisfaction. Tip ride alongside the review, not next to the dollar. The flow should be: Approve & Pay → "Thanks, payment released" → Review prompt (1-5 stars + optional written review) → If 4+ stars, tip suggestion appears with one-tap presets ($5, $10, 20%, custom). Below 4 stars, no tip prompt — instead, "Anything we should know?" channels into dispute or feedback flow.
Make tipping low-friction or skip it. Three preset chips and a "no tip" option. No guilt copy, no default-selected tip, no "your cleaner can see if you tip." Pre-selected tips are a dark pattern that shows up in tipping research as actively reducing customer trust over time.
Industry data point worth knowing: TaskRabbit, Handy, and similar platforms saw measurable rating drops when they introduced default-on tip pre-selection. Customers felt manipulated and downrated the platform itself, not the worker. Don't make this mistake. Earn the tip.

One more thing. Make sure the cleaner sees tip notifications immediately and that 100% of tips pass through to them (not the platform). Some platforms quietly take a cut of tips. Don't. It's a trust killer when cleaners find out.

On rescheduling and cancellation policy
This is where I have to push back hardest. Your strategy has good intuition but a few critical problems.

Your stated policy:

Customer pays full price if they cancel within 24 hours
Reschedule allowed within 24 hours (your question)
Reschedule = request to other party, who can accept or deny; deny → original time stands
Where this breaks down:

Problem 1: "Customer pays full price for cancellation within 24 hours" is correct policy direction, but the time window is wrong for the cleaning industry. Cleanings are usually booked 2-7 days out. A 24-hour cancellation window is too short to protect cleaners. Industry standard is 24h for the lighter penalty, 48h or more for the harder one. Recommended structure:

Cancel 48+ hours before: free cancellation, full refund
Cancel 24-48 hours before: 50% charge (cleaner gets the 50%)
Cancel <24 hours before: 100% charge (cleaner gets ~75%, you keep platform fee)
No-show: 100% charge + reliability score hit on customer
Problem 2: Allowing reschedule within 24 hours without penalty creates a loophole. A customer who wants to dodge the cancellation fee will just "reschedule" to a placeholder date and never rebook. You've now lost the cleaner's blocked time AND you can't charge. Fix: reschedule within 24 hours follows the same penalty schedule as cancellation, OR is allowed once free per booking and subsequent reschedules incur the cancellation fee.

Problem 3: The "request → other party accepts/denies → revert to original" pattern is too symmetric. It treats both sides as equally able to absorb the disruption. They aren't. A customer asking to reschedule from Tuesday to Thursday might cost the cleaner nothing if they're flexible — but a cleaner asking to reschedule a 9 AM cleaning to 3 PM might wreck the customer's whole day. Different parties, different costs.

Better model:

Customer-initiated reschedule: If 48+ hours out, free, single-tap, cleaner notified (not approval-gated). If <48 hours, becomes a request that cleaner can accept (free) or decline (revert to original). If <24 hours, treated as cancellation under penalty schedule above.
Cleaner-initiated reschedule: Always a request to customer, regardless of timing. Cleaner gets a strike on their reliability score every time they initiate a reschedule. Three strikes in 90 days = automatic review and possible suspension. Cleaners rescheduling on customers must be friction-heavy, not friction-free, because customer trust is more fragile than cleaner schedule.
Problem 4: "Deny → revert to original" sounds clean but has a sharp edge. Imagine: customer requests reschedule, cleaner declines, customer no-shows out of spite. Now what? You need a side rule: "If you request a reschedule that's denied, you can still cancel under the standard penalty schedule." Don't let the system trap people into showing up out of obligation when they've already decided not to.

Problem 5: You haven't addressed weather, illness, or emergencies. Both sides will have legitimate emergencies. Build a "Mark as emergency" option that requires a brief reason and bypasses penalty — but flag it for review. Abusive use (more than 1 in 6 months) = the option disappears for that user. Cleaning marketplaces that don't have this end up with angry customers stuck paying for cleanings they couldn't help missing, and angry cleaners who got sick and can't afford to lose the wage.

Other insights I think you're missing
1. Photo confidentiality matters more than you think. Customers are letting you photograph the inside of their home. The terms of service must clearly state photos are stored encrypted, used only for dispute resolution and the customer's own review, and deleted after a defined window (suggest 90 days). Make this prominent in the booking flow. Some customers will refuse to book if this is unclear.

2. The customer needs to be able to opt OUT of photos for sensitive areas. Home offices with confidential papers visible. Children's bedrooms. Personal items. Build a "skip this room" flag during booking with a free-text note ("Office is locked — please don't enter"). The cleaner sees these notes before starting. Skipped rooms still get logged but no photo required.

3. The cleaner needs a way to flag issues they encounter. Mid-job: "Found unexpected damage", "Owner changed scope", "Pet not in crate as agreed". These need to be a one-tap flag that pauses the timer and pings both parties. Without this, cleaners eat the cost of scope creep and disputes get nasty.

4. Two-tap rebooking from customer dashboard. A returning customer should be able to rebook the same cleaner for the same service in two taps. This is the real lifetime-value lever for your marketplace. Most marketplaces buried this. Make it the most prominent action on the customer dashboard.

5. Cleaner-side: "Ready for next job" status. Cleaner finishes a job at 11 AM with nothing else booked until 2 PM. Lets them mark themselves available to take a same-day request. Powerful for fill-rate, especially on Airbnb turnovers.

6. You haven't said anything about insurance. Cleaners breaking things in customer homes happens. You need either (a) a platform-level liability policy that covers $X per incident, or (b) a clear policy that cleaners are responsible and a process for claim. Most platforms do (a) with a $500-1000 deductible and a low cap. Talk to a lawyer before launch — this is one of the most-likely-to-bite-you items on the entire build.

Updated booking lifecycle
Reflecting all the decisions above, here's the corrected flow that the cleaner-app and customer-app screens need to support.


Three things to notice in this lifecycle:

Color = role. Blue is pre-job state. Amber is the cleaner working. Teal is the approval moment. Purple is post-payment. The user (customer or cleaner) always knows which "phase" they're in.

GPS only runs in the amber zone. From "On my way" through "Clock out." Before that, the cleaner is just going about their day. After that, payment processing handles itself.

Tip is downstream of review, not bundled with payment. Review-then-tip catches happy customers when their satisfaction is fresh. Payment moment stays clean and judgment-free.

What's next
Three options for the next round of design:

Cleaner active-job screen wireframe — the most complex screen in the whole product. Includes the "On my way" button, geo-fence clock-in, photo capture grid with required rooms, room-by-room progress, clock-out, and the issue-flag escape hatch.
Customer approve & pay screen wireframe — what the customer sees when reviewing the job. Photo gallery, clock times, before/after pairs, approve button, dispute path.
Reschedule/cancel policy screens — both customer-side and cleaner-side. The penalty schedule, the request flow, the emergency override.
I'd recommend option 1 next. It's the highest-complexity screen and locks in the cleaner-side product reality. Once that's solid, the customer approval screen mirrors it, and the reschedule flows are straightforward modal patterns we can lay on top.

Reaction? Anything in my pushback on cancellation policy or tipping you want to argue with — that's the right time to do it, before I wireframe the screens that bake those decisions in.

May 2
Claude responded: This is a strong batch of refinements.
This is a strong batch of refinements. You're catching real edge cases. Let me address each in order, push back where I think you're wrong, and surface a few things you didn't ask but should think about.

Cleaners running late
Two different problems hiding inside one issue.

Cleaner knows ahead of time (previous job ran long, traffic, emergency lunch). Solution: on the cleaner-side active-job screen, a "Running late" button. They tap it, pick a reason from a dropdown (Previous job overrun / Traffic / Personal emergency / Other), enter a new ETA. Customer gets a push notification + in-app message immediately: "James is running late — new ETA 11:45 AM. Reason: Traffic on I-80." Job status updates everywhere.
Cleaner is silent and just doesn't show up. That's the no-show problem below.
The "Running late" button should be available from the moment the booking is confirmed (the day before, day of, anytime) and stays available until they tap "Clock in." Critical: cleaners get a strike on their reliability score after 3 lateness flags in 30 days, regardless of reason. Lateness happens; chronic lateness is a pattern. Reliability score is what the platform uses to surface or suppress cleaners on the homepage cleaner row.

Edge case to plan for: cleaner taps "Running late" multiple times. Don't let them push the ETA back indefinitely. After the second push, customer gets an option button: "Accept new ETA" or "Cancel job (free refund)." Customer trust deteriorates fast when ETAs slip twice. Give them an out.

When is no-show activated
Your proposal: "1 hour past service start with no communication or reschedule" → marked no-show, full refund. I'd tighten it.

Reasoning: 1 hour is too long. A customer waiting an hour past service time is a customer who's already lost faith in your platform — the trust damage is done by minute 60. Plus, a cleaner who's truly running late by 30+ minutes should have communicated. If they haven't, they're not coming.

Recommended timing:

15 minutes past service time, no communication: automated reminder push to cleaner ("You're 15 min late — tap to update or confirm arrival")
30 minutes past service time, still no communication: customer gets push + email: "Your cleaner hasn't arrived. We're checking in with them. You can rebook anytime." Customer gets option to cancel free at this point.
45 minutes past service time, still no communication: automatic no-show. Job marked failed. Full refund issued instantly. Cleaner gets a no-show strike.
Three no-show strikes in 90 days = automatic suspension pending review. This needs to be in your cleaner ToS and surfaced in onboarding. Cleaners need to know the rules.

On the "suggest other cleaners" idea: yes, but carefully. The moment of a no-show is a moment of high frustration — the customer is more likely to leave the platform entirely than rebook. Your job is to retain them. Suggested copy when refund processes:

"We're sorry — [Cleaner name] didn't show up. Your money is back in your account. [3 verified cleaners with availability today]: [Cleaner cards with same-day available badge]. [Book one now, save 15% — apology credit]."

The 15% apology credit is critical. It costs you a small amount in margin but recovers the lifetime value of a customer you'd otherwise lose. Frame it as an apology, not a discount, so it doesn't train customers to wait for cancellations.

Don't auto-rebook. That feels controlling. Show them options, let them choose.

Geofencing cadence and radius
You said: every 15 min after "On my way" tap, 100m radius for arrival.

My take: the 100m radius is fine — that's about a city block, which accounts for GPS drift in dense urban areas. The 15-minute cadence is too sparse for the on-my-way phase.

Here's why: imagine a cleaner taps "On my way" at 9 AM for a 9:30 job, then driving from Berkeley to Oakland. At 9:15 they're 4 miles out. At 9:30 they're 1 mile out. The customer is checking the app at 9:25 because the cleaner is supposed to be there in 5 minutes — and the last GPS ping is from 10 minutes ago, showing them 4 miles away. That's a worse experience than no tracking at all.

Recommended cadence:

"On my way" tap → first ping immediately
Then every 3 minutes during transit
Geo-fence triggers automatically when within 100m of address: app prompts cleaner with "You've arrived — clock in?" prompt
After clock-in, GPS pings drop to once at clock-in, once at clock-out (per your earlier "I don't want to track them all the time" — agreed)
3 minutes is still battery-friendly (modern phones use cell-tower triangulation when fine GPS isn't needed), and gives customers a believable real-time map. 15 minutes makes the map feel broken.

Counter-proposal: if battery drain is your concern, do 5-minute pings with a "burst" mode in the last 10 minutes before ETA. Most marketplaces use this pattern.

Same-day fill from gaps in schedule
You said no — and you're right. I retract my earlier suggestion. Cleaners squeezing in a job between two scheduled appointments creates lateness risk for the second appointment, and lateness is what kills marketplaces. Better policy:

A cleaner can mark themselves available for "future jobs only" at the end of any active job.
The system never auto-suggests a same-day job if it would compress travel time below a comfortable margin (suggest 90 min between back-to-back jobs).
If you ever build same-day fill, gate it behind a cleaner-set "I'm comfortable with same-day requests today" toggle that resets daily.
Skip same-day fill at launch entirely. It's a Phase 3 feature.

Auto-rebook nudge after positive review/tip
This is a good marketplace lever — and it's what gets cleaning marketplaces from one-time bookings to recurring revenue. Recurring is where the real money is.

Recommended logic:

Customer leaves a 4+ star review or tips → trigger eligibility for auto-rebook nudge.
24 hours after the cleaning, push notification + email: "Loved your cleaning with [Cleaner name]? Book again."
Tap the notification → simple page with three time options: 2 weeks from now, 4 weeks from now, custom. Same cleaner pre-selected. Same service type. Estimated price shown.
One tap to book. Goes through the standard booking flow with everything pre-filled.
Smart defaults by service type:

Standard cleaning → suggest 2 or 4 weeks
Deep clean → suggest 8 or 12 weeks (deep cleans aren't monthly)
Move-out → no auto-rebook (one-time event)
Airbnb turnover → suggest weekly recurring (this is the killer feature for hosts)
Recurring booking is its own product feature. Build a "Set up recurring" flow that creates auto-bookings on a cadence. Customer can pause, skip a cleaning, or cancel anytime. Cleaners see recurring bookings as a separate stream in their inbox — they're more valuable because they're predictable income.

Customer dashboard top section: rather than a 2-tap reschedule widget at the top, I'd suggest a "Next cleaning" hero card that shows the upcoming booking with three action chips: Reschedule / Cancel / Message cleaner. Plus a "Book again" pinned at the top showing your last cleaner. The reschedule flow itself is two taps from this card, so you get the speed without making reschedule the dominant action.

Reschedule shouldn't be the highlight. Booking should.

Customer-driven scheduling on cleaner availability
Yes, this is a much better model than "request → cleaner accepts." Here's why:

The request-and-wait model adds friction that loses bookings. A customer who books at 8 PM for a Tuesday cleaning doesn't want to wait until tomorrow morning to find out if it's confirmed. They'll book the next cleaner who has open availability.

Recommended:

Cleaners set their availability in advance (calendar with bookable time blocks).
Cleaners set their service area as a radius from a home base (5 miles, 10 miles, 25 miles).
Customers see only cleaners whose availability AND service area match their request.
Customer picks a time from cleaner's open slots. Confirmation is automatic. No request-accept dance.
Cleaner gets a notification: "New booking: Tuesday 10 AM, [Customer name], [Address]." They can decline within 1 hour for a free swap (system finds another cleaner). After 1 hour, the booking is locked and declining = cancellation strike.
This is how Airbnb works (Instant Book), how OpenTable works, how Calendly works. The "request to book" pattern was the right answer for early Airbnb when supply was unverified — once you have verified cleaners, instant book is faster, more reliable, and converts better.

Caveat: some cleaners may want a "review before accept" gate for new customers. Let them toggle it on for first-time customers from a particular customer (default off — most cleaners want bookings). But the default is instant book.

Service area radius isn't enough — also need: travel-time buffer setting. Cleaner sets "I need 60 min between jobs" and the calendar greys out time slots that violate that buffer. Pure radius doesn't account for traffic — buffer time does.

Mid-job issues — scope creep, animals, damage
You're correct that the timer should NOT pause. I retract my earlier suggestion. Cleaners on hourly are paid for time on site — pausing the timer is the platform stealing wages from them. The flag should:

Mark the booking with an issue tag (Scope change / Animal not crated / Damage found / Unsafe condition / Other).
Push notify the customer immediately with the cleaner's note.
Continue the timer.
If the customer doesn't acknowledge within 10 minutes, the issue is logged in the job record and a support ticket auto-opens for human review post-job.
One more thing on scope creep specifically: if a customer asks for additional services mid-job, the cleaner should have a "Quote additional work" option. They tap a list of common adds (move heavy furniture, clean inside fridge, additional bathroom — with their pre-set rates) and the customer gets a notification with a confirm button. If confirmed, the additional charge is added to the booking. Clean, documented, no awkwardness.

This converts scope creep from a dispute risk into a revenue stream. Cleaners get paid for extra work; customers get what they want; you take your cut. Win across the board.

Photo opt-out — better proposal
Your refined model is much better than mine. Here's how I'd structure it:

Default photo set per service type (the ones cleaners always shoot):

Standard: Kitchen, Main bathroom, Master bedroom (made bed only)
Deep: All Standard + inside oven, baseboards (one wide shot per major room)
Move-out: All Standard + inside oven, inside fridge, empty closets
Airbnb turnover: Kitchen, all bathrooms, all bedrooms (made beds), living room
Photo etiquette rules baked in for the cleaner:

Wide angle, no close-ups of personal items
Avoid family photos, mail, papers, electronics
No people in the photo
No medication, prescription bottles, or financial documents
A 30-second cleaner training screen on photo etiquette during onboarding. Don't trust the cleaner's instincts; teach them.

Customer-side photo controls during booking:

"Skip a room" — text input, e.g., "Don't photograph the office, my home gym, or kids' rooms"
"No photos at all" — opt out entirely (with the dispute waiver below)
The dispute waiver for full opt-out: if customer opts out of all photos, they sign an inline acknowledgment: "I understand that without photo documentation, dispute resolution will rely on the cleaner's clock-in/out times and any evidence I provide. I waive the right to dispute solely on the basis of cleaning quality without providing my own photo evidence." This is critical. Without this, you have no way to fairly resolve disputes when a customer opts out and then claims the job was bad.

What this protects: the cleaner. A no-photo job becomes a he-said-she-said. The waiver puts the burden of proof on the customer to show their own evidence, which is reasonable.

Photography terms of service
Yes — this should be its own document, displayed at three points:

In the booking flow, as an inline summary with a "View full terms" expand. User must check a box to confirm.
In the booking confirmation email, linked but not auto-attached.
In account settings, searchable any time.
Don't email the full ToS document with every booking. That's overwhelming and people stop reading. The booking confirmation should have one line: "Photo policy: Photos taken during your cleaning are encrypted, used only for dispute resolution, and deleted after 90 days. [Read full policy]"

What the photography policy should cover (high level — get a real lawyer to draft the actual document):

Why we take photos: documentation of work completed, dispute resolution, customer reassurance.
What we photograph: rooms identified at booking time. Wide-angle composition. No close-ups of personal items.
What we never photograph: people, identifiable personal documents, medical information, financial paperwork, weapons, anything explicitly opted out by the customer.
How photos are stored: encrypted at rest. Access limited to: the customer, the cleaner who took them, support staff during dispute review.
How long photos are kept: 90 days from job completion, then automatically deleted.
Customer rights: can request deletion at any time, can opt out entirely (with dispute waiver), can request a copy of all photos in their job history.
What happens in a dispute: photos are reviewed by support staff. Cleaner and customer each see all photos. Photos are not shared with anyone outside the dispute.
What we'll never do: sell photos, share with third parties, use for marketing without explicit opt-in, train AI/ML on customer photos.
Data breach disclosure: we'll notify within 72 hours if any breach affects your photos.
Jurisdiction-specific rights: California users have rights under CCPA, etc.
Last point matters because you're launching in California. Get a lawyer who knows CCPA. This isn't optional.

Emergency tickets — your refinements
3 in 6 months before the option disappears: good. This is more humane than my original 1 in 6.

Cleaner safety / weather concerns separated as emergency support tickets: absolutely correct. This is a category I missed. Specifically:

Cleaner safety threats: unsafe property condition, hostile pet not contained, inappropriate behavior from customer, illegal activity at property, unsafe structural condition.
Weather / external: wildfire smoke (a real Northern California issue — AQI over a threshold should trigger automatic emergency review), severe storm, flooding, road closure.
Recommended ticket categorization in the support system:

Standard: general questions, payout questions, profile updates. SLA: 24-48 hours.
Job-specific: issues during or after a specific job. SLA: 4-12 hours.
Dispute: payment dispute. SLA: 24 hours, structured review.
Emergency: safety, weather, mid-job danger, suspected fraud, accusations of theft. SLA: 1 hour, human review required, escalation chain to founder/admin.
Build emergency tickets with a separate UI entry point. A red "Report emergency" button on the active-job screen, separate from the regular "Help" link. Different visual treatment so cleaners don't avoid using it because it's buried.

For wildfire/weather automation: integrate with PurpleAir or a similar AQI API. AQI over 200 in the cleaner's service area auto-flags any active jobs as eligible for cancellation without penalty. Saves you a flood of manual support tickets every wildfire season.

Reschedule denial → spite no-show
You said: customer should pay because they had a contract. I agree, with one nuance.

The cleaner declined the reschedule, which means the cleaner is committed to the original time. The customer trying to get out of the booking by reschedule failed. Showing up or canceling under the standard penalty schedule are the only honest options at that point.

My nuance: make the consequence visible in the reschedule denial UI. When the cleaner taps "Decline reschedule," the customer immediately sees: "[Cleaner] is unable to reschedule. Your original booking on [date/time] still stands. Cancel now under the standard policy: [refund amount based on time]." This sets expectations clearly and prevents the spite no-show by making the right path obvious.

If they no-show after this, full charge, full reliability score hit on customer. Customer reliability score matters too — chronic no-show customers should be flaggable by cleaners and eventually bannable. Two-sided trust.

Reschedule once free per booking — confirmed
Locked in. Each booking gets one free reschedule (if 24h+ out). Subsequent reschedules incur the standard cancellation penalty schedule.

Photo time window — 15-20 min instead of 5 min
You're right, I was wrong. A 5-minute window doesn't account for walkthroughs, customer instructions, supply unloading. Let's go with 15 minutes from clock-in for the first photo to be uploaded.

But add a second guardrail: at least one photo must be uploaded every 30 minutes during the active job. This catches the cleaner who clocks in, takes one photo, then doesn't actually clean. They have to keep documenting work. If 30 minutes pass without a new photo, the cleaner gets a soft prompt: "Still working? Upload a quick photo to keep customers updated." After 60 minutes silent, the customer gets a notification.

This is still humane to cleaners — every 30 minutes is reasonable for documentation of room-by-room work — but it kills the clock-in-and-coast attack vector.

Things you didn't ask but should think about
1. Repeat customer-cleaner pairs build trust faster than ratings do. Once a customer has used the same cleaner 3+ times, surface them as "Your regular." This becomes the dominant rebook path. Repeat-pair bookings should bypass the cleaner-list page entirely on the customer dashboard.

2. Cleaner pay timing affects retention more than rate does. Daily payouts (every 24 hours) for completed jobs is the killer feature for cleaner acquisition. Most marketplaces do weekly. If you can support daily, it's a meaningful recruiting advantage. Use Stripe Connect's instant payout feature.

3. Customer can save cleaning preferences once. "I have a dog (friendly), please use only natural cleaners, my mother sleeps in the back bedroom on weekday mornings." Saved to the customer profile, auto-injected into every booking note. Cleaners read these before clocking in. Big quality-of-life win that costs nothing.

4. Dispute resolution timeline visible to both parties. When a dispute opens, both customer and cleaner see a status: "Under review — response within 24 hours." Don't leave either party in limbo. Status updates as the dispute progresses. Resolution comes with a written explanation, not just a verdict.

5. Edge case worth solving early: customer not home at clock-in. A common scenario for Airbnb hosts is "the cleaner has the lockbox code." For regular customers, "I leave the key under the mat." Build a "Customer present?" question at booking. If no, capture access instructions (lockbox, key location, gate code, neighbor) and treat them with the same encryption/deletion policy as photos. Cleaners see access instructions only after clock-in, never beforehand (privacy).

6. Cleaner reliability score should be visible to cleaners, not just to admin. Show them the number, what affects it, and what's at risk. Make the rules transparent — cleaners hate platforms with secret algorithms determining their work.

Updated booking lifecycle reflecting these decisions
The earlier lifecycle diagram still holds at the high level, but here's what's changed underneath:

"Running late" is a button available throughout the pre-arrival phase
No-show timer kicks in at 15 / 30 / 45 minute checkpoints
GPS pings every 3 minutes during transit, geo-fence at 100m, then 2 pings only during job
First photo within 15 minutes of clock-in, ongoing photos every 30 minutes
Booking is instant-confirm based on cleaner availability calendar, not a request
Mid-job issue flag pings customer, doesn't pause timer, opens auto-ticket if no acknowledgment
Tip prompted only after 4+ star review
Auto-rebook nudge 24 hours after positive review
Recurring booking is its own product flow
What's next
Three options:

Cleaner active-job screen wireframe — now significantly more complex with all these refinements (running late button, no-show automation visible, photo cadence, issue flag with quote-additional-work, scope-add UI).
Customer-side reschedule + cancel screens — locking the policy with real UI so we can pressure-test it.
Recurring booking + auto-rebook flow wireframes — the lifetime-value feature you just confirmed wanting.
I'd still recommend option 1. The cleaner active-job screen is the heart of your differentiation — GPS, photos, clock-in/out, mid-job flags, time tracking, all in one screen. If we get it right, the rest of the product is easier. If we get it wrong, nothing else matters.

Reaction? Push back on anything that doesn't sit right. The longer we spend arguing here, the less rework later.
