Claude responded: Good batch.
Good batch. Locking your decisions, then digging into the reliability scoring system because you're right that it has to be precise — vague scoring is the fastest way to lose cleaner trust.

Decisions locked
Cleaner payouts: weekly batch every Friday at ~noon (Pacific). Plus optional instant payout after job approval, 5% fee. Stripe Connect supports both natively.
Photo cadence: first photo within 15 min of clock-in, ongoing photos every 60 min (was 30).
Free reschedule: 12-hour cutoff (was 24-hour). Past 12 hours = standard cancellation penalty schedule.
Entry instructions: revealed to cleaner only after they tap "On my way." (Privacy-protective, good call.)
A note on instant payout fee: 5% is reasonable but check what Stripe charges you. Stripe's instant payout fee is around 1.5% (minimum $0.50). So your 5% leaves you 3.5% margin on instant payouts — that's a small profit center. Just make sure the fee is disclosed every time, with an opt-in, so cleaners aren't surprised. UI: when a cleaner taps "Get paid now" the screen should show "$120 → $114 (5% instant fee = $6). Or wait until Friday for full $120."

A note on Friday weekly batch: Friday at noon is Pacific time, but your cleaners may be in different time zones (Reno is Pacific too, so this is fine for now — just flag it for when you expand). Funds typically take 1–2 business days to land in cleaner bank accounts via standard ACH, so cleaners will see Friday-noon initiation and money in their account Monday or Tuesday. Set expectations clearly in cleaner onboarding.

Reliability scoring — the full system
You're right that this has to be precise, transparent, and explained to both parties. Vague algorithms erode trust. Here's the full proposed system. Push back on any of it.

Design principles for the scoring system
Before the metrics, the principles that govern them:

Transparent. Both parties see their own score. Both parties see what's measured. No hidden penalties, no secret algorithms.
Recoverable. Mistakes don't permanently tank the score. A rolling 90-day window means a bad month doesn't follow a cleaner forever.
Two-sided. Customers have scores too. Bad customers get visible to cleaners and eventually penalized.
Behavioral, not subjective. The score measures what people do, not how friendly they seem. Reviews are separate from reliability.
Consequence-mapped. Each score band has clear outcomes: visibility on the homepage, ability to instant-book, suspension thresholds.
Cleaner reliability score
Range: 0–100. Starts at 90 (new cleaners begin slightly below ceiling so they have room to earn trust). Computed over a rolling 90-day window. Refreshes nightly.

What's measured:

Metric	Weight	What counts as a deduction
On-time arrival	25%	Late by >10 min (without "Running late" tap = double deduction)
Job completion	25%	Cancellations within 24 hours, no-shows, jobs failed mid-completion
Photo compliance	15%	Missing required photos, photos uploaded outside the 15-min / 60-min cadence
Customer ratings	15%	Average star rating below 4.5 starts deducting; below 4.0 deducts heavily
Communication	10%	Failure to respond to customer messages within 4 hours during active bookings
Reschedule frequency	10%	Cleaner-initiated reschedules within 48h of booking
Score bands:

90–100 (Trusted): Full homepage visibility. Boosted in cleaner list. Eligible for premium "Trusted" badge.
75–89 (Good standing): Standard visibility. Normal sort order in cleaner list.
60–74 (Warning): Visible warning banner in cleaner dashboard. Reduced visibility in cleaner list. Onboarding to coaching resources.
40–59 (Probation): Cannot accept new bookings until score recovers. Existing bookings still valid. Mandatory check-in with support.
Below 40: Account suspended pending review.
Hard triggers (instant penalties regardless of score):

3 no-shows in 90 days = automatic suspension
1 confirmed safety incident = automatic suspension pending investigation
1 confirmed fraud/theft = permanent termination
What's NOT measured:

Job duration. Hourly cleaners shouldn't be penalized for being thorough.
Customer tip rate. Tipping is voluntary; punishing cleaners for low tips creates the wrong incentive.
Subjective feedback. "I didn't like her vibe" is a 1-star rating — the rating affects the score, but the comment alone doesn't.
How cleaners see it (the dashboard widget):

A circular progress ring showing the score, with the band label below. Click in for breakdown: each metric's current contribution, recent events that affected the score, and what would improve it. Direct, no jargon, no surprises.

Customer reliability score
Range: 0–100. Starts at 90. Computed over a rolling 90-day window.

What's measured:

Metric	Weight	What counts as a deduction
Honor commitments	30%	Cancellations within 24 hours, no-shows, late access (cleaner waits >15 min for entry)
Payment reliability	20%	Failed payment, payment disputes ruled in cleaner's favor
Cleaner ratings of customer	20%	Cleaners rate customers post-job; aggregate star rating
Communication	15%	Failure to respond to cleaner messages within 4 hours during active bookings
Dispute pattern	15%	Disputes filed; weighted by outcome (won = 0 impact, lost = significant deduction, withdrawn = small deduction)
Score bands:

90–100 (Trusted): Full booking access. Eligible for instant-book with all cleaners.
75–89 (Good standing): Standard booking access.
60–74 (Warning): Cleaners can see this score band before accepting; some may decline.
40–59 (Restricted): Cannot book without cleaner pre-approval (request-to-book mode for this customer).
Below 40: Account suspended; must contact support to rebook.
Hard triggers:

3 no-shows in 90 days = restricted to request-to-book mode
2 confirmed safety incidents (cleaner reports unsafe property + verified) = suspended pending review
1 confirmed fraud (chargeback abuse, fake disputes, identity fraud) = terminated
Cleaners rating customers post-job: This is the part most marketplaces skip. After every job, the cleaner gets the same 1–5 star prompt as the customer, with categories: Property cleanliness on arrival, Communication, Honored agreement, Safe environment. The customer never sees individual cleaner ratings of them. They see only their aggregate score. This protects honest cleaner feedback.

What customers see: Same dashboard widget design as cleaners. Score ring, band label, breakdown on click. Direct visibility into their own behavior pattern.

Two-sided visibility rules
This is where most marketplaces get it wrong. Here's what each party sees:

Customer-facing (the customer themselves):

Their own score and breakdown — full transparency
Cleaner reliability score band only (not exact number) on the cleaner profile
Cleaner-facing (the cleaner themselves):

Their own score and breakdown — full transparency
Customer reliability score band before accepting a booking (not exact number)
Why bands not numbers across the table: showing "Customer score: 73" feels like grading a person and is dehumanizing. Showing "Customer: Good standing" or "Customer: Warning" feels like a status. Same information, different feeling. Same applies in reverse.

Communicating it to users
This is where the design work matters. Three places this lives:

Onboarding — both customers and cleaners see a 3-screen explainer during signup: "What we measure / How it affects you / How to improve it." Brief, clear, no legal jargon. Acceptance is a checkbox step.
In the dashboard, visible always. Score widget on home dashboard for both parties. Tappable for full breakdown. Recent events shown ("Score went down 3 points: cancellation within 24 hours on June 4").
In the policy doc. Full written policy in the help center, plain language. Linked from onboarding and dashboard.
The most important rule: never penalize without notification. Every event that changes the score generates a push notification: "Your score changed: -3 points (late arrival on yesterday's booking). [What this means]." Surprise penalties are the fastest way to lose user trust.

What this looks like in the cleaner's dashboard
I'll wireframe this when we get to the cleaner-side dashboard. For now, here's the conceptual layout:

Top: score ring, current band label
Middle: this week's stats (jobs completed, on-time rate, photo compliance rate)
Below: recent events that affected the score (with explanation)
Bottom: "How to improve" tips contextualized to current weakest metric
Customer dashboard mirrors this with their own metrics.

"On my way" button — when does it appear?
You're asking the right question. Two factors govern timing.

When the button is enabled for the cleaner
Earliest: 2 hours before service start time. Latest: at service start time (cleaner late if they tap after).

Why 2 hours: covers most realistic departure times for a cleaning job. Driving 1 hour + 30 min buffer + setup time = roughly 2 hours. Gives cleaners flexibility without being so wide that they could tap it the night before.

Why not earlier: tapping "On my way" the night before isn't useful information for the customer ("They're on their way... in 14 hours") and erodes the meaning of the button.

Why not later: if a cleaner taps "On my way" at the appointment start time, they're already late. The on-time arrival metric has already been hit. The "On my way" button should be a pre-arrival tool, not a damage-control tool.

What the cleaner-side "On my way" page consists of
Single screen, opened by tapping the "On my way" button. Components:

Top: confirmation banner. "You're now sharing location with [Customer first name]. They've been notified."

Middle: the booking summary card. Customer first name, address (full now, was partial before), service type, estimated duration, hourly rate, total estimated charge. Tap to expand for booking notes the customer left at booking.

Below the booking card: the entry instructions card (this is what unlocks after On my way is tapped).

Access method (lockbox / key / customer present / other)
If lockbox: code, location of box
If key: where key is hidden
If customer present: their phone number, any noted preferences
Parking guidance if customer provided it
Pet info if customer provided it (e.g., "Friendly dog will be in crate in living room")
Special instructions ("Please use side gate, front door is broken")
Below entry instructions: in-app messaging button. "Message [Customer name]." Pre-filled prompt: "Hi, on my way! ETA is X minutes." Customer-facing message thread, encrypted.

Below messaging: the running-late escape hatch. Already-discussed "Running late" button. Available from this screen and throughout transit until clock-in.

Bottom: the prominent action button. Once the cleaner is within 100m of the address, this morphs from "Update ETA" to "Clock in" with the geo-fence triggered.

Privacy on entry instructions
You said make instructions visible only after On my way. I agree, but want to add: instructions should also auto-expire 4 hours after the booking ends. The cleaner shouldn't be able to scroll back through their job history three months later and find the lockbox code for someone's house. Encryption at rest + automatic expiration is the right model.

Also: customers can update entry instructions up until the cleaner taps "On my way." After that, the instructions are locked for the cleaner's reference. This prevents confusion mid-transit ("wait, did the customer change the door code?"). If the customer needs to communicate something after that, it goes through in-app messaging.

When the customer sees "On my way"
The moment the cleaner taps the button, the customer gets:

Push notification: "[Cleaner first name] is on their way. ETA: X minutes."
In-app status update on the booking card
Live location ping every 3 minutes (the cadence we agreed on)
Map view available (optional click — not auto-displayed because most customers don't want to stare at a moving dot)
Updated decisions reflected in the lifecycle
The earlier lifecycle diagram still works at the high level. Here's what's changed underneath this round:

"On my way" button appears 2 hours before service start
Tapping it triggers entry instructions reveal + GPS-on + customer notification
Photo cadence: first within 15 min, ongoing every 60 min (not 30)
Free reschedule cutoff: 12 hours, not 24
Reliability scores: 0–100, 90 starting, 6 metrics each side, score bands, hard triggers
Score visibility: own score = number, other party's score = band only
Cleaner payouts: weekly Friday batch + optional 5%-fee instant
Entry instructions: revealed at "On my way" tap, expire 4 hours after booking ends
What's next
Three options:

Cleaner active-job screen wireframe — incorporates the "On my way" page details, photo cadence, mid-job issue flag, scope-add quote, clock-out flow.
Reliability score dashboard widget wireframes — both customer and cleaner side. Locks in how the scoring system communicates to users in real time.
Customer reschedule/cancel policy screens — surfaces the penalty schedule, the 12-hour free reschedule cutoff, the request-with-decline mechanics.
I'd push for the cleaner active-job screen still, but I'd add the score widget design to that same wireframe set since both live in the cleaner dashboard. So the next deliverable would be:

Cleaner dashboard (home screen with reliability widget, today's schedule, pending requests)
"On my way" page
Active-job screen (clock-in, photo grid, mid-job actions, clock-out)
That's three wireframes covering the full cleaner-side daily flow.

Push back on any of the reliability scoring choices — weights, thresholds, hard triggers, what's measured vs. what isn't. This is the part that affects livelihood, so getting it right matters.

May 2
Claude responded: Two pieces.
Two pieces. The reschedule wording, then the tiered pricing system — which is a really interesting strategic move and worth digging into properly because it has both real upside and real risks.

Reschedule policy — corrected
Locked: One free reschedule per booking, allowed up to 12 hours before the appointment. Any subsequent reschedule on that same booking falls under the standard cancellation penalty schedule.

So the sequence is:

Customer reschedules once for free → new appointment time set, no penalty
Customer wants to reschedule again on the new appointment → treated as cancellation under standard policy (48h+ free, 24-48h = 50%, <24h = 100%)
That's cleaner and matches your intent. The "free pass" is one per booking, period — not one per attempt.

Tiered pricing tied to reliability score
This is a strong strategic instinct. Let me give you honest feedback because there's a lot to like and a few things that will hurt you if not handled carefully.

What's strong about this idea
1. It solves the "new cleaner" problem. Most marketplaces have no answer for "how does a new cleaner get their first job when everyone with reviews looks better?" Your tier system gives new cleaners a clear value proposition: lower price, same trust verification. Customers who care about price get matched with cleaners who need to build reputation. Win-win.

2. It creates earned advancement. Cleaners can see exactly how to increase their earnings — reliability, not luck. That's motivating in a way that random surge pricing or platform-decided rate caps aren't.

3. It protects the platform's brand at the top. All-Star Expert at $65/hr signals quality. Without tiers, your highest-rated cleaners would either undercharge (because they don't know they could ask more) or you'd have a few outliers charging $80 and looking off-brand.

4. It's a real differentiator. Most cleaning platforms either let cleaners set their own rates with no guidance (TaskRabbit) or set platform-wide pricing (Handy). Yours splits the difference: pricing power earned through performance.

Where I'm going to push back hard
Problem 1: Your tier structure has a math problem at the top.

Your tiers:

Rising Pro: $20–30 (range = $10)
Proven Specialist: $20–40 (range = $20)
Top Performer: $20–50 (range = $30)
All-Star Expert: $20–65 (range = $45)
The bottom of every tier is $20. That means an All-Star Expert can charge as little as a Rising Pro. That defeats the tier system — a brand-new cleaner and a 5-star elite cleaner could be priced identically. It also kills the cleaner's incentive to climb because the floor doesn't move. Why bother getting to All-Star if you can still only charge $20?

Recommended fix: floors should also rise with tier.

Rising Pro: $20–30
Proven Specialist: $30–45
Top Performer: $40–55
All-Star Expert: $50–75
Now climbing tiers actually means more money, not just the option for more money. The cleaner's economic incentive is real.

Problem 2: The score thresholds don't match what we just designed.

We just locked a scoring system where:

90–100 = Trusted (full access, premium badge)
75–89 = Good standing
60–74 = Warning (reduced visibility)
40–59 = Probation (cannot accept new bookings)
Below 40 = Suspended
Your tier thresholds are 0–49, 50–69, 70–89, 90–100. Those don't align. A "Proven Specialist" at score 50 is in our scoring system's Probation band — meaning they'd be suspended from accepting bookings. They literally couldn't earn money in this tier.

Recommended fix: align tiers with score bands or change the score system. I'd align tiers with score bands.

Rising Pro: 0–59 (in our scoring: Probation through low Warning)
Proven Specialist: 60–74 (Warning band — recovering or new cleaners)
Top Performer: 75–89 (Good standing)
All-Star Expert: 90–100 (Trusted)
This actually makes more sense pedagogically too. The tier names can reinforce the scoring narrative: Rising Pro = "you're new or recovering, building your reputation." All-Star Expert = "you've earned the highest tier."

Problem 3: New cleaners starting at score 0 (bottom of Rising Pro) is rough.

We locked earlier that new cleaners start at score 90. That gave them homepage visibility, the chance to earn first jobs, etc. If you start them at 0 (bottom of Rising Pro tier), they have to crawl their way up just to get visibility — which means they can't earn jobs to build score, which means they can't escape Rising Pro.

This is the chicken-and-egg problem that destroys marketplace cold starts.

Recommended fix: keep new cleaners starting at 90, but lock their tier-billing access to "Rising Pro" rates for the first 10 completed jobs. Score and tier are decoupled for the trust-building phase. Mechanics:

New cleaner signs up → score = 90 (visible, can take bookings)
Tier = Rising Pro by default, regardless of score, for first 10 completed jobs
After 10 completed jobs, current score is computed and tier is assigned by score band
Score continues to evolve from there; tier moves with score on a 30-day rolling check
This solves the cold-start problem (new cleaners are visible and bookable) while preserving the tier value (you can't charge premium rates until you've actually proven reliability).

Problem 4: "Same-day booking accepted" as a Top Performer perk contradicts what we agreed.

Two messages back, you said you don't want same-day fill from gaps in schedule. You're right. So "same-day booking accepted" shouldn't be a perk in any tier — it's just not a feature.

Recommended fix: replace with something real. What Top Performers actually have that lower tiers don't:

Verified specialty endorsements (e.g., move-out specialist, eco-friendly products)
Larger service radius (they've earned the right to take farther bookings)
Recurring booking eligibility (regulars can lock them in)
Higher visibility on homepage cleaner row
Pick one. I'd suggest "Eligible for recurring bookings" since that ties into the rebook flow we already designed.

Problem 5: "Guaranteed on-time arrival" as a Top Performer perk is dangerous.

Promising on-time arrival to customers means you're on the hook when it doesn't happen. What's the guarantee? Refund? Discount? Free reschedule? You've now committed your platform to compensating customers for cleaner lateness, which means you eat the cost when a Top Performer hits traffic.

Recommended fix: change to "Track record: 95%+ on-time arrival." It's a stat, not a guarantee. Same trust signal, no platform liability.

Strategic concerns to think about
Concern 1: Customers will see this and game it.

If your homepage shows tiered pricing with All-Star Experts at $65/hr, some customers will sort by price and only ever book Rising Pros. That's fine for cleaners who want to build reputation. It's bad for All-Star Experts whose pipeline drops because they're price-filtered out.

Mitigation:

Don't show tiers as the primary filter on the cleaner list. Let customers filter by price range, but show the tier badge as secondary information ("Maria L. — All-Star Expert, $52/hr").
Show the value of the tier on the cleaner profile, not just the price. "All-Star Experts complete 98% of jobs on time" tells the customer why the price is higher.
Offer first-cleaning discount across all tiers, so cost-anchored customers can sample All-Star Experts.
Concern 2: A cleaner who drops a tier loses pricing power retroactively.

A Top Performer who has a bad month and slips to Proven Specialist tier — what happens to their existing recurring bookings priced at $50/hr? Do those rates drop? Does the customer get a price cut?

Recommended fix: existing booked work stays at booked rate. Tier pricing applies to new bookings only. A cleaner who slips a tier sees their max rate for new bookings drop, but in-flight bookings honor original pricing. Otherwise you have customers checking the app every week to see if their cleaner got demoted.

Concern 3: Cleaners will resent the system if it feels arbitrary.

You'll need to be ironclad about transparency. Every tier change generates a notification: "Your tier changed: Top Performer → Proven Specialist. Score dropped to 73 (was 76). Reason: 2 customer cancellation responses. Effect on earnings: max rate now $45/hr. [How to recover]." If cleaners don't see exactly why their earnings dropped, they'll quit the platform.

Concern 4: The bottom of the system has a labor-rights edge case.

A Rising Pro doing the same work as an All-Star Expert is being paid roughly half. If they're doing identical work to identical quality, that's a fairness question. Your platform's defense: they haven't proven the quality yet. After 10 jobs, they'll move tiers if their work matches their pricing.

This holds up legally and ethically as long as the path to advancement is genuinely achievable. If 80% of cleaners get stuck at Rising Pro forever, you have a structural problem and a possible PR liability. Track tier-mobility as a platform health metric. Aim for 60%+ of cleaners progressing past Rising Pro within 60 days of starting.

Where this lives in the product
Three surfaces to design for tiered pricing:

Cleaner dashboard: prominent "Your tier" widget alongside the score widget. Same circular ring metaphor. Shows current tier, current rate range, score progress toward next tier. Tappable for full breakdown.
Cleaner profile (customer-facing): tier badge near the cleaner's name. Tier explanation tooltip. Score band shown but not the exact number.

Claude responded: Excellent instincts.
Excellent instincts. You're catching that the system I designed is too binary — it can torch a great cleaner over a bad week. Let me rebuild this properly. This is going to be a longer response because the scoring and matching system is the engine of the platform, and getting it wrong cascades into every other decision.

I'm going to organize this in five pieces:

The grading philosophy (rebuilt from scratch)
The scoring system (with tier-drop protection)
Tier benefits (revised again)

1. Grading philosophy — rebuilt from scratch
The system I gave you was too punitive. One bad week could drop a cleaner from Top Performer to Rising Pro overnight, which would tank their earnings for weeks even if it was a fluke. That's not how trust works in real life — a doctor with 20 years of good practice doesn't lose their license over one bad month.

New principles:

Trust accumulates slowly, decays slowly. A cleaner's reputation is built over hundreds of jobs and shouldn't unwind over five.
Recent matters more than old, but old still matters. Weighted recency, not a hard 90-day window that erases history.
Tier mobility has friction in both directions. Climbing requires sustained good behavior. Dropping requires sustained bad behavior. One bad event doesn't move the tier.
Hard triggers exist for genuinely serious things. No-shows, safety, fraud — these bypass the gradual system because they're not statistical noise.
Cleaners can see exactly where they stand and exactly what changes things. The system is a glass box, not a black box.
2. Scoring system — rebuilt
The two-track model
Your instinct to "grade on a curve" was right. The fix is splitting score into two layers:

Track 1: Performance Score (0–100) A statistical measure of recent behavior. Moves easily up and down. This is the metric we've been calling "reliability score." Same six metrics as before, same weights, same calculation — refreshes nightly.

Track 2: Tier Standing (the one customers see) The tier a cleaner is currently in. Changes only when sustained performance justifies a move. Insulated from short-term volatility.

The Performance Score is the input. The Tier Standing is the output, and it has guardrails.

The tier-drop protection rules
Three rules that protect established cleaners from one-off bad weeks:

Rule 1: A tier drop requires sustained low performance, not a single spike. A cleaner doesn't drop tiers on a single day's bad number. They drop only when their Performance Score stays below the tier threshold for 14 consecutive days.

Example: Top Performer (75–89 band) at score 78. Bad week pulls them to 71. They don't drop to Proven Specialist immediately. They have 14 days to recover. If on day 15 their score is still <75, the tier drops. If they recover to 75+ before day 15, no drop.

This eliminates whiplash from one bad customer or one unlucky day.

Rule 2: Maximum one tier drop per 14-day window. Even if a cleaner's score crashes hard, they can only drop one tier per 14-day cycle. Top Performer → Proven Specialist takes 14 days minimum. Proven Specialist → Rising Pro takes another 14 days. So worst case, a cleaner falls from All-Star to Rising Pro in 6 weeks of sustained low performance — which is fair, because by that point they're genuinely struggling.

Rule 3: Tier-drop appeal window. Before a tier officially drops, the cleaner gets a 48-hour notice with the reason. They can submit a one-paragraph response if there's context (medical emergency, family death, customer abuse). A human reviews. About 90% of appeals will be denied (fair — most drops are earned), but the 10% with real justification get caught. This is the safety valve that prevents the platform from being seen as cold/algorithmic.

Tier promotion rules (mirror logic)
Same friction in reverse — climbing also takes sustained good behavior:

Rule 1: Tier promotion requires the new tier's Performance Score for 14 consecutive days. A Rising Pro hitting score 65 on a single day doesn't become a Proven Specialist immediately. They need to hold 60+ for 14 days.

Rule 2: Maximum one tier climb per 14-day window. Going from Rising Pro to All-Star Expert takes a minimum of ~8 weeks of sustained excellence. This prevents gaming (one perfect week to get a quick promotion).

Rule 3: First promotion accelerates after the new-cleaner phase. Cleaners completing their first 10 jobs get their first tier assigned immediately based on current score. So someone who came in strong out of the gate doesn't have to wait an extra 14 days to leave Rising Pro — the first tier assignment is the reward for surviving onboarding.

The "veteran cushion"
For cleaners who've been on the platform a long time with consistently high scores, add an extra layer of protection. This is your "grading on a curve" instinct made real.

Veteran status: 6 consecutive months above 75 score.

A veteran cleaner has:

30-day tier-drop window instead of 14. A bad two weeks doesn't drop a veteran. They have a full month to recover.
No automatic tier drop without manual review for the first drop. A veteran's first drop in 12 months gets a human eye on it, not just an algorithm.
This rewards loyalty and prevents the "20-year doctor loses license over one bad month" problem. Veterans are your platform's spine — protect them.

Updated metric weights (refined)
Looking at the original metrics again, I want to adjust two things:

Customer ratings weight feels right but the trigger threshold was wrong. Penalizing "below 4.5" is too harsh — most service businesses operate happily at 4.3. Let me redo the brackets:

4.7+ average → +contribution to score
4.3–4.6 average → neutral (no contribution either way)
Below 4.3 → deduction
Below 3.8 → heavy deduction
Reschedule frequency penalty needs a "gracious window." First cleaner-initiated reschedule per month doesn't count (people get sick). Second and beyond count. This prevents penalizing legitimate human moments.

Updated table:

Metric	Weight	Trigger
On-time arrival	25%	Late by >10 min without "Running late" tap
Job completion	25%	Cancellations within 24h, no-shows, mid-job failures
Photo compliance	15%	Missing required photos or cadence violations
Customer ratings	15%	Average below 4.3 starts deducting
Communication	10%	No response within 4 hours during active bookings
Reschedule frequency	10%	Second+ cleaner-initiated reschedule per month within 48h
Customer score table follows the same refinements (4.3 threshold, monthly grace).

Hard triggers (bypassing the gradual system)
These are the only things that move tiers instantly:

2 no-shows in 30 days → automatic drop one tier (was 3 in 90 days — too lenient)
3 no-shows in 60 days → suspended pending review
Confirmed safety incident → immediate suspension pending investigation
Confirmed fraud/theft → permanent termination
The 14-day buffer doesn't apply to these. No-show is too damaging to customers to soften.

3. Tier benefits — revised again
I'll restructure these around what cleaners actually care about (income, scheduling power) and what protects platform brand at the top.

Rising Pro — score 0–59 (or first 10 jobs)
Rate range: $25–35/hr

Background check, ID verification, GPS, photo proof, escrow
Featured in "New cleaner spotlight" rotation (see section 4 below)
Standard search visibility
Eligible for one-time bookings (no recurring)
"Building reputation" badge — honest, signals "give me a chance"
I raised the floor to $25 from $20. NorCal labor cost is real — $20/hr can't sustain a cleaner after platform fees, taxes, supplies, and travel. $25 is the realistic minimum.

Proven Specialist — score 60–74
Rate range: $35–50/hr

All Rising Pro features
Eligible for recurring bookings — this is the big unlock
Service area can expand to 15-mile radius (Rising Pro is capped at 10)
Standard search visibility, slight boost over Rising Pro in tied results
"Proven reliability" badge
Recurring bookings is the income stabilizer. Once a cleaner reaches Proven Specialist, they can build a steady book of regulars. This is the tier where cleaning starts being a viable income source.

Top Performer — score 75–89
Rate range: $50–65/hr

All Proven Specialist features
Track record: 95%+ on-time arrival displayed on profile
Service area up to 20-mile radius
Top placement in standard search results
Specialty endorsements (move-out specialist, eco-friendly, pet-friendly, etc.) — earned via repeat positive ratings in those categories
Eligible for "Trusted by neighbors" badge if they've completed 10+ jobs in a single ZIP code
Specialty endorsements are interesting — they let cleaners differentiate without arbitrarily inflating prices. A Top Performer who's a verified move-out specialist can charge near tier ceiling justifiably.

All-Star Expert — score 90–100
Rate range: $60–80/hr

All Top Performer features
Track record: 98%+ on-time arrival displayed
Service area up to 25-mile radius
Featured placement on homepage cleaner row
"First pick" priority — when a regular customer's primary cleaner is unavailable, All-Stars in the area are surfaced first
Eligible for "Customer favorite" badge if they have 5+ active recurring customers
Premium support response (4-hour SLA on cleaner-side support tickets)
The veteran cushion (6+ months at 75+ score) gives extra tier-drop protection but doesn't unlock new features — being a veteran is structural, not a perk.

A new tier mechanic worth considering: ZIP-locked badges
Beyond tier, let cleaners earn neighborhood-level reputation. "Trusted by neighbors in the Mission" or "Top-rated in Sacramento downtown." These are sub-tier badges that show locality-specific trust. Customers searching their own neighborhood see these prominently. This makes the platform feel more local and rewards cleaners who serve their community well.

4. Featuring new cleaners
Your concern is correct: without a featuring mechanism, new cleaners can't earn jobs to build reputation, and the marketplace cold-starts on the supply side.

Three mechanisms working together:

Mechanism A: New cleaner spotlight rotation
A dedicated section on the homepage and cleaner list page: "New on PureTask — verified and ready to clean." Shows 3–4 Rising Pros at a time, rotated daily. Honest framing — these are people building their reputation. Customers shopping for value or willing to take a chance see this section right after the top-rated row.

How rotation works:

Pool: all cleaners who have been on the platform <30 days OR have completed <10 jobs
Rotation: 3–4 cleaners shown per day per ZIP code
Each new cleaner gets at least 7 days of spotlight visibility before rotating out
After 30 days OR 10 completed jobs (whichever first), they exit the spotlight pool and rely on tier-based visibility
Spotlight slot guarantee: every new cleaner gets at least 7 spotlight days. This ensures every approved cleaner has a real shot at their first jobs. No cleaner is approved and immediately invisible.

Mechanism B: First-cleaning incentive for customers
Customer-facing offer: "Try a new cleaner — $15 off your first cleaning with any New Pro." Costs you $15 per first-job match, but it solves cold-start by paying customers a small amount to take a chance on supply you need to validate.

The math: if a $15 incentive converts 1 in 4 first-time customers to recurring, your blended customer-acquisition cost on those customers is $60. That's well below industry CAC for service marketplaces. Worth every dollar.

Critical: the $15 comes out of platform margin, not the cleaner's earnings. Cleaners get paid full rate. This protects the cleaner-side economics and keeps cleaners motivated to take new-customer jobs.

Mechanism C: Direct match referrals from established cleaners
When an established cleaner is fully booked or out of their service area, the platform can suggest: "Maria can't take this booking, but here are 2 New Pros she's vouched for." Established cleaners can vouch for newer cleaners they trust (informal network — vetting other cleaners they know). Vouches expire annually and can be revoked. This builds an organic mentorship layer and accelerates trust transfer to new supply.

This one is Phase 2 — too complex for launch. Mention it now so it's in the architecture.

5. Cleaner suggestion algorithm — honest and fair
This is the most important section. The way cleaners get surfaced to customers is the lever that determines who earns and who doesn't. Get it wrong and you're either accused of favoritism (real or perceived) or you waste your supply-side investment by hiding good cleaners.

The honest path: explain exactly how cleaners are sorted, in plain language, visible to both parties.

The default sort order
When a customer enters their address and service type, the cleaner list shows results sorted by what I'll call Match Score — a weighted combination of factors. Customers can change the sort, but the default is Match Score.

Match Score components:

Factor	Weight	What it measures
Distance from customer address	25%	Closer cleaners surfaced first; respects cleaner's set radius
Tier × score	25%	Higher tier and higher score within tier ranks higher
Availability fit	20%	Cleaners with the customer's preferred time blocks open rank higher
Recency of activity	10%	Active in last 14 days ranks higher than dormant
Customer affinity	10%	If customer has booked similar profiles before, similar cleaners boosted
Spotlight boost (Rising Pros only)	10%	New cleaners in their spotlight window get a fixed boost to compete
Tied results: broken by random seed that rotates daily. So two equally-matched cleaners alternate top position day to day. No cleaner is permanently buried because of an arbitrary alphabetical tie.

What's explicitly NOT in the algorithm
These are the things that would compromise fairness and that I want banned by design:

Pay-to-play. Cleaners cannot pay for higher placement. Period.
Demographic filtering. No age, gender, ethnicity, or photo-based factors in Match Score.
Customer demographic targeting. A cleaner's previous customer demographics don't affect what new customers see them.
Hidden penalties. Every score change is shown to the cleaner. If the algorithm ever penalizes them, they can see why.
The "Why am I seeing this cleaner?" disclosure
On the cleaner list, each card has a small info button. Tapping it shows: "Maria L. is matched because: 3.2 miles from your address, available Tuesday 10 AM, Top Performer tier with 4.8 rating across 240 cleanings, served 12 customers in your ZIP code."

This is the honesty layer. Customers see exactly why each cleaner is shown. No hidden ranking factors. If you can't explain a placement to a customer, the algorithm shouldn't be making it.

Explaining placement to cleaners too
On the cleaner dashboard: "Your visibility in customer searches" widget. Shows current placement rank in their primary ZIP, recent search appearances, tap-through rate, and what's affecting it (tier, score, availability fit, recency).

This is unprecedented in marketplaces. Almost no platform shows workers their own search ranking. Doing so prevents the "shadow ban" suspicion that plagues TaskRabbit, Handy, Uber. Cleaners can see exactly where they stand and what to do about it.

Search filters customers can apply
Customers can override default sort with explicit filters:

Sort by: Match Score (default) / Distance / Rating / Price (low to high) / Price (high to low) / Most recent
Filter by: Tier, price range, availability, specialty endorsement, language spoken, female cleaner only (if customer requests this for safety reasons), pet-friendly, eco-friendly
Note on "female cleaner only": this is a contentious filter that some platforms allow and others don't. It's legitimate in some contexts (single women living alone may want a woman in their home), but it can also be discriminatory. My recommendation: allow the filter but don't make it default-visible. It appears under "More options" and requires the customer to explicitly opt in. Document it as a safety preference, not a default browse option.

Repeat customer logic
If a customer has booked a specific cleaner before and rated them 4+ stars:

That cleaner appears in a "Your previous cleaners" section above the regular list
Pre-selected for repeat bookings
Bypasses the Match Score algorithm (the customer has already chosen)
This protects long-term customer-cleaner pairs from being disrupted by algorithm changes.

Summary of all changes locked this round
Two-track scoring: Performance Score (statistical, daily) + Tier Standing (insulated, monthly)
Tier-drop protection: 14-day sustained low performance required, max 1 drop per 14 days, 48-hour appeal window
Tier-promotion friction: mirrors drop rules
Veteran cushion: 6 months at 75+ → 30-day drop window + manual review on first drop
Refined trigger thresholds: 4.3 rating threshold, monthly reschedule grace, hard no-show triggers tightened
Tier rate ranges raised: $25–35, $35–50, $50–65, $60–80 (NorCal-realistic)
New cleaner featuring: spotlight rotation (7-day guarantee), $15 first-cleaning customer incentive, vouch system (Phase 2)
Match Score algorithm: transparent, explainable, no pay-to-play, no demographic targeting, every placement explainable
Visibility widget for cleaners: see their own ranking and what affects it
"Why am I seeing this cleaner?" for customers: every placement explained on tap
What's next
Two ways to go:

Wireframe the cleaner dashboard — score widget, tier widget, visibility widget, today's schedule, payments. This is where the cleaner sees the entire scoring system in action.
Wireframe the customer cleaner-list page — the Match Score sort, the spotlight section, the "Your previous cleaners" section, the filter UI, the "Why am I seeing this?" disclosure.
Both matter equally. The cleaner dashboard locks the trust story for cleaners (they see the system clearly). The cleaner list locks the trust story for customers (they see how matching works).

I'd recommend the cleaner dashboard first because it's the higher-stakes screen — if cleaners don't trust the scoring system, they leave the platform, and there's no marketplace.

Push back on any of the rebuilt scoring system. Specifically I want you to pressure-test the 14-day window. Is two weeks too long for tier drops? Too short? The right answer depends on how fast you think performance signals stabilize, and you have better instinct on cleaning industry dynamics than I do.

Photo compliance: drop the cadence violation penalty. Just require first photo within 15 min and required-room photos before clock-out.
A note on the rating threshold change. 3.8 is a sympathetic floor — most service marketplaces consider sub-4.0 a real problem. You're saying you'd rather give cleaners more grace than be tough on quality. That's a deliberate trade. The risk: customers who consistently see 3.8-rated cleaners on the platform may lose faith in the brand. Mitigation: 3.8 is the deduction floor, but a cleaner has to be averaging 3.8 over many jobs for it to materially hurt their score. One bad review doesn't drag a 4.7-average cleaner below threshold. That's fine.

A note on the suspension fix. You're right and I was wrong. Fixed 1-week suspension with clear endpoint is more honest than "pending review" with vague timeline. Cleaners can plan around a known week off. Vague suspensions create anxiety and erode trust in the platform. If a suspension is going to be longer than a week, that's a separate decision (termination review) and should be communicated as such.

What is an "active booking"?
Locked definition:

A booking is active from the moment the cleaner accepts (or the system instant-confirms it on cleaner's behalf via availability calendar) until the moment the cleaning is approved by the customer or auto-approved by 24-hour timeout.

Active booking phases:

Confirmed — booking exists, payment authorized, scheduled time hasn't arrived yet
Imminent — within 2 hours of scheduled start (this is when "On my way" button enables)
In-transit — cleaner has tapped "On my way," GPS pings every 3 min
In-progress — cleaner has clocked in, working
Awaiting approval — cleaner has clocked out, customer has 24 hours to review
Closed — approved by customer or auto-approved at 24-hour timeout
Communication SLAs (the 4-hour response rule we use in scoring) apply during phases 1–5. After closing, response time isn't tracked because the booking is done.

ZIP-locked badges — fully fleshed out
You said this is your favorite, so let me give it real depth. This is genuinely a strong feature and the implementation matters.

What ZIP-locked badges are
A badge a cleaner earns by performing well within a specific ZIP code. The badge is locked to that ZIP — it doesn't transfer to other areas. Customers searching within that ZIP see the badge prominently on the cleaner's card and profile.

Reasons this works:

Customers value local trust. A "Top-rated in 94110" cleaner feels more relevant than a "Top-rated overall" cleaner to someone in 94110.
It rewards localized work. Cleaners who work consistently in one neighborhood build deep familiarity (parking, building access, common requests). Badging this builds value into their local expertise.
It creates anchor cleaners per neighborhood. Each ZIP gets its own "trusted faces" who become the area regulars. This is how neighborhoods feel like communities.
How a cleaner earns a ZIP badge
Trusted by Neighbors badge — earned per ZIP code:

Minimum 10 completed jobs in that ZIP
Minimum 4.5 average rating across those jobs
All jobs completed within the last 12 months
Cleaner is currently in good standing (not on probation/suspension)
Once earned, the badge is displayed for 6 months. Renewal happens automatically as long as the criteria remain true on a rolling 12-month window. If criteria slip, the badge fades to "Inactive" status, then disappears after another 30 days. Cleaner gets notification at each transition.

Tier-locked variants
Higher tiers unlock harder-to-earn variants:

Trusted by Neighbors (any tier): 10+ jobs, 4.5+ rating in a ZIP
Top-rated in [ZIP] (Top Performer or above): 25+ jobs, 4.7+ rating in a ZIP
Customer favorite in [ZIP] (All-Star Expert): 5+ active recurring customers in a ZIP
A cleaner can hold multiple badges across multiple ZIPs. A great cleaner who works the Mission, Bernal Heights, and Noe Valley could be "Top-rated in 94110," "Trusted by Neighbors in 94110," "Top-rated in 94114," and so on. Customer-facing displays show badges for the customer's current search ZIP only — clean visual hierarchy.

How customers see ZIP badges
When a customer searches in 94110, every cleaner card shows their relevant 94110 badges. Cleaners with no 94110 history show no ZIP badges (their tier and overall rating still display). Cleaners from another ZIP serving 94110 show their tier and rating but no local badge — visually distinguishable from neighborhood regulars. This makes the local-vs-traveling distinction obvious and rewards local commitment.

Anti-gaming protections
Two protections against badge farming:

The 4.5+ rating threshold is calculated only on the ZIP-specific jobs, not platform-wide. Can't cherry-pick.
If a cleaner has 10 jobs in a ZIP but several disputes, they need extra rating margin — minimum 4.7 if any disputes are on file. This prevents "10 OK jobs and 1 horrendous one" from earning a badge.
Future ZIP-badge categories worth considering
Move-out specialist in [ZIP] — 10+ move-out jobs in that ZIP at 4.5+ rating
Airbnb regular in [ZIP] — 10+ Airbnb turnovers from same property cluster
Pet-friendly verified in [ZIP] — earned through customer pet-related ratings
These are Phase 2. Just the basic Trusted by Neighbors badge for launch.

Featuring new cleaners — your concern about volume
You're right to flag the influx problem. If 50 new cleaners sign up in one week and only 3-4 get spotlight slots per ZIP per day, some new cleaners wait weeks for visibility. That's a real cold-start failure.

Fix: dynamic spotlight allocation based on supply pressure.

Default: 3-4 spotlight slots per ZIP per day, rotating among new cleaners.

When supply pressure exceeds capacity (more new cleaners than slots can rotate within their 7-day window), expand the spotlight section to 6-8 slots per ZIP per day. The section grows; nobody waits.

When the section grows, customers notice — that itself is a signal of marketplace health ("lots of new cleaners coming on"). Honest framing: the section header changes from "New on PureTask" to "New cleaners joining this week" when expanded. No deception.

Hard guarantee: every approved cleaner gets a minimum 7 days of spotlight visibility, period. If volume forces longer rotation cycles, slots expand to maintain the guarantee. This is a non-negotiable promise and should be in cleaner onboarding.

You also pushed for showing new cleaners before the regular cleaner row. I agree. Restructured homepage cleaner row order:

New cleaners spotlight — top of cleaner section, header: "Joining PureTask this week"
Top-rated cleaners on PureTask — established cleaners, sorted by Match Score
Your previous cleaners (only shown if customer has booking history)
This gives new cleaners primacy on the page — first thing customers see. Established cleaners earn their visibility through tier and Match Score below.

You also moved the threshold from 10 to 6 jobs. Locking that. New cleaner spotlight pool = cleaners with <6 completed jobs OR <30 days on platform. Whichever clears them out faster.

Service area restrictions — your pushback
You said: my proposed restrictions had it backward. New cleaners should travel farther; veterans shouldn't have to.

You're right. Let me think through why.

My original logic was wrong. I had service area as a tier perk — bigger radius = better. The implicit assumption was that veterans deserve the convenience of staying close to home, while new cleaners earn distance.

Your logic is correct. New cleaners need more opportunity, not less. A new cleaner who can't travel can't get the volume of jobs they need to build score and tier. Restricting them to a 10-mile radius shrinks their addressable market and slows tier progression. Veterans, meanwhile, can afford to be picky — they have a regular customer base.

Revised:

No platform-imposed service area restrictions. Cleaners set their own radius from 5 to 50 miles, regardless of tier.
Veterans get the option to set "preferred area" filters — a smaller radius for primary work plus a larger "willing to travel for premium jobs" radius. This is a feature, not a restriction.
Service area becomes purely cleaner-controlled. Tier doesn't gate it.

"Top cleaners" dedicated section
You proposed a separate area for top cleaners with its own positioning advantage. I agree — let me design it.

Section name: "Top cleaners in your area"

Placement: appears between New Cleaners Spotlight and the regular cleaner list. Visible on:

Homepage (after customer enters address)
Cleaner list page
Service category pages
ZIP-specific city pages
Who qualifies:

All-Star Expert tier (score 90+)
AND at least one ZIP badge in the customer's search ZIP
AND completed 50+ total platform jobs
This creates a deliberately small pool. The intent is "these are the platform's elite cleaners in your area" — not "everyone with a high rating." Customers who want the absolute best, willing to pay top tier rates, click here.

Visual treatment:

Distinct card style (slightly larger, premium border treatment)
Badge prominent: "Top Cleaner"
Stats forward: "98% on-time, 247 cleanings, 4.9 average"
Photo larger, more prominent
What customers get from booking here:

All-Star tier service quality
Local reputation (ZIP badge proven)
Highest-trust signal on the platform
Strategic note: keeping this section small (5–10 cleaners per major ZIP) makes it feel curated. If 200 cleaners qualified, the badge would mean nothing. Tight gates = strong signal.

Customer favorites — your correction
You're right. Customers can favorite any tier cleaner without restriction. A customer who loves a Rising Pro should be able to favorite them and rebook them. The "first pick" mechanic for All-Stars was supposed to help All-Stars, but it accidentally created friction for customers who'd already chosen.

Revised customer favorites system:

Customer can favorite any cleaner regardless of tier
"Your favorites" section appears at the top of customer dashboard
Favorited cleaners get push notifications when their favoriter books a similar service ("Sarah just booked a deep clean — would you like to send her a quote?")
Favorited cleaners appear in customer's "Your previous cleaners" section even if they haven't booked them in a while
Cleaner-side benefit: the Customer Favorite badge for cleaners with 5+ active favorites is still meaningful — it just isn't gated to All-Star tier. A Rising Pro who blows away their early customers can earn this badge.

This makes the system more meritocratic. Customers vote with their favorites; cleaners earn badges based on those votes; tier doesn't artificially block the bottom from getting visible recognition.

Badges — full proposed list
You asked for a list. Here's a comprehensive starter set, organized by category:

Trust & verification badges (earned by all cleaners through onboarding)
ID Verified — government ID confirmed
Background Checked — Checkr or equivalent passed
Insurance Verified — proof of personal liability insurance on file
Annual Re-Verified — passed annual re-screening (separate from initial)
These are baseline. Every approved cleaner has these.

Performance badges (earned through metrics)
On-Time Pro — 95%+ on-time arrival across last 50 jobs
No-Show Free — zero no-shows in last 90 days, minimum 20 jobs
Photo Champion — 100% photo compliance on last 30 jobs
Quick Responder — average message response time under 1 hour over last 30 days
Reliable Communicator — zero communication violations in last 60 days
Customer relationship badges
Customer Favorite — 5+ active favorites
Repeat Booked — 75%+ of customers in last 30 days have booked them before
5-Star Streak — 10 consecutive 5-star reviews
ZIP-locked badges
Trusted by Neighbors in [ZIP] — 10+ jobs, 4.5+ rating per ZIP
Top-Rated in [ZIP] — 25+ jobs, 4.7+ rating per ZIP
Customer Favorite in [ZIP] — 5+ active recurring customers per ZIP
Specialty endorsement badges

Deep Clean Specialist — 15+ deep cleans at 4.5+ rating
Airbnb Pro — 25+ Airbnb turnovers at 4.5+ rating
Eco-Friendly Verified — uses verified eco products, 10+ jobs with positive eco-related notes
Pet-Friendly — 15+ jobs at homes with pets, 4.5+ rating
Family Home Pro — 15+ jobs at homes with children, 4.5+ rating
Veteran badges
One Year on PureTask — 12-month anniversary at 75+ score
Founding Member — first 100 cleaners on the platform (one-time award, lifetime badge)
Total: roughly 25 badges across 6 categories. Not all visible at once — cleaner profile shows top 4–6 most recently earned, full list available in expanded view.

Visual treatment: monochrome icons in a grid, with hover/tap revealing the badge name and how it was earned. No gold stars, no flashy graphics — clean, restrained, feels professional rather than gamified.

Anti-gaming: all badges show the date they were earned and the date they were last verified. Stale badges (earned >1 year ago, criteria no longer met) auto-archive. A cleaner can't display badges they've stopped earning.

Now — the pricing model
I have to be honest with you. Your proposed model has a real problem and I'd be doing you a disservice not to surface it.

What you proposed
Customer pays: cleaner's job subtotal + 15% service fee + $9.99 platform fee
Cleaner keeps: 100% of their listed subtotal
Cleaner pays: small monthly marketplace fee
Instant payout fee: 5% (cleaner pays)
What's appealing about this
The instinct is genuinely good. Most marketplaces take a cut from worker earnings, which feels exploitative. Your model says "cleaners keep 100% of what they earn for the work" — that's a powerful recruiting message and a real differentiator.

The hard problems
Problem 1: The math doesn't work for low-priced bookings.

Take a 2-hour Rising Pro cleaning at $25/hr. Cleaner's subtotal = $50.

Customer pays: $50 + $7.50 (15%) + $9.99 = $67.49
That $9.99 platform fee is 20% of the cleaner's subtotal.
Customer is paying 35% on top of cleaning cost in fees alone.
For a customer comparing PureTask to a private cleaner, that overhead looks bad. They might rationally book the Rising Pro off-platform on the second cleaning to avoid the $17.49 in fees.

Problem 2: The customer fee is regressive.

A $9.99 flat fee is ~5% of a $200 deep clean but ~20% of a $50 standard. The cheaper the cleaning, the worse the fee feels. This punishes Rising Pros and customers who need value cleanings — exactly the segment you're trying to grow.

Problem 3: The monthly marketplace fee creates a bad onboarding moment.

A new cleaner finishes their first month. They've earned $200 from 4 small jobs. A bill arrives: "Your monthly marketplace fee is $X."

This is the moment marketplace platforms lose cleaners. They've worked for a month, earned modestly, now they have to pay to keep working. Compare that to "we take 15% of each job" — at least the cost is invisible because it never enters the cleaner's account.

Problem 4: Monthly fees create a perverse incentive to off-platform.

Once a cleaner has built a customer relationship, they only need the platform if the value of being on it exceeds the monthly fee. A great cleaner with 5 regulars can text those regulars directly and skip the fee. Per-job commission models don't have this leak (every job pays the platform).

Problem 5: The 15% customer fee + monthly cleaner fee adds up to more than industry-standard 25-30% cleaner commissions in many scenarios.

Without doing your full unit economics, my back-of-envelope: at typical bookings around $80-150, your model takes more from the system than a 25% cleaner commission would, with the cost spread across customer and cleaner. You're not actually delivering "fair to cleaners" — you're shifting where the fee comes from.

What I'd recommend instead
A hybrid that captures your fairness instinct without the structural problems:

Per-job commission, transparent, low.

Cleaner pays 12% of subtotal per job, taken at payout time.
Customer pays a $4.99 booking fee per job (covers payment processing, fraud detection, dispute reserve).
No monthly fees on either side.
Instant payout: 5% fee, opt-in (already locked).
Weekly payout: free (already locked).
Run the same example: 2-hour Rising Pro at $25/hr.

Cleaner subtotal: $50
Customer pays: $50 + $4.99 = $54.99
Cleaner receives: $44 ($50 - 12% commission)
Platform earns: $4.99 + $6 = $10.99 per job
Compare to your model: $67.49 to customer, $50 to cleaner, $7.50 to platform. Your model takes more from the customer, gives more to the cleaner, takes less to the platform. That sounds great until you realize the platform needs to fund the very things you listed: GPS, photos, support, hosting, dispute resolution. Underfunding the platform fund kills the trust story.

Why 12% commission is fair to cleaners
Industry standard: 25-30% (TaskRabbit), 25% (Handy), 35% (Care.com)
12% is less than half industry standard. Real "fair to cleaners" positioning.
Cleaners can use this in their own value prop: "I keep 88% of what I earn."
Why the small customer fee is necessary
Platforms with no customer-side fee subsidize fraud and disputes from commission. With a 12% commission, you don't have margin to absorb a $200 dispute. The $4.99 fee creates a small reserve fund per booking that pays for the trust infrastructure.

Marketing the message
Your fairness instinct is the right marketing message. Reframe:

"Cleaners keep 88% of what they earn. We charge a flat 12% to fund the trust system that protects everyone — GPS verification, photo proof, payment escrow, customer support, and dispute resolution. No monthly fees. No hidden charges. The cleaner you book is paid fairly, and you get the platform protections every booking deserves."

That tells the same story (fair to cleaners, transparent) without the structural problems of the original model.

My pushback summary
Don't do flat-fee + monthly-fee model. It looks fair on paper, fails in practice, alienates value-seeking customers, and creates an off-platform leak. Use a low per-job commission with a small customer fee. Same fairness story, sustainable economics.

If you really want flat-fee, I can dig deeper into the unit economics with realistic booking distributions and show you the breakeven curves. But my strong recommendation is hybrid commission + small customer fee.

What's next
Reaction time. Specifically:

The pricing model. Do you want to argue your original model with more numbers? Or move forward with my hybrid recommendation? Or land somewhere in between?
Veteran cushion thresholds. Is 6 months at 75+ the right entry to veteran status? Should the cushion window be longer than 30 days?
The "Top cleaners in your area" qualification gates. All-Star + ZIP badge + 50 jobs — is that too tight? Too loose?
Once we lock these, we have an unprecedented amount of system design done. Then the wireframes can finally start in earnest, and they'll actually represent the real product instead of being placeholders that change every time we revisit a decision.

I think we're 85% done with system design. Three or four more rounds of decisions and we can wireframe with confidence.

May 2
Claude responded: Three big topics here.
Three big topics here. I'm going to take them one at a time because each one has real depth, and the "independent contractors" question fundamentally changes how I should be advising you on dispute policy.

1. Independent contractors — yes, this changes a lot
You raised the most important business-structure question on this entire project, and I've been wrong to not flag it earlier. Let me correct that now.

What "independent contractor" actually means here
If your cleaners are 1099 independent contractors (not W-2 employees), the legal framework you operate under is fundamentally different from what most "marketplace" platforms can assume. California is the strictest state in the country on this distinction because of AB5 and the ABC test.

The ABC test (California's standard): A worker is presumed to be an employee unless the hiring entity proves all three:

A: The worker is free from control and direction of the hiring entity in performing the work
B: The worker performs work outside the usual course of the hiring entity's business
C: The worker is customarily engaged in an independently established trade
Where this gets tricky for PureTask:

Prong B is where most "gig economy" platforms struggle. If your business is "house cleaning marketplace," and your workers do "house cleaning," you arguably fail prong B. Uber and Lyft fought this and ultimately got Prop 22 passed (2020) which carved out app-based drivers — but cleaning isn't covered by Prop 22. Other gig categories had to find different solutions.
Prong A — control — is where your platform design choices matter enormously. Every rule you set, every required photo, every clock-in mechanism, every reliability score is potentially evidence of "control."
What this means for the system we've designed
Some of our decisions need re-examination through the contractor-classification lens:

Things that strengthen the contractor argument:

Cleaners set their own rates (within tier ranges)
Cleaners set their own availability
Cleaners set their own service area
Cleaners can decline bookings
Cleaners use their own supplies and transportation
No required uniforms or branding
No exclusivity (cleaners can work other platforms)
Things that weaken the contractor argument (control flags):

Mandated photo cadence and required rooms
Mandated GPS tracking
Mandated clock-in/clock-out
Reliability scores that affect their access to work
Tier system that controls what they can charge
Required communication response times
Suspension powers for behavior
The honest truth: your platform sits in legally contested territory.

Plenty of cleaning marketplaces operate as "independent contractor" platforms in California, but several have been sued, and the landscape is unsettled. You need a real labor and employment attorney before launch. Not a generic startup lawyer — someone who specifically does worker classification under AB5. Budget $3-5k for a thorough review.

Specific changes worth considering
These are not legal advice (I'm not a lawyer), but they're patterns I've seen marketplaces use to stay defensible:

Frame all "rules" as platform requirements, not work direction. "Cleaners who use the platform must use the photo system" is structurally different from "PureTask requires cleaners to take photos." The former is a marketplace tool; the latter is an employer mandate.
Make tier benefits truly opt-in. Cleaners can choose not to participate in tier system, accepting lower visibility but full pricing freedom. Forced tier participation is a control flag.
Reframe reliability scoring as customer-facing trust signals, not job-allocation tools. "These are how customers see you" is different from "these determine your work."
Don't dictate methods. You can require deliverables (a clean home, photo proof) without dictating how to clean. Avoid checklists like "vacuum first, then dust."
Allow off-platform pricing freedom. A cleaner can take a booking and negotiate higher pay with the customer for additional services, without the platform forcing all transactions through it. Trying to lock all payments through your system is a control flag.
The dispute policy question — through this lens
This is now a much sharper question.

2. Your dispute policy proposal
You said: refund only on no-shows; if the cleaner showed up and cleaned, customer and cleaner work it out themselves; you mediate only when they can't.

My honest assessment: this is a smart instinct, contractor-aligned, but it has practical problems.

Why it's smart
It reinforces the contractor relationship. The cleaner-customer transaction is the primary one; PureTask is the marketplace, not the employer. Disputes between two independent parties are theirs to negotiate.
It limits your operational cost. Disputes are expensive to handle. A "platform decides" model means you're hiring dispute-resolution staff. A "let them work it out" model means most disputes never escalate.
It pushes accountability to the parties involved. Cleaners who show up and do honest work face customers directly, not a platform algorithm.
Why it has problems
Problem 1: The trust story falls apart at the moment of dispute.

Your entire homepage trust narrative is "GPS check-in, photo proof, pay only when satisfied, escrow." Then when a real dispute happens, you say "work it out with the cleaner." Customers will perceive this as a bait-and-switch. The trust system was supposed to enable easy resolution, not ask them to negotiate with the person they have a complaint about.

Problem 2: Power asymmetry.

In a customer-vs-cleaner dispute, who has more leverage to walk away? The customer, almost always. They can file a credit card chargeback if you don't refund. They can leave a 1-star review. They can post on social media. The cleaner has to honor the platform's process or risk their account. "Work it out" usually means the cleaner caves to keep their rating clean. That's not fair resolution; that's the cleaner subsidizing customer disputes.

Problem 3: Chargeback risk.

If a customer disputes with the cleaner, doesn't get satisfaction, and files a chargeback with their credit card company — you (the platform) lose. Stripe will pull the funds back, plus a $15-25 chargeback fee. Three or four chargebacks per 1000 transactions can put your Stripe account at risk. You can't actually opt out of dispute resolution, because the credit card system will impose one on you.

Problem 4: Cleaner vulnerability.

A customer who files a dispute, doesn't reach agreement with the cleaner, and chargebacks the platform — what happens to the cleaner who already got paid (or was about to)? Either the platform absorbs the loss (which is the trust system you didn't want to fund) or the cleaner gets their payout clawed back (which is brutal for someone who did the work).

What I'd recommend instead
A structured tiered dispute process that respects contractor relationships but doesn't leave anyone exposed:

Tier 1 — Direct resolution (0-48 hours after job).

When customer flags an issue, the system opens an in-app dispute thread between customer and cleaner.
Both parties have access to: clock-in/out times, all photos, messaging history, booking notes.
The cleaner can offer: partial refund, free re-clean, additional service to make it right.
The customer can accept or counter.
If they reach agreement, payment is adjusted accordingly. Done.
Most disputes resolve here. Probably 70-80% in healthy marketplaces.
Tier 2 — Platform mediation (after 48 hours of stalemate).

Either party requests mediation. A support specialist reviews evidence (the photos, GPS data, clock times, both narratives).
Mediator proposes a resolution. Most common outcomes: 25%, 50%, or 100% refund based on photo evidence.
Both parties can accept or escalate to Tier 3.
This handles maybe 15-25% of disputes.
Tier 3 — Final platform decision (rare).

Stalemate after mediation = platform makes a binding call.
Decision is based on a documented rubric (clock times honored, photos show completed work, customer's complaint is or isn't substantiated).
Decision applies. Either party can dispute their account standing if they think the call was wrong.
Maybe 5% of disputes end here.
Hard policy: no-shows always full refund. That's locked.

Hard policy: confirmed safety incidents always full refund + cleaner suspension review. Non-negotiable.

Soft policy: documented partial completion (cleaner clocked in late, missed required rooms, left early) gets proportional refund. This is where photo proof and clock data do the work.

How to talk about disputes publicly
Your public-facing dispute policy can keep your "we're here to help mediate, but the relationship is between you and the cleaner" framing. The contractor-aligned messaging is fine. What you can't do is have no mechanism — the credit card system will impose one whether you want it or not.

Recommended dispute policy section to write:

"PureTask connects you with independent professional cleaners. Disputes are first an opportunity for you and your cleaner to make things right — most issues are resolved this way. If you and your cleaner can't reach agreement, our mediation team will review the evidence: GPS check-in/out times, photo documentation, and your messaging history. We'll suggest a fair resolution. In rare cases that can't be settled through mediation, PureTask will make a final decision based on the platform's documented dispute rubric. No-show situations are always fully refunded."

That's contractor-aligned, customer-trustworthy, and credit-card-system-compliant.

3. Pricing model — the corrected version
I owe you a clearer answer. Let me address what I missed.

What I didn't account for in my first answer
You're right that I didn't model tier ranges and earnings potential thoroughly. Let me do that now.

Cleaner earnings under hybrid model (12% commission, $4.99 customer fee):

Tier	Rate	2-hr job customer pays	Cleaner earns	Platform earns
Rising Pro	$30/hr	$64.99	$52.80	$12.19
Proven Specialist	$40/hr	$84.99	$70.40	$14.59
Top Performer	$55/hr	$114.99	$96.80	$18.19
All-Star Expert	$70/hr	$144.99	$123.20	$21.79
For 4-hour jobs, double the cleaner subtotals. Customer fee stays $4.99 (per booking, not per hour).

Your minimum-hour idea — yes, this is smart
Locking: minimum 4 hours for Rising Pro and Proven Specialist; minimum 2 hours for Top Performer and All-Star Expert.

This is a real solution to several problems:

Makes Rising Pro economically viable. A 4-hour minimum at $30/hr = $120 cleaner subtotal. After 12% = $105.60. That's a real day's pay anchor, not a $50 token.
Reduces customer-side feeling of "fee overhead." The $4.99 fee is 4% of a $120 booking, not 10% of a $50 booking.
Aligns with what the lower tiers actually deliver — Rising Pros are still proving themselves, so a 4-hour booking gives them real time to do the job well.
Higher tiers earn the privilege of shorter bookings (and higher rates) — small touch-up bookings are a perk for the top, not a default.
About cleaner attitudes toward platform commissions
Let me re-answer your question precisely.

You asked: "Cleaners on other platforms don't have an issue with the platform taking a cut?"

Honest answer: they have a huge issue with it. Cleaners on TaskRabbit, Handy, Thumbtack consistently complain about platform fees. Reddit threads, cleaner forums, Twitter — high-commission platforms get torched constantly. Cleaners feel exploited.

But here's the nuance you were missing: they accept it because the platform delivers value (bookings, payment processing, marketing, dispute infrastructure, customer support). The complaint isn't "I don't want to pay anything." It's "I'm paying too much for what I get."

The right business question isn't "how do I avoid taking a commission?" It's "how do I take the smallest commission that still funds a sustainable platform?"

Your instinct — making customers pay more so cleaners pay less — is good values, but it has a cap. Customers will tolerate fees if the value is clear. They won't tolerate fees that look unreasonable next to obvious alternatives. Your $9.99 + 15% structure crosses that line on small bookings.

The right mental model
A platform fee is the price of the trust system you're selling. Customers pay because the platform delivers something off-platform alternatives don't: verification, photo proof, GPS, escrow, support. Cleaners pay (via commission) because the platform delivers customer flow they couldn't get on their own.

Both parties pay because both parties get value. The trick is making each party feel like the value exceeds their cost.

Final pricing recommendation
Pulling everything together:

12% cleaner commission — taken at payout. Cleaner sees their net earnings clearly: "You completed 5 jobs this week. Subtotals: $480. Platform commission: $57.60. Net earnings: $422.40."
$4.99 customer booking fee — flat per booking, displayed prominently in checkout.
No monthly fees — eliminate the off-platform leak risk and the bad onboarding moment.
Weekly free payouts; 5% instant payout fee — locked.
Tier-based hour minimums — Rising Pro & Proven Specialist 4-hour minimum; Top Performer & All-Star Expert 2-hour minimum.
Public-facing message:

"Cleaners keep 88% of what they earn. PureTask takes a flat 12% to fund the trust system: GPS verification, photo proof, payment escrow, dispute resolution, and ongoing platform support. Customers pay a small $4.99 booking fee per cleaning — that's it. No monthly cleaner fees, no hidden charges, no surprises. The price you see is the price you pay."

That positions you as fair-to-cleaners (12% is half industry standard), transparent (one clear customer fee), and sustainable (real revenue per booking).

A counter-proposal worth considering
If you genuinely want to differentiate on "cleaners pay nothing per job," there's a model that works: subscription instead of monthly fees. Cleaners pay nothing per job, but pay a tiered monthly subscription based on tier.

Rising Pro: free (no monthly fee, builds platform supply)
Proven Specialist: $20/month
Top Performer: $45/month
All-Star Expert: $75/month
The subscription is a fixed cost that high-tier cleaners (earning more) absorb easily, while new cleaners pay nothing. Customer fees become $4.99 + 15% (your original instinct), and cleaners keep 100% per job.

Why this works better than your original:

Rising Pros pay nothing while building reputation
High earners pay flat fees that don't scale with income (so a great month doesn't cost more)
Customer fees fund baseline platform; subscriptions fund supply-side benefits
Why I still don't recommend it for launch:

Subscription billing is operational complexity you don't need at launch
High-tier cleaners might churn off-platform once they hit sub fee threshold
Per-job commission is simpler and more sustainable
But it's a viable Phase 2 if you want to make the "no commission per job" story your brand.

Independent contractor classification confirmed. Real lawyer needed before launch.
Dispute policy: structured 3-tier (direct → mediation → platform decision). Public messaging keeps your contractor-aligned framing.
Hour minimums: 4-hour for Rising Pro and Proven Specialist; 2-hour for Top Performer and All-Star Expert.
Pricing: 12% cleaner commission, $4.99 customer booking fee, no monthly fees. Subscription model deferred to Phase 2.
