On photo proof as the dispute equalizer
You're right, and this sharpens the dispute system meaningfully. Let me work through it.

Your argument: with real photo evidence, both parties have to be reasonable. A customer can't claim the kitchen was filthy when there's a photo of a clean kitchen. A cleaner can't claim they did the job if photos show missed rooms.

This works because:

Photos are objective. Either the after-photo shows a clean room or it doesn't.
Both parties know photos exist. This shapes behavior before a dispute even happens — a cleaner who knows their work is documented does better work; a customer who knows the cleaner has photos thinks twice before claiming the job was sloppy.
Disputes get resolved on evidence, not on who's louder.
Where it still doesn't fully solve disputes:

"The photos don't show the corner where I found the dirt" — photos can't capture everything
"The cleaner stole something" — photos don't address theft
"The cleaner damaged my couch" — photos document state at clock-out, not damage discovered later
Service quality complaints that aren't visible (smell, "feels not clean enough")
So photo proof handles maybe 70-80% of dispute scenarios cleanly. The remaining 20-30% still need a mediation path.

Refined dispute policy (your model + safety nets)
Step 1: Direct resolution with photo evidence (0-48 hours).

Customer flags an issue → in-app dispute thread opens
Both parties have full access: photos, GPS times, messages, booking notes
The cleaner can respond with the photos as their defense ("Here's the kitchen at clock-out — does this match your concern?")
The customer can respond with their own photos showing the issue
Negotiation happens between them
Step 2: Photo-based auto-resolution (rare, but worth building).

If the cleaner provides full photo coverage of all required rooms AND the customer hasn't uploaded counter-evidence within 48 hours, the dispute auto-resolves in the cleaner's favor
Customer is notified at 24 hours: "If you don't upload counter-evidence by [time], the cleaner's documentation will stand"
This protects cleaners from passive-aggressive disputes where the customer just doesn't engage
Step 3: Mediation (only when photos don't settle it).

Theft accusations
Damage discovered after clock-out
Service quality issues not photographable
Cases where both parties have evidence but disagree
Mediator reviews, proposes resolution
Step 4: Final platform decision.

True stalemates only
Documented rubric
Why this is better than "platform decides everything": the photo evidence model puts the burden of proof in the right place. The cleaner has to provide documentation. The customer has to provide counter-evidence to dispute it. Both parties are accountable for their claims.

Why this is better than "they work it out, no platform involvement": chargeback risk is still handled. When a customer escalates to credit card dispute, you can show Stripe the photo evidence and dispute thread — that's evidence of completed service, which generally wins chargebacks.

This is the right balance. Locked.

One critical addition: the "offer a make-right" option
In Step 1 of dispute, the cleaner should have a structured way to offer a remedy without admitting full liability. Three options the cleaner can propose:

Free re-clean — cleaner returns at no cost to redo the disputed area
Partial refund — cleaner offers a percentage back ($X off the booking)
Cleaner stands by work — submits photos as evidence, dispute escalates if customer rejects
Most disputes get resolved by option 1 or 2. Customers usually want their issue fixed, not their money back. Re-cleans are cheap (one extra trip) and usually leave the customer happier than a refund. This is the smartest move for cleaners and the platform should make it easy.

Now — the pricing model
You proposed:

10-15% cleaner commission, scaled by tier (lower commission for higher tiers)
$9.99 customer booking fee
Let me run actual numbers on this, because tier-scaled commissions are an interesting idea I want to take seriously.

How tier-scaled commissions could work
Higher tiers earn more revenue per job, so flat commission means platform earns more from them in dollar terms. But scaling commission down with tier rewards loyalty and retention.

Tier	Proposed commission
Rising Pro	15%
Proven Specialist	13%
Top Performer	11%
All-Star Expert	10%
Running real numbers across tiers
Rising Pro: 4-hour standard at $30/hr (their tier minimum)

Cleaner subtotal: $120
Customer pays: $120 + $9.99 = $129.99
Cleaner earns: $120 - 15% = $102
Platform earns: $9.99 + $18 = $27.99
Proven Specialist: 4-hour standard at $40/hr

Cleaner subtotal: $160
Customer pays: $160 + $9.99 = $169.99
Cleaner earns: $160 - 13% = $139.20
Platform earns: $9.99 + $20.80 = $30.79
Top Performer: 2-hour standard at $55/hr

Cleaner subtotal: $110
Customer pays: $110 + $9.99 = $119.99
Cleaner earns: $110 - 11% = $97.90
Platform earns: $9.99 + $12.10 = $22.09
All-Star Expert: 2-hour standard at $70/hr

Cleaner subtotal: $140
Customer pays: $140 + $9.99 = $149.99
Cleaner earns: $140 - 10% = $126
Platform earns: $9.99 + $14 = $23.99
Strategic analysis
What's good about this model:

Customer fee stays manageable on real bookings. $9.99 is 5-8% of total cost across realistic bookings (because hour minimums prevent tiny bookings). At $129.99 minimum (Rising Pro 4-hour), the fee is 7.7% of total. That's livable.
The tier-scaled commission is a real cleaner retention lever. A Rising Pro paying 15% vs. an All-Star paying 10% feels like earned reward. It's also a powerful recruiting message: "Stay on PureTask and your platform fees go down as you prove yourself."
Platform earns reasonable revenue per booking. $22-31 per booking is healthy. At 1000 bookings per month, that's $22-31k MRR — enough to fund infrastructure and a small team.
Higher-tier cleaners see real income gains. All-Star Expert at $70/hr × 2 hours = $140 subtotal × 90% = $126 net. That's a high-end NorCal hourly rate, sustainable for full-time cleaning income.
What I'd push back on:

Concern 1: Rising Pro 15% might be too high to attract new cleaners during cold start.

If you're competing for cleaning supply and Handy charges 25%+, then 15% is competitive. But your messaging is "cleaners keep 85-90%." A new cleaner doing the math — "I can keep 85% on PureTask or 75% on Handy, but Handy has more customers" — might still pick Handy at launch when your customer base is small.

Counter-recommendation: Make Rising Pro 12% during the 6-job onboarding window. After the 6-job graduation, commission moves to 13-15% based on tier. This gives new cleaners the "lowest barrier" message during the period they're most likely to abandon your platform.

Concern 2: The customer fee at $9.99 still feels regressive at low tiers.

Even with the 4-hour minimum at Rising Pro, $9.99 on a $120 cleaner subtotal is 7.7% — and customers see it as a fixed line item, which feels worse than a percentage. They notice "$10 fee" more than they'd notice "8% fee."

Counter-recommendation: Consider $7.99 as the customer fee. Reasoning:

Still well within the range that customer-pays-platform models work
6.7% on Rising Pro's $120 minimum, 5.7% on All-Star's $140 — both feel reasonable
Round number alternative ($7.99 vs. $9.99) — psychologically, the gap from $7.99 to acceptance is smaller
If you want to keep $9.99, that's fine — you're sacrificing some conversion at the bottom for more platform revenue. Reasonable trade. Just be aware of the tradeoff.

Updated combined recommendation
Cleaner commissions (tier-scaled):

Rising Pro (during first 6 jobs): 12%
Rising Pro (post-graduation): 15%
Proven Specialist: 13%
Top Performer: 11%
All-Star Expert: 10%
Customer booking fee: $9.99 (per your call) or $7.99 (my recommendation).

Other fees: weekly payouts free, 5% instant payout fee, no monthly fees.

Hour minimums: 4-hour for Rising Pro / Proven Specialist, 2-hour for Top Performer / All-Star Expert.

Marketing message (refined)
"Cleaners keep 85-90% of what they earn. PureTask takes a small commission that lowers as you prove yourself — earn your way to keeping more. New cleaners pay only 12% during their first 6 jobs while building reputation. No monthly fees, no hidden charges. Weekly payouts are free; instant payouts are available with a 5% fee.

Customers pay a flat $9.99 booking fee per cleaning — that's it. No surprise charges. You see the full price before you book."

That tells two stories at once: cleaners are fairly compensated and rewarded for excellence; customers pay one transparent fee.

Other things to surface from this conversation
Two implications of the tier-scaled commission system worth flagging:

1. Tier drop = commission goes up. A Top Performer (11%) who slips to Proven Specialist (13%) sees their commission rise by 2%. That's a real income hit on top of the rate-range drop. Make sure the tier change notification clearly states this: "Your commission rate is now 13% (was 11%)."

This actually reinforces the tier system — there's now a financial cost to falling, not just a rate-ceiling cost. Cleaners care more about their tier when slipping costs them margin too.

2. Tier promotion = commission goes down. A Proven Specialist climbing to Top Performer sees their commission drop from 13% to 11%. That's tangible reward beyond the rate-range bump. Make this prominent in the promotion notification: "Your commission rate is now 11% (was 13%) — you're keeping more of what you earn."

Both directions, the financial layer reinforces the tier narrative. Good.

Cleaner can offer free re-clean, partial refund, or stand by work in dispute thread.
Cleaner commission: tier-scaled, 10-15%, with first-6-jobs window at 12% for Rising Pros.
Customer fee: $9.99 (your call) — though I'd recommend $7.99.
Tier changes carry commission-rate changes that should be communicated explicitly.

A note on the dispute window
Your 48-hour window is reasonable, but I want to surface one nuance before we lock the visual design.

The math of the dispute timeline:

Cleaner clocks out → customer has 24 hours to approve, dispute, or auto-approve happens
Customer approves → 48-hour dispute window opens from approval moment
Customer doesn't engage → 48-hour dispute window opens from auto-approval moment
So the maximum total time from clock-out to "dispute window closed" is 24 + 48 = 72 hours. That's three full days of dispute eligibility from the moment the cleaner finishes — fair to customers without leaving cleaners exposed for weeks.

One edge case worth thinking about: theft and damage discovered later.

Theft and damage are sometimes not noticed for days. A customer comes home, doesn't see anything wrong, approves the booking, and three weeks later discovers a missing necklace. After the 48-hour window, they can't dispute through PureTask.

Two ways to handle this:

Option A — strict 48-hour window for everything (your current decision). Clean policy, easy to communicate. The risk: a customer with a legitimate but late-discovered theft case has no platform recourse and may go to law enforcement, social media, or chargeback. Chargebacks bypass your dispute window entirely (Stripe gives 60-120 days depending on card network).

Option B — 48-hour window for service quality disputes; 30-day window for theft and damage claims that require police report or insurance documentation. More complex policy, but matches the reality that theft is rarely discovered in 48 hours.

My recommendation: Option B, but only if the longer window requires harder evidence. A theft claim filed at day 20 needs a filed police report. A damage claim needs photos with timestamps and an insurance assessment. This prevents abuse (frivolous late claims) while protecting customers with real cases.

If you want to keep your simpler 48-hour rule for everything, that's fine — just understand the chargeback exposure. The credit card networks will give the customer 60+ days regardless of your platform policy, so you're not actually saving yourself from theft disputes; you're just routing them through a more expensive channel (chargebacks cost $15-25 per case plus the funds clawback).

Which way do you want to go?

Insurance and liability — what PureTask covers when a cleaner damages something. This affects the dispute system (you need a payout source for confirmed damage claims) and your terms of service. Real lawyer territory but I can help you scope what to ask them.
Customer account gates — when does a customer have to create an account? Guest checkout is conversion-friendly but creates fraud risk. Required signup is friction. There's a middle path.
Cleaner onboarding flow — step-by-step what "become a cleaner" looks like. Identity verification, background check, payout setup, profile creation, training screens. This is a multi-screen flow that affects supply growth.
Once those three are locked, we have everything needed to wireframe with confidence — the cleaner dashboard, the cleaner active-job screen, and the customer cleaner-list page. Those three screens will demonstrate the entire system design we've built.

Moving to insurance and liability. This is the topic where I most strongly need to caveat: I am not a lawyer. Everything below is a structural framework to help you scope conversations with a real attorney. None of this is legal advice. Get a labor and employment lawyer plus a small-business insurance broker before launch.

Insurance and liability — the structural decisions
The core problem
A cleaner working in a customer's home will, over time, break something. A vase, a TV, a window during cleaning, a stained couch from a chemical reaction. It's statistically certain — across thousands of cleanings, a non-trivial percentage involve damage.

The question is: who pays?

There are three possible structures, and the choice shapes your business model, your terms of service, and your relationship with cleaners.

Structure A — Cleaner is fully responsible
PureTask takes no liability. The cleaner is an independent contractor; if they damage something, they pay. PureTask facilitates the conversation but doesn't fund the outcome.

Pros:

Reinforces independent contractor classification (good for your AB5 defense)
Zero insurance overhead for the platform
Cheapest model
Cons:

A cleaner who breaks a $2000 TV faces a financial catastrophe they can't absorb
Without coverage, cleaners may simply walk away from the platform after damage incidents
Customers expect platforms to stand behind their service
Without a payout source, dispute resolution is theoretical — you can rule "cleaner owes $500" but if the cleaner doesn't pay, the customer loses
This is what TaskRabbit did originally, and it created constant cleaner-customer disputes that the platform got blamed for.

Structure B — PureTask provides platform-level coverage
PureTask carries a commercial general liability policy that covers damage caused by cleaners during platform-booked jobs. Coverage limits are clearly defined ($2-5k per incident, $1M aggregate is typical for marketplaces). Cleaners are covered as a benefit of platform use.

Pros:

Powerful customer trust message ("Every booking is backed by $X in damage protection")
Powerful cleaner recruiting message ("Work on PureTask without worrying about ruining your life if you break something")
Real dispute payout source
Industry-standard for marketplace platforms
Cons:

Insurance cost is real (estimate $3-8k/year for a starting marketplace, scaling with GMV)
Possible erosion of independent contractor argument (you're providing employee-like benefits)
Subject to policy exclusions, deductibles, and claim approval
This is what Handy, Care.com, and most established marketplaces do.

Structure C — Hybrid, with cleaner-required insurance
PureTask doesn't carry coverage but requires every cleaner to carry their own personal liability insurance ($300-500/year for cleaning professionals, $1M coverage is standard) as a condition of being on the platform. Cleaners upload proof during onboarding, renewed annually.

Pros:

Strongest independent contractor defense (cleaners are real businesses with real insurance)
No platform insurance cost
Customer still gets coverage outcome (just from cleaner's policy, not platform's)
Real damages get real payouts
Cons:

Real friction at cleaner acquisition. New cleaners often don't have insurance and balking at $300-500 to join your platform is a real conversion killer.
Inconsistent coverage limits across cleaners
Verifying insurance is real and current is operational overhead
Insurance lapses mid-year are hard to catch
This is what Thumbtack does for high-stakes service categories.

My recommendation
Hybrid leaning toward B with a clear scope.

PureTask carries a small platform damage-protection guarantee for the first year of operation. Coverage up to $1000 per incident, with a $100 customer deductible, paid from a self-insured pool funded by 1% of GMV.
Cleaners are encouraged but not required to carry personal insurance for Year 1. Listed as a benefit of higher tiers ("Insurance Verified" badge for cleaners who upload proof).
Year 2 and beyond, evaluate moving to Structure C (cleaner-required insurance) as supply grows. By then you have leverage with cleaners — they need PureTask more than PureTask needs them, so insurance requirement becomes accepted.
The self-insured pool is critical. Don't go to a real insurance underwriter at launch — you don't have the volume data they want, premiums will be punishing, and you'll be locked into terms. Self-insured (you set aside 1% of every booking into a damage fund) is cheaper and more flexible. Once you have 12+ months of damage data, take it to an underwriter and likely get better terms than launching cold.

The customer-facing message:

"Every PureTask booking includes up to $1000 in damage protection. If your cleaner damages something during a cleaning, file a claim within 48 hours and we'll work with you on resolution."

Clean, simple, defensible. The $100 deductible exists to discourage frivolous claims (someone scratching a wall and demanding $50) while still being reasonable for real damages.

Damage claim flow
Tied to the dispute system we already built:

Customer flags damage during the 48-hour dispute window
Photo evidence uploaded by both parties (customer shows damage, cleaner shows clock-out condition)
If damage is clearly visible in customer's photos but absent in cleaner's clock-out photos: approved for payout from damage fund
If damage is ambiguous or contested: mediation with documentation
Approved claims paid from self-insured pool (PureTask earns goodwill, cleaner doesn't pay out of pocket, but cleaner gets a strike against reliability score for confirmed damage incidents)
This is why photo proof at clock-out matters so much — it's not just service documentation, it's damage baseline documentation.

What's NOT covered
Be explicit in your TOS:

Pre-existing damage (cleaner's clock-out photos help here)
Damage from improper customer instructions (customer asked for chemical that damaged their counter)
Theft (handled through dispute mediation, not damage fund)
Personal injury (separate liability insurance, not damage protection)
Damage outside the cleaning area (customer's car parked in driveway, etc.)
Theft policy specifically: confirmed theft (police report filed, criminal charges) results in cleaner termination plus customer being made whole through the damage fund up to coverage limit. This is a serious enough event to bypass normal dispute flow. Stays within the 48-hour window for filing through PureTask, but a customer can pursue criminal charges independently.

Personal injury — separate concern
If a cleaner falls down a staircase at a customer's home and is injured, that's workers' compensation territory — and independent contractors don't get workers' comp from the platform. You should:

Inform cleaners during onboarding that they're responsible for their own health insurance and disability coverage
Recommend (not require) they consider occupational accident insurance
Make clear that PureTask is not their employer
If a customer is injured by a cleaner (assault, gross negligence), that's commercial general liability — your platform policy should cover up to a reasonable limit ($1M is standard).

Cleaner-side protection
The damage fund protects customers. Cleaners need a parallel protection: false claims.

A customer who files a damage claim that's denied based on photo evidence pays a $25 frivolous claim fee on their next booking
A pattern of denied claims (3+ in 6 months) flags the customer for restricted booking status
Cleaners can see the claim denial in their reliability score breakdown — denied claims do not count against their score
This balances the system. Customers can't weaponize the damage process; cleaners can't be permanently scarred by an accusation that didn't hold up.

Required documents to draft (lawyer territory)
Before launch, you need a real lawyer to draft:

Cleaner Independent Contractor Agreement — defines the relationship, includes worker classification language, includes cleaner indemnification of platform for cleaner-caused damages above coverage
Platform Terms of Service (customer side) — defines the damage protection, dispute process, and liability limits
Damage Claim Policy — formal version of what we just outlined, including filing requirements, evidence standards, payout procedures
Privacy Policy — covers photo handling, data retention, CCPA compliance
Insurance certificates — once your self-insured pool is funded, you need documentation that the pool exists for your records
Estimated total legal cost for these documents: $5-10k from a small-business attorney. Required before public launch. Not optional.

Lock these decisions
Answer those three and we'll move to customer account gates next, then cleaner onboarding flow. After that — wireframes.

I want to be honest about how much we still don't know. The insurance recommendations above are the framework I'd give a founder before they talk to a lawyer. Real numbers (coverage limits, premium costs, deductibles) will come from your actual lawyer and broker conversations. Don't lock these as final business decisions — lock them as "this is the structure I want to discuss with my lawyer." The lawyer may push back on specifics for legal reasons I can't anticipate.

Claude responded: Got it.
Got it. Locking the simpler structure you want.

Insurance and liability — locked
PureTask carries no platform-level damage coverage. Cleaners are independent contractors, fully responsible for damages they cause during cleanings. Cleaners are strongly encouraged to carry personal liability insurance but it is not required for platform participation.

This is closer to Structure A from my earlier breakdown, with elements of C (insurance encouraged but not mandated). It's simpler, cheaper, and reinforces your independent contractor classification. Let me make sure you understand exactly what you're accepting and what needs to be done to make it defensible.

What this means in practice
For damage incidents:

Customer files dispute within 48-hour window with photo evidence
Cleaner responds with their clock-out photos and explanation
Direct resolution between parties (cleaner can offer to pay, partial refund, or contest)
If they can't agree, mediation reviews evidence and proposes resolution
If platform makes a final call, the ruling determines responsibility — but PureTask doesn't fund the payout from a damage pool. The cleaner pays the customer directly, or the customer's claim goes unpaid if the cleaner refuses.
For unrecoverable damages:

A cleaner without personal insurance who breaks a $2000 TV and refuses to pay: customer files a chargeback through their credit card. Stripe pulls the funds back from PureTask. PureTask debits the cleaner's account or future payouts. If the cleaner has no future earnings to claw back from, you eat the loss.
For most damage incidents, this is rare. The dispute thread + photo evidence + the cleaner's reliability score on the line is usually enough to get cleaners to pay reasonable damage claims.
What you need in your terms of service
Three things must be airtight in your customer-facing TOS to make this structure work:

1. Liability disclosure. Customers need to know upfront that PureTask doesn't insure damages — the cleaner does (or doesn't). Burying this in fine print is bad faith and a chargeback magnet.

Suggested customer-facing language:

"Cleaners on PureTask are independent professionals responsible for the quality and outcome of their work, including any damages caused during a cleaning. PureTask provides the platform tools — photo verification, dispute mediation, payment escrow — to help resolve issues fairly, but does not directly insure or guarantee against damages. Cleaners are strongly encouraged to carry personal liability insurance, and many do. You can see which cleaners are insurance-verified on their profile."

2. Dispute mediation scope. Be clear that you mediate, not adjudicate, and that mediation outcomes depend on evidence quality.

3. Cleaner indemnification clause. In the Cleaner Independent Contractor Agreement, cleaners explicitly agree to indemnify PureTask against damage claims. This means if a customer sues the platform for cleaner damage, the cleaner is the responsible party and PureTask passes legal costs along. Lawyer-required clause.

What you need on the cleaner side
During onboarding:

Clear disclosure: "You are an independent contractor. PureTask does not insure your work. If you damage a customer's property, you are responsible. We strongly recommend carrying personal liability insurance — it typically costs $300-500/year and protects you from financial catastrophe."
Optional insurance upload during onboarding. Cleaners who upload proof get an "Insurance Verified" badge on their profile.
Annual reminder to renew/upload insurance certificate.
The Insurance Verified badge becomes a competitive advantage. Customers shopping cleaners can filter or sort for insured cleaners. This creates organic pressure for cleaners to insure themselves without you mandating it. Market dynamics solve what policy doesn't.

Two protective additions worth adding
1. Damage fund optional, but at the cleaner's expense.

Build a feature: cleaners can opt into a "platform damage protection plan" for a small per-job fee (say, 1% of subtotal) that gives them up to $500 coverage per incident from a pooled fund. This isn't required, isn't subsidized by you, and isn't visible to customers as a platform guarantee — it's just an opt-in cleaner-paid benefit. This is a real product feature for risk-averse cleaners and creates a small revenue stream for you without changing the platform's liability posture.

This is Phase 2 — too complex to launch with. Just architect for it.

2. Frivolous claim protections for cleaners.

Even without a platform damage fund, you should still protect cleaners from abusive customers. A customer who files a damage claim that's denied based on photo evidence:

First denial: warning email
Second denial in 6 months: account flagged for review
Third denial: account restricted to request-to-book mode (cleaners can decline)
No fees. Just a behavioral gate that protects cleaners from serial false claims. This protection costs you nothing and helps cleaner retention.

Final pricing summary (everything locked together)
Element	Decision
Cleaner commission, first 6 jobs (Rising Pro onboarding)	12%
Cleaner commission, Rising Pro post-graduation	15%
Cleaner commission, Proven Specialist	13%
Cleaner commission, Top Performer	11%
Cleaner commission, All-Star Expert	10%
Customer booking fee	$9.99 per booking
Weekly payouts	Free, every Friday ~noon Pacific
Instant payouts	5% fee, opt-in
Monthly fees (cleaner or customer)	None
Hour minimums	4 hours for Rising Pro / Proven Specialist; 2 hours for Top Performer / All-Star Expert
Damage liability	Cleaner is fully responsible. Insurance encouraged, not required.
Insurance Verified badge	Available to cleaners who upload proof
Dispute window	48 hours from approval (or auto-approval)
Chargeback handling	Photo evidence used to defend chargebacks; losses absorbed by platform when cleaners can't be debited

Quick framing on the questions before you answer:

Account gates. The trade is conversion vs. fraud risk. Guest browsing converts better; required signup gates fraud. For your launch stage, my lean is "guest until payment" — first impressions matter, and your fraud risk is low while volume is low.

Signup method. Multiple options is the default modern pattern but adds dev complexity. Email + magic link is the cleanest single-method choice for a launch product. Phone + SMS is great for mobile-first products but creates SMS cost overhead.

Cleaner onboarding. Hybrid is what most platforms do for the first 50-100 cleaners. You personally interview/approve early cohorts (catches bad fits, builds personal cleaner relationships, reduces fraud), then automate as you scale. Self-serve from day one risks bad supply early; fully manual doesn't scale past the first 20.

Answer these and we'll move to wireframes — or if you want to iterate on any, push back.

May 2
Claude responded: Good.
Good. All three locked. Let me give you my honest read on each before moving on.

Locked this round
Customer account gate: payment step only. Customers can browse cleaners, build a booking, see prices, view reviews — all as guest. Account creation triggers at the moment of payment.

Customer signup: email, Google, Apple, phone. Four sign-in methods at the auth gate.

Cleaner onboarding: hybrid self-serve + manual review. Cleaners apply through a self-serve form, you review and approve manually for the first cohort, then automate as scale demands.

Honest read on these choices
Account gate is the right call. Conversion-friendly. Standard ecommerce pattern. The one thing you need to plan for: when the customer creates their account at payment, the booking they were building must persist through that signup flow. If they fill out their address, pick a cleaner, pick a time, then bounce to signup — the booking state must survive the auth handoff. This is non-trivial dev work but critical. Don't make customers re-enter anything after they create the account.

Multiple signup methods is the right call but expensive to build well. Each provider has its own integration:

Google OAuth — easy, well-documented, 1-2 days
Apple Sign In — required if you ever ship to iOS App Store, 2-3 days
Email + password OR magic link — pick one, magic link is more modern, 2-3 days
Phone + SMS — Twilio integration, ongoing SMS costs (~$0.01 per OTP), 3-5 days
Total: roughly 2 weeks of auth work. Worth it for conversion. Just budget for it.

One thing to think about: auth providers tie identity. A customer who signs up with Google one day and email another day creates two accounts unless you implement account linking. Build email-as-canonical-identifier from day one and link providers to it. Otherwise you'll have duplicate-customer cleanup hell in six months.

Hybrid cleaner onboarding is the right call. Manual review for the first 50-100 cleaners is a feature, not a constraint. You personally meeting (video call, even brief) early cleaners means:

You catch fraud and bad fits before they're on the platform
You build personal rapport with founding supply
You learn what cleaners actually care about (informs the product)
Each cleaner becomes a referral source for other cleaners
This is exactly what successful service marketplaces do. DoorDash, Instacart, even Uber in the early days — founder-led supply onboarding.

Plan for the transition to automation. At what point does manual review break? Realistically, 100-150 cleaners is where it stops being feasible. Build the automation hooks now (background check API, document upload flow, automated approval triggers) so you can flip the switch when manual review becomes a bottleneck.

Cleaner onboarding flow — the specifics
Since this is the most complex of the three, let me lay out the actual steps.

Phase 1: Application (self-serve)
A multi-step form on /become-a-cleaner or /cleaners:

Welcome screen. Quick honest summary: what cleaners earn, how the platform works, what's required to apply.
Basic info. Name, email, phone, address, languages spoken.
Service area. Pin home base on map, set willing-travel radius.
Availability. General weekly availability (refined later in cleaner app).
Experience. Years cleaning, services offered (standard, deep, move-out, Airbnb), specialties.
Why PureTask. Short text field — why are you applying? This is informal screening.
Identity verification. Government ID upload. Selfie for ID match. Use Stripe Identity or Persona — both are sub-$2 per verification.
Background check consent. Authorization for Checkr or similar background check. Cost: $25-50 per cleaner.
Tax info. SSN or EIN for 1099 filing. Stored encrypted, used only for tax forms at year-end.
Payout setup. Bank account or debit card via Stripe Connect.
Insurance upload (optional). Personal liability insurance certificate. Optional but earns "Insurance Verified" badge.
Submit application.
Total time for the cleaner: roughly 20-30 minutes. Save-and-continue allowed at any step.

Phase 2: Manual review (you)
After submission, application lands in your admin dashboard. You review:

ID verification result (auto-flagged if mismatch)
Background check result (auto-flagged for any criminal history needing context)
Application content (the "why" answer, completeness, gut check)
Service area conflicts (oversupplied ZIP? Undersupplied?)
Brief video interview (15 min, optional but recommended for first 50)
You approve, reject, or request more info. Average decision time: 2-3 days from submission.

Phase 3: Cleaner onboarding inside the app (post-approval)
Once approved, cleaner gets a magic link to set up their cleaner-side account:

Welcome. Score starts at 90, tier is Rising Pro for first 6 jobs.
Profile setup. Photo, bio, "about me" — what makes them different.
Rate setting. Pick rate within Rising Pro range ($25-35/hr to start).
Availability calendar. Set bookable time blocks for next 4 weeks.
Photo training. 30-second screen on photo etiquette and required rooms by service type.
Platform tour. 4-5 screens walking through the cleaner app (dashboard, job inbox, active job, earnings, score).
First-job readiness check. Quick self-affirmation: "I have my supplies, I've reviewed photo guidance, I'm ready to take bookings."
Go live. Profile becomes visible in cleaner spotlight.
Total time: 15-20 minutes. Cleaner can start accepting bookings immediately after.

What to plan for in the admin tooling
Manual review needs decent admin UI:

Application queue with filterable status (pending, in-review, approved, rejected, needs-info)
One-click rejection with reason templates
One-click approval with auto-trigger of cleaner welcome flow
Search and filter by ZIP, application date, source
Notes field for each cleaner (why approved, concerns, follow-ups)
This is its own product surface. Plan to spend 1-2 weeks building it. Don't try to manage applications from email — you'll lose track and miss good cleaners.

Set 1 — Cleaner-side core flow (4 screens)
The screens cleaners use daily: dashboard (home base), job inbox, active job (the differentiator screen), and earnings & payouts.

These are the four screens I want to nail. Cleaner trust in the platform lives or dies in these four screens. Customer-facing screens come second because the cleaner-side defines what the customer-side has to deliver.

Starting with the cleaner dashboard — the densest screen in the entire product.

Wireframe 1: Cleaner mobile dashboard

Quick notes on key decisions in this dashboard:

Score and tier are the top of the screen. Cleaners care most about these. Both tappable for detail.

The "Next cleaning" card has the "On my way" button right there. This is the most-tapped action in the app — surfaced at the top of the screen, available the moment the cleaner opens the app within 2 hours of a job.

Earnings show net (after commission), not gross. $386 is what they're getting paid. Friday auto-deposit messaging removes anxiety.

"Your visibility" widget is the trust-builder. Cleaners see their search rank, profile views, conversion rate. No black-box algorithm. This is the screen that prevents the "shadow ban" anxiety endemic to gig platforms.

Badges row at the bottom — earned reputation, with the ZIP-locked badge ("Top-rated in 94110") visible. Tap to see all badges and how to earn more.

Bottom nav has 4 tabs: Home, Jobs (inbox), Earnings, Profile. Standard mobile pattern.

Wireframe 2: Cleaner job inbox

Quick notes on key decisions in the job inbox:

New requests at top, with countdown timer. The 1-hour decline window is visible on each card. Cleaners know how long they have to respond before declining counts against their score.

Customer rating band shown, not exact number. "Good standing" instead of a numeric score. Same logic we locked: bands across the table, numbers only for own score.

Booking notes (pet info, cleaner preferences) shown inline. Cleaners need this info to decide whether to accept. Buried-in-detail-view = friction.

Earnings shown net (after platform commission). $112 = what they take home, not gross subtotal.

Recurring customer status surfaced. A 4th booking from Marcus T. is more valuable than a first-time booking — cleaners know which bookings are building lifetime relationships.

Past tab exists but is third priority. Most action is on Upcoming and New Requests.

Wireframe 3: Active job screen (the differentiator)
This is the most important screen in the entire product. Cleaner taps "On my way" from dashboard or job inbox, and lands here. State changes through the lifecycle.

V

Connecting to visualize...
Quick notes on key decisions in the active job screen:

Top status bar always visible. Active timer, clock-in time, progress through booked duration. Cleaner can glance and know where they stand.

Photo grid is the centerpiece. Each required room shown as a card with Before / After slot. Done rooms have solid borders; pending rooms have dashed borders. Visual progress is immediate.

"Add another room" lets cleaner exceed minimum. Extra documentation protects them in disputes — it's framed as a positive action.

Mid-job actions are bundled in a separate card. Quote additional work (turns scope creep into revenue), Flag issue (documents problems), Message customer, Report emergency (red, separate visual treatment).

Clock out is disabled until all required photos uploaded. Hard gate. The grayed-out button + the message above it tells cleaners exactly what's stopping them. Active button only appears when everything is complete.

Timer never pauses. Locked from earlier conversation. Cleaner is paid for time on site, not for time "actively cleaning" by some platform metric.

Wireframe 4: Earnings & payouts

Quick notes on the earnings screen:

Available balance is the hero. Big, prominent, "$386.00." Cleaner sees what's theirs at a glance.

Get paid now vs. wait for Friday is a live decision. The $386 → $366.70 math is shown so cleaners understand exactly what the 5% costs them. No surprise fees.

Weekly breakdown shows the math. Gross subtotals → minus commission → net earnings. Full transparency.

Recent jobs show net per job, with star rating + tip. Cleaners care which jobs went well — connecting good ratings with good income reinforces what behaviors are valuable.

Tax disclosure with the 25% rule. Year-to-date totals + a reminder to set aside taxes. Tiny touch that protects cleaners from the classic gig economy tax shock in April.

No GPS map shown. Earlier decision: GPS pings happen during transit, not during cleaning. The cleaner is in the customer's home — tracking them room-by-room would be invasive. Status timeline is the right level of visibility.

Wireframe 12: Approve & pay
Cleaner has clocked out. Customer gets a push notification and lands here.

V

Connecting to visualize...
Notes on approve & pay:

Time card surfaces clock-in/out and exact duration worked. Customer paid for 2 hrs; Maria worked 2 hrs 2 min. The 2 minutes are visible — small honesty signal.

Photo evidence is room-by-room, before/after pairs. This is the dispute-prevention design. Customer scrolls, sees their actual rooms, sees the work documented. Tap any pair to expand to full-screen swipe.

Two CTAs, same weight visually. Approve is the dark filled button (the assumed action). Dispute is the outlined button (real, not buried). Customer makes a real choice — you're not pushing them into approval.

Auto-approval timer surfaced. "23h 47m" is concrete. Customer knows what happens if they ignore the screen.

48-hour dispute window mentioned in footer. Reminds them they have time even after they approve.

Wireframe 13: Customer dashboard (final screen)
Returning customer's home base.



Notes on the customer dashboard:

Next cleaning is the hero, not Reschedule. The card surfaces upcoming booking with three action chips (Reschedule / Message / Cancel) but the action focus is "this is what's coming." Reschedule is reachable but not the headline.

Book again row. Three previous cleaners, one-tap rebook. This is the lifetime value lever. Customer doesn't need to browse — they have their team.

Recurring suggestion card. "You've booked Maria 3 times. Set up recurring." Smart prompt at the right moment. Doesn't bother first-time customers; surfaces when the relationship is established.

Recent cleanings shows the customer's own ratings. "You rated 5 stars" — anchors customer to past satisfaction, makes rebooking feel right.

4-tab bottom nav: Home, Browse, Inbox, Profile. Mirrors cleaner-side structure. Browse is for finding new cleaners; Inbox is for messaging.

Honest framing before we start
Build planning is where most solo founders fail. Not because the plan is wrong, but because they over-scope MVP, run out of money or willpower halfway through, and ship nothing. I'm going to be aggressive about cutting scope. You'll probably push back on some cuts — that's fine, that's the conversation we need to have.

The goal of this session: leave with a buildable 12-16 week plan that gets PureTask to a real public launch with real cleaners doing real cleanings for real customers.

The fundamental MVP question
What does "launch" mean for PureTask? Three possible definitions:

Definition 1 — Friends and family beta. 5 cleaners, 20 customers, all hand-curated. You're testing the product mechanically. Public marketing not yet started. Build target: ~8 weeks.

Definition 2 — Soft public launch. 15-30 cleaners, open signups for customers, focused on 2-3 ZIP codes (Mission, Bernal, maybe Sacramento downtown). Real money flowing. Build target: ~14 weeks.

Definition 3 — Full NorCal launch. 50+ cleaners across SF to Reno corridor, all 12 city/service SEO pages active, Google Ads spend, aggressive customer acquisition. Build target: ~24 weeks + marketing budget.

These are not just timelines — they're scope decisions. A friends-and-family beta doesn't need recurring bookings, ZIP badges, or the customer dashboard's full feature set. A soft public launch needs all of that. A full NorCal launch needs additional supply tooling and admin dashboards.

My strong recommendation: Definition 2 — Soft public launch.

Reasoning:

Definition 1 is too small to validate the marketplace. 5 cleaners isn't enough supply for any customer to find someone they like. You'll get false signal.
Definition 3 is too ambitious for solo. You'll burn out before launch.
Definition 2 is a real public product with real GMV but contained scope. If it works, scale up. If it doesn't, you've learned at the cheapest possible cost.
I'm proceeding with Definition 2 unless you push back.

Every feature we designed falls into one of three buckets:

Must have for launch. If this isn't built, the product doesn't function or the trust story collapses.

Should have soon. Important but can ship in the first 60 days post-launch without breaking the product.

Phase 2. Designed, valuable, but defer until we have signal that the core works.

Where I'm cutting things you might want to keep
A few of the cuts above are emotionally hard. Let me defend them:

Cutting recurring bookings from MVP. Painful — recurring is your lifetime value lever. But it's also a complex feature: scheduled job creation, customer pause/skip flow, cleaner availability conflict resolution, recurring booking modification, recurring cancel rules. Three weeks of build minimum. Defer to Week 15+ when you have real customer-cleaner pairs requesting it. Until then, customers can manually rebook (2-tap from dashboard).

Cutting ZIP-locked badges from MVP. The qualification logic (10 jobs in a ZIP at 4.5+) requires that you have job history at all. At launch, no cleaner has 10 jobs. Badge calculation runs over historical data — pointless until that history exists. Defer to Week 15+.

Cutting Match Score algorithm from MVP. The weighted sort (distance + tier + availability + recency + affinity + spotlight) is sophisticated. At launch, you'll have ~30 cleaners. Customers can browse the whole list. Sort by simple criteria (distance, rating, price) until volume justifies the algorithm. Defer to Week 20+.

Cutting customer reliability score from MVP. Cleaners rating customers post-job is valuable but adds a screen for cleaners and analysis logic for customers. Customers without scores can still book. Defer.

Cutting Reno from launch. Northern California from SF to Reno was the brand. But Reno is 200+ miles from your other cities. Different metro, different cleaner pool, different SEO. Splitting your supply and customer acquisition across two regions weakens both. Launch in SF metro first, prove it, then expand to Sacramento, then Reno. Defer to Phase 2.

Cutting tipping from MVP. Tipping infrastructure (calculation, payout, separate from job total, tax implications) is genuinely complex. Customers can tip in cash directly to cleaners until Phase 2 ships in-app tipping. Yes, this is a real cut — most marketplaces have tipping at launch. I'm cutting it because it's not load-bearing for the trust story.

If any of those cuts feel wrong to you, push back now. Adding things back to MVP costs weeks; we should fight about it before code starts.

Critical path risks
Three things that can kill this timeline:

Risk 1: Stripe Connect onboarding for cleaners. This is the most underestimated complexity in marketplace builds. Stripe requires identity verification, tax info, address verification, sometimes phone verification. Cleaner drop-off at this step is 30-40% in most marketplaces. Build it early, test relentlessly.

Risk 2: Photo upload at scale. Mobile photo upload with poor connectivity (cleaning in a basement) breaks. You'll need: progressive upload, retry on connection drop, offline queue, compression, server-side processing. Allocate Week 9 fully to this — it's not a 2-day task.

Risk 3: Push notifications cross-platform. iOS web push only works on iOS 16.4+. Email fallback is essential. SMS is even more reliable but costs $0.01/message. Plan the notification system as a pluggable layer that can use any of the three.

What's fixed and what's flexible
Fixed (don't compromise):

Identity verification before cleaner approval
Background check before cleaner approval
Photo proof before clock-out
Escrow until customer approves (or auto-approves)
Reliability scoring (basic version)
Flexible (can ship simpler):

Search algorithm (start with: distance + rating + price filters)
Tier benefits (start with: rate range only)
Badges (start with: zero badges, add later)
Onboarding polish (start with: ugly but functional, polish later)
Recurring bookings (deferred entirely)
Things you need to do that aren't code
These run parallel to development:

Cleaner recruitment (Direction D, separate session). Need to start by Week 6 to have supply by Week 14.
Legal documents (Direction C, separate session). Need lawyer engagement by Week 4 to have docs ready by Week 12.
Branding (logo, colors, photography). Hire a designer or DIY by Week 8 or you launch with placeholder visuals.
Test data and dummy bookings. You'll need realistic-looking data for screenshots, testing, and demos. Allocate weekend in Week 5.
Customer support setup. Email at minimum, ideally Intercom or similar. Set up by Week 12.
Stripe Connect application approval. Stripe approves marketplace accounts manually, can take 2-4 weeks. Apply Week 1.

I have to be honest with you before we move forward, because three of those answers worry me and one I want to celebrate.

Where I'm pushing back hard
You picked the most ambitious version of every choice. Full NorCal launch, full geographic corridor, recurring bookings in MVP, no time constraint. I want to be straight: this is the path that kills most marketplace founders.

Not because the plan is wrong. Because the plan is too big for the team building it.

You are one person. You are the designer, the developer, the founder, the cleaner recruiter, the customer support team, the bug fixer, the legal liaison, the marketing department. Every additional scope decision multiplies across all of those roles.

Let me show you concretely what each "go big" choice costs, and then you decide for real with full information.

What "full NorCal launch" actually demands
Definition 3 means: 50+ cleaners across SF, Oakland, Berkeley, San Jose, Sacramento, Davis, Roseville, Folsom, Reno, Sparks. Customer acquisition spend in all of those metros simultaneously. SEO landing pages for every city × service combo (12+ pages). Operational support across two metros 200 miles apart. Localized cleaner recruitment in 8+ neighborhoods.

To pull this off solo:

Cleaner recruitment alone is 200-300 hours of work. Cold outreach, interviews, onboarding, getting them through Stripe Connect verification, hand-holding the first jobs. 50 cleaners × 4-6 hours per cleaner = 200-300 hours. That's two full months of doing nothing else.
Customer acquisition across multi-metro requires a marketing budget. Realistic: $15-30k in the first 90 days for ads alone, on top of your build costs.
Two-metro support means you're answering tickets at 8 AM Reno and 8 PM SF. Single-founder support with 50+ cleaners and 200+ customers = nights and weekends, no weekends off, for months.
What "recurring bookings in MVP" actually adds
Recurring is genuinely 3 extra weeks minimum. But it's not the 3 weeks that worries me — it's the second-order complexity:

Scheduled job creation (cron jobs running weekly to spawn the next booking)
Customer pause/skip flow (and the UI that shows what's coming)
Cleaner availability conflict resolution when recurring bookings collide with one-off requests
Recurring booking modification (change time, skip a week, change cleaner)
Recurring cancel rules (can't just cancel a single occurrence; might affect the whole series)
Different cancel/reschedule policies for recurring vs. one-off
Edge cases: cleaner suspended mid-recurring-series, customer payment failure on a future occurrence
This is genuinely 3-4 weeks of careful work if it's solid. Solid is required because recurring bookings are the lifetime-value lever — they have to feel reliable or customers churn off them and never come back.

What "no timeline" actually means
This is the one I want to push back on most. "I'll work on it until it's complete" sounds disciplined. In practice, it's the most dangerous answer because there's no forcing function for cuts.

The thing about deadlines is they make you choose. When you have unlimited time, every "should I add this feature?" gets a yes. Six months in, you have a 60-feature product, none of them polished, and you still haven't launched.

A real launch date is a tool. It tells you what to cut.

The honest problem
I'd be doing you harm by spending another long session generating 50+ wireframes. Here's why:

Generating 60 wireframes takes the visualizer past its reliable limit. We hit timeouts at 13 screens. 60 in one session would degrade quality fast and probably fail partway through.

Most of these screens follow patterns we've already established. A reschedule flow uses the same multi-step pattern as the booking flow. A dispute thread uses the same messaging pattern as the in-app messaging widget. A settings page is a list of toggles and inputs you've seen on every app. Generating them would mostly be confirming "yes, the design language extends to this screen too" rather than discovering anything new.

You don't need wireframes for admin tools. Those screens are ugly-by-design — function over form. The cleaner application queue is a table with a few action buttons. You design those as you build them.

Most founders ship with placeholder versions of these screens. A real product launch ships with quick, scrappy versions of settings pages, error states, and FAQ pages. They get polished post-launch based on what actually breaks. Building 60 wireframes upfront for screens that will change after first contact with users is wasted work.

The work you actually need now is the database schema, not more pixels. Without a data model, none of these screens can be built. With a data model, the screens become straightforward implementations of patterns we've already established.

What I'd recommend instead
Three options. None of them are "build all 60 wireframes."

Option 1 — Skip wireframing, move to database schema
Trust that the design language is established, the patterns are clear, and the remaining screens will follow the patterns we've already locked. Move directly to Direction B (database schema) so you can actually start building.

When you hit a screen during development that genuinely needs design (not just pattern application), come back and we wireframe it specifically. This is how most founders build — design what's unclear, implement what's obvious.

Best for: Moving fast toward shipping.

Option 2 — Wireframe only the high-stakes edge cases
Some of the remaining screens have real design questions that pattern-matching won't solve:

Reschedule flow with penalty disclosure (how do you communicate "you'll be charged 50%" without scaring the customer away?)
Cancel flow (similar challenge)
Dispute filing screen (customer is frustrated; UI tone matters)
Recurring booking setup (genuinely new flow we haven't designed)
Tip prompt (the post-review-tip pattern)
Review prompt (the friction-free 5-star + optional written)
Cleaner profile editor (multi-tab, complex)
That's roughly 7-10 screens with real design decisions. Skip the rest.

Best for: Filling in the legitimate gaps without wasting time on placeholder screens.

Option 3 — Build a design system document instead
Rather than 60 more wireframes, write a design system document that codifies the patterns we've established:

Header pattern (left arrow, title, action right)
Card pattern (border, padding, header row, content)
Bottom nav (4 tabs, icon + label)
Button hierarchy (filled primary, outlined secondary, text tertiary)
Tier badge color logic
Score widget pattern
Photo grid pattern
List vs. card list patterns
Form field treatments
Empty state pattern
Loading state pattern
Error state pattern
Notification pattern
You build remaining screens by composing these patterns. Faster than wireframing each screen individually, and gives you a reference document you'll use for years.

Best for: Long-term build efficiency.

My strong recommendation
Option 2 + Option 3, in that order.

First, wireframe the 7-10 genuine edge cases (1-2 sessions of work). Then write a design system document that codifies the patterns. That gives you everything you need to build the remaining 50 screens without coming back to me for each one.

Combined cost: roughly 2 more sessions. Reasonable.

Building all 60 wireframes is the perfectionist's path. It feels productive but mostly produces redundant work. The screens follow patterns; the patterns are established; you don't need pixel-perfect mocks of every single screen to build them.

What I want you to decide
Push back if you have reasons for wanting full coverage I'm missing. There are legitimate ones — if you're hiring a designer to refine these, comprehensive wireframes give them a complete spec to work from. If you're working with an investor who wants to see thoroughness. If your build process needs every screen reviewed before code.

But if your reason is "feels safer to have everything spec'd," I'd argue against that. Patterns are more useful than enumerated screens. Pick a path.

Wireframe 14: Reschedule flow
The customer is on their dashboard, taps "Reschedule" on their next cleaning. Showing two states: the policy disclosure + new time picker, and the confirmation.

V

Connecting to visualize...
Notes on reschedule:

Policy disclosure surfaces upfront in the left card. "Free until 11 PM tonight; 50% fee after." The customer makes an informed choice rather than discovering a fee at confirmation.

Maria's actual availability is shown for the new date. Customer can only pick times Maria has open. No "request and wait."

The fallback is stated clearly: if cleaner declines, original booking stays. No spite no-show option.

Confirmation screen sets the wait — 4 hours for cleaner response. Concrete, not vague.

Wireframe 15: Cancel flow with penalty disclosure
The harder version of reschedule. Customer wants to cancel; they need to see what it costs.

V

Connecting to visualize...
Notes on the cancel flow:

Penalty disclosed in dollars, not percentages. "$58 of the $116 booking" hits harder than "50%." Customer makes a real decision.

Reschedule offered as escape hatch. "Want to avoid the fee?" Reduces cancellations into reschedules — better for cleaner, better for marketplace.

Reason field is optional. You'll get useful aggregate data on why people cancel without forcing a friction step.

The right card is the policy reference. Customer can see the full schedule. No surprises later.

Wireframe 16: Dispute filing screen (customer)
Customer has approved & paid (or is in the 24-hr review window) and wants to flag an issue.

V

Connecting to visualize...
Notes on dispute filing:

Issue type forces categorization upfront. Quality vs. damage vs. theft vs. other — different types route differently. Customer makes a clean choice.

Photo evidence is "recommended" not required. Soft pressure, with the consequence stated honestly: "Without evidence, disputes rely on Maria's photos and your description." Customer decides if they want their case strong or weak.

"What outcome do you want" channels expectations. Re-clean is the lightest, full refund is the heaviest. Customer says what they actually want, which de-escalates most disputes.

Frivolous claim warning at bottom. Honest disclosure: bad-faith disputes have consequences. Discourages weaponizing the system.

Wireframe 17: Dispute response screen (cleaner)
The cleaner just got a notification that Maria has a dispute. They open it.



Notes on dispute response:

Customer's complaint shown verbatim with their requested outcome. Cleaner sees the actual ask.

Side-by-side photo comparison. Sarah's evidence (3 photos) next to cleaner's clock-out evidence (16 photos with bathroom flagged). Photo-vs-photo conversation made visual.

Three response options mirror what we designed — re-clean, partial refund, stand by work. Each has clear consequences spelled out.

The amber warning bar at top ("Action needed within 24 hours") creates urgency without panic.

Wireframe 18: In-app messaging thread
The messaging widget that appears when customer or cleaner taps "Message" anywhere.



Notes on messaging:

Disclosure banner at top. "Messages are part of your booking record and visible to support if a dispute opens." Sets tone — both parties know it's recorded.

Date dividers separate threads cleanly.

System messages render differently. "On my way" appears as a structured event card, not a free-text message. Status updates inline.

Quick reply chips ("I'll be home", "Side door is unlocked", "Running 10 min late") above the keyboard. Reduces typing friction for the most common responses.

Tap-to-call accessible from header for emergency or fast coordination.

Wireframe 19: Notification center
What customer or cleaner sees when they tap the bell or "Inbox" tab.



Notes on notifications:

Unread items have subtle gray background + dot indicator. Read items lighter. Visual hierarchy without overwhelming.

Three filter tabs keep the inbox manageable. Most users will browse "All" but power users can filter to bookings or messages.

Each notification has actor + verb + object structure. "Maria sent you a message" — clear who did what. Preview text below for context.

Reviewable cleaning has a $5 incentive nudge. Reviews are critical to your trust system; small carrot accelerates them.

Wireframe 20: Review prompt screen
After approval & pay, customer sees this. The friction-free 5-star + optional written review.

V

Connecting to visualize...
Notes on review prompt:

One tap = full review submitted. Customer taps a star, that's it. The "Submit review" button is active. Everything else is optional.

Trait chips replace open-ended writing. Most customers won't write a paragraph. "Thorough", "Friendly", "On time" tags give you structured data and feed into specialty endorsements automatically.

Tip card appears only at 4+ stars (this is a 5-star screen). Below that, this card disappears — we honor the "no tip prompt at low ratings" decision.

$10 is pre-selected as default tip but customer can pick any chip including "No tip." No dark patterns. The "100% goes to her" disclosure builds trust.

Save as favorite is a soft checkbox. Easy yes, easy skip.

Skip button at top. Customer can dismiss the whole thing. Reviews shouldn't feel mandatory.

Wireframe 21: Recurring booking setup
Customer is on a cleaner's profile or post-review and chooses to set up recurring. This is the key flow.

V

Connecting to visualize...
Notes on recurring setup:

Cadence options pre-selected with "most popular" anchor. Every 2 weeks is the default — matches industry data on standard residential cleaning cadence.

Day/time shown as Maria's actual availability. No hypothetical scheduling — customer picks from what's real.

"Charged per cleaning, not upfront." Critical detail for trust. Customer doesn't fear locking up cash.

Wireframe 22: Recurring booking management
Customer's existing recurring schedule with all the controls.



Notes on recurring management:

Active status indicator with green dot. At-a-glance health.

Three upcoming cleanings shown with skip buttons inline. Most-common action one tap away.

Manage schedule actions in priority order: Pause (least disruptive), Change frequency, Change time, End (most disruptive). End recurring is in coral text — visually flagged as the destructive action.

"Existing bookings stay" disclosure for End recurring. Customer doesn't lose what's already scheduled.

Wireframe 23: Tip prompt screen
The standalone tip screen for when customer didn't tip in the review flow but is rebooking later.



Notes on tip prompt:

Three preset chips with percentage equivalents. Customers can think in dollars or percentages. Both shown.

$10 highlighted as default but not pre-selected. The blue accent border is suggestion, not commitment. Customer must actively pick.

"No tip" is equal-weight option. Same visual treatment as Custom amount. No guilt UI.

100% to cleaner disclosure. The trust point we locked.

Optional thank-you note. Customers who want to write something can; most won't, and that's fine.

Wireframe 24: Auto-rebook nudge
24 hours after a positive review, customer gets this push notification → tap → land here.



Notes on rebook nudge:

Two preset cadences with one custom option. Two weeks (popular for standard) and four weeks (lighter). Custom for everything else.

"Make this recurring" upgrade. Customer can convert one-off rebook into recurring with a single checkbox. This is the lifetime-value escalator — gentle, optional, value-aligned.

"Maybe later" softens the dismiss. No "X" close that feels like rejection. Customer leaves with positive sentiment.

Pre-filled context shown. Same address, same service. Customer doesn't have to re-decide anything they already decided.

Wireframe 25: Customer favorites management
Customer's saved cleaners, organized for quick rebook.

V

Connecting to visualize...
Notes on favorites:

"Your Regular" promoted to top with prominent card. A cleaner you've used 5+ times deserves bigger visual real estate.

Other saved cleaners listed compactly with last-booked timestamp. Customer can see freshness of relationship.

Heart icon shown on each card for one-tap unfavorite.

Carlos M. example shows a saved-but-not-yet-booked cleaner. Customer can save anyone they're interested in, even before first booking — surfaces in this list as a future possibility.

Wireframe 26: Cleaner profile editor
What the cleaner sees when they tap "Edit profile" from their dashboard. Multi-section.



Notes on profile editor:

Rate sliders, one per service. Cleaner can charge different rates for different services (deep clean usually higher than standard). Range is bounded by current tier — slider stops at $65 for Top Performer.

Tier upgrade hint. "Reach All-Star to unlock $60-80/hr." Reinforces the tier system as path to higher earnings.

Service area uses same map preview from application. Cleaner can adjust radius without leaving this screen.

Services offered as toggles. Cleaner can turn off Airbnb turnover if they don't want those bookings (different work, different cadence).

Bio character counter. 142 of 300 — guides cleaners to write a bit more without forcing it.

Wireframe 27: Cleaner availability calendar
Where cleaner sets bookable hours.



Notes on availability calendar:

Weekly schedule pattern with per-day toggles. Cleaner sets typical week once; system spawns availability automatically.

Sunday "Off" with toggle off — visually different (lower opacity).

Buffer between bookings. 60 min default. Prevents back-to-back booking that creates lateness risk. This is the protection we discussed earlier.

Booking lead time. 24 hrs default. Cleaner can require 2 hrs (same-day open) or 48 hrs (no last-minute).

Time off tab at top for date-specific overrides — vacation, sick days, holidays. Default tab is weekly schedule because that's what changes least.

Notable design decisions in this batch
Tier drop warning has a real appeal flow. Not just a notification — a 48-hour human-review window with explicit "submit appeal" action. This is the safety valve we designed in the scoring system, made concrete.

Score explainer shows weighted bars per metric. Cleaners see exactly which metric is hurting them most (reschedule frequency at 80% in this example). Reduces the "shadow ban" anxiety endemic to gig platforms.

Emergency support gets explicit visual weight. Red box, urgent typography, 1-hour SLA promise. Cleaners and customers shouldn't have to dig for this when it matters.

Batch 7 — Admin tools (7 screens, all desktop):

54 · Admin dashboard — KPI grid (bookings, GMV, new cleaners, active disputes), 14-day GMV trend sparkline, recent activity feed, "needs your attention" priority queue (escalated dispute, application backlog, payout review), weekly funnel showing visit → booking conversion at 4.1%

57 · Dispute mediation interface — The most important admin screen. Customer claim, photo evidence side-by-side (customer's 3 photos vs cleaner's 16 across 4 rooms), full message thread, three resolution options (cleaner stands / partial refund / full refund) with consequences spelled out, decision rationale field, complete event timeline
58 · Booking lookup — Search bar, tabbed status filters, full booking table with all key fields, color-coded status pills (disputed bookings highlighted), pagination
59 · Cleaner detail view — KPI strip (score, jobs, earnings, rating), 90-day score history bar chart, recent bookings, status & flags panel, manual actions (adjust score, override tier, issue manual payout), internal notes
60 · Customer detail view — Reliability score, booking history with disputed booking flagged, reliability breakdown (the 5 metrics), account details, manual actions including "restrict to request-to-book" for frivolous-claim customers
We're at 60 of ~70 wireframes.

Notable design decisions in this batch
Information density is intentional. Admin tools are operated by you, at a desk, on a real keyboard. Mobile-first design language doesn't apply. Tables, sidebars, dense KPI strips — these are right for the context.

The dispute mediation screen is the most important admin screen. It earns the most space because it's the highest-stakes decision you'll make. The three resolution options sit side-by-side with consequences, the timeline shows every event in the booking, and the rationale field becomes the audit trail.

Manual override actions exist throughout. Adjust score manually, override tier, issue manual payout, send credit. These are the safety valves — every algorithmic system needs human override capability when reality breaks the model.

Internal notes everywhere. Permanent record of admin context for both cleaners and customers. "Won previous dispute on photo evidence. Good documentation habits." This is the institutional memory that survives founder turnover and protects future decisions.

Claude responded: Locked.
Locked. Direction B — database schema and data model.

Quick honest framing before I start building tables:

What this session produces
A complete entity-relationship model for PureTask. Specifically:

All entities (User, Cleaner, Booking, Photo, Tier, Score, Dispute, etc.)
Every field on every entity (name, type, constraints, defaults)
Every relationship (foreign keys, cardinality, cascade rules)
Every index that needs to exist for performance
Every enum/lookup value
Migration strategy (what ships in MVP, what's deferred)
The output should be something you can hand to Postgres directly via migrations, or to a TypeScript ORM (Prisma, Drizzle), or to a backend developer. No ambiguity.

How I'm going to think about this
I'm going to model this in three layers, building outward:

Identity layer — Users, Cleaners, Customers, Admins (who can do things)
Marketplace layer — Bookings, Services, Schedules, Reviews (the transactional core)
Trust layer — Scores, Tiers, Badges, Disputes, Photos (the differentiation)
Each layer depends on the one before. We build identity first because everything else references it.

Two upfront decisions I want to validate before I start
These shape every table that follows. Let me explain each, then I'll ask.

Decision 1: Single User table vs. separate Customer/Cleaner tables
Option A — Single User table with role flag. One users table with a role enum (customer, cleaner, admin). Profile-specific fields go in adjacent tables (cleaner_profiles, customer_profiles).

Pros: A user can be both customer and cleaner. Auth is simple — one login, one identity. Easier admin lookups. Cons: Some role-specific logic at the application layer needs to check roles before accessing fields.

Option B — Separate Customer and Cleaner tables. Different tables, different IDs, no shared identity.

Pros: Cleaner separation of concerns. Each table is purpose-built. Cons: A cleaner can't easily book cleanings as a customer (would need two accounts). Auth becomes more complex.

My recommendation: Option A. Cleaners often want to book their own cleanings (or for family). Single user identity is cleaner long-term. Most marketplaces converge on this pattern (Airbnb, Uber, Etsy all use single user + role).

Decision 2: Embedded vs. separate Reliability Score
Option A — Score lives on the cleaner_profiles table. Single current_score field, updated nightly.

Pros: Fast lookups (no join). Simpler queries. Cons: No history. Can't see how score changed over time.

Option B — Separate score_snapshots table. Daily snapshots stored as rows. Current score is the latest snapshot.

Pros: Full history. Admin can see score graph (we wireframed this in W59). Disputes can reference "what was the score on Nov 5". Cons: More joins. Slightly more storage.

My recommendation: Option B. We wireframed score history graphs and admin score-history bars. We need this data. The storage cost is trivial (one row per cleaner per day).

Two questions before I lock the schema
Answer these two and I'll build out the full schema in the next message — entities, fields, relationships, the whole thing. Once it's locked, you have a database design ready to migrate.

If you'd rather I just pick the recommended options and proceed without the back-and-forth, say "go with your recommendations" and I'll build it that way.

A: B — Separate score_snapshots table for history (recommended)

May 2
Claude responded: Good.
Good. Score is locked — we go with separate snapshots. Let me unpack the user identity decision properly because it's the most consequential schema choice we'll make.

The user identity question, in plain language
When someone signs up for PureTask, do they get one identity that can do everything, or do they get a customer-shaped identity OR a cleaner-shaped identity?

This sounds abstract. Let me make it concrete with three real situations.

Situation 1: Maria the cleaner needs her own house cleaned
Maria is a cleaner on the platform. Her sister visits next week and Maria wants to hire James (another cleaner) for her own apartment so she can focus on entertaining.

Option A (single user with role flag): Maria opens the app, switches into "customer mode," books James. Same login, same email, same payment method.

Option B (separate tables): Maria has to create a second account with a different email or somehow link two accounts. Or she just doesn't book and grumbles about it.

This isn't hypothetical. Cleaners book cleanings. A lot.

Situation 2: Sarah the customer is a cleaner-curious person
Sarah books a few cleanings, loves the platform, decides she wants to clean part-time on the side for extra income. She applies to be a cleaner.

Option A: Sarah's existing account gets a cleaner role added to her existing identity. Her customer history is preserved. She can switch contexts.

Option B: Sarah creates a new cleaner account from scratch. Her customer reputation doesn't carry. She has two logins.

The former is a smoother conversion path. The latter loses you signups.

Situation 3: Nathan (you) needs admin access
You're the founder. You also book cleanings sometimes (testing the product, real personal use). You also need admin powers to mediate disputes.

Option A: Your single account has customer + admin roles. You don't need three logins.

Option B: You have separate customer and admin accounts. Confusing in practice.

The pattern
Every major marketplace converged on Option A because real users don't fit cleanly into one role. Airbnb hosts book trips. Uber drivers take Ubers. Etsy sellers buy from other sellers.

What "single User with role flag" actually means in tables
Here's the structure so it's concrete:

users
├── id (uuid, primary key)
├── email
├── phone
├── full_name
├── auth fields (password hash, OAuth tokens, etc.)
├── created_at
└── (no role-specific fields here)

user_roles
├── user_id (FK → users)
├── role (enum: 'customer', 'cleaner', 'admin')
└── granted_at

customer_profiles
├── user_id (FK → users)
├── (customer-specific fields: saved addresses, payment methods, photo policy preferences)

cleaner_profiles
├── user_id (FK → users)
├── (cleaner-specific fields: bio, services offered, availability, current_tier, score, etc.)
Key insight: A user can have multiple rows in user_roles. Maria has both customer and cleaner rows. She has a cleaner_profile (her work side) AND a customer_profile (when she books for herself). Same users.id ties them together.

When she opens the app, she sees a context switcher: "Cleaning" / "I want to book." Both views read from the same underlying user.

Why I'm pushing for this even though it's slightly more complex
The "complexity" of Option A is concentrated in two places:

Auth/middleware layer must check roles. Standard pattern, maybe 30 lines of code.
You can't assume a user is one role. Forces you to write better queries from day one.
The "simplicity" of Option B costs you:

Cleaners can't book. You lose every cleaner who wanted to use the product themselves.
No conversion path from customer → cleaner. A meaningful percentage of your future cleaners will be customers first.
Two databases of users to maintain. Admin tools have to query both.
Locked into a decision that's painful to reverse. Migrating from separate tables to a unified user model later is a multi-week refactor.
The tradeoff in one sentence
Option A is harder by roughly 5% on day one, easier by 30% over the lifetime of the product.

My recommendation, more concretely
Go with Option A. Here's how I'd model it for PureTask specifically:

users          ← shared identity
user_roles     ← who can do what
customer_profiles  ← customer-specific
cleaner_profiles   ← cleaner-specific
A user has 1 row in users, 1+ rows in user_roles, and 0-1 rows in each profile table. Most early users have just customer role and a customer_profile. Cleaners get added when they apply and approve.

Pick one and we move
Once you answer, I'll build the full schema in the next message — every table, every field, every relationship. We're talking ~25-30 tables for the full vision (smaller subset for MVP).

If "I have a follow-up question first," ask anything. Schema decisions are easier to change before code than after.
