Claude responded: Locked.
Locked. Single User with role flag for identity, separate score_snapshots for history. Building the full schema now.

This is going to be long because it's the actual deliverable — a schema you can hand to a database. I'm structuring it so it's both readable and copy-paste-able into Postgres migrations.

PureTask · Database Schema (v1)
How to read this document
For each table I'll show:

Purpose — one-sentence description
Fields — name, type, constraints, notes
Relationships — what links to what
Indexes — what queries we expect to be fast
MVP vs. Phase 2 — what ships at launch vs. defers
I'm using PostgreSQL syntax. The conventions:

uuid for IDs (better than autoincrementing integers for distributed systems and security)
timestamptz for all timestamps (timezone-aware)
text over varchar (Postgres treats them identically; text is simpler)
numeric(10,2) for money (never use floats for money)
snake_case for everything
Layer 1: Identity
Table: users
Purpose: Single source of truth for any human in the system — customer, cleaner, or admin.

sql
CREATE TABLE users (
  id              uuid PRIMARY KEY DEFAULT gen_random_uuid(),
  email           text NOT NULL UNIQUE,
  phone           text UNIQUE,
  full_name       text NOT NULL,
  display_name    text,
  
  -- Auth (managed by Clerk/Supabase, but we mirror key fields)
  email_verified_at  timestamptz,
  phone_verified_at  timestamptz,
  auth_provider      text,        -- 'email', 'google', 'apple', 'phone'
  external_auth_id   text UNIQUE, -- ID from auth provider
  
  -- Account state
  status          text NOT NULL DEFAULT 'active',  -- 'active', 'suspended', 'deleted'
  suspended_at    timestamptz,
  suspension_reason text,
  
  -- Metadata
  created_at      timestamptz NOT NULL DEFAULT now(),
  updated_at      timestamptz NOT NULL DEFAULT now(),
  last_seen_at    timestamptz,
  deleted_at      timestamptz   -- soft delete for CCPA
);

CREATE INDEX idx_users_email ON users(email);
CREATE INDEX idx_users_phone ON users(phone) WHERE phone IS NOT NULL;
CREATE INDEX idx_users_status ON users(status);
Notes: display_name is what appears publicly (Maria L. style). full_name is legal name for tax/legal docs. We mirror auth provider identity so admin tools work even if Clerk is down.

Table: user_roles
Purpose: Who can do what. A user can have multiple roles.

sql
CREATE TABLE user_roles (
  user_id     uuid NOT NULL REFERENCES users(id) ON DELETE CASCADE,
  role        text NOT NULL,  -- 'customer', 'cleaner', 'admin'
  granted_at  timestamptz NOT NULL DEFAULT now(),
  granted_by  uuid REFERENCES users(id),  -- which admin granted this (for admin role)
  revoked_at  timestamptz,
  
  PRIMARY KEY (user_id, role)
);

CREATE INDEX idx_user_roles_active ON user_roles(user_id) WHERE revoked_at IS NULL;
Notes: Composite primary key prevents duplicate role grants. We track who granted admin role for audit trail.

Table: customer_profiles
Purpose: Customer-specific data and preferences.

sql
CREATE TABLE customer_profiles (
  user_id              uuid PRIMARY KEY REFERENCES users(id) ON DELETE CASCADE,
  
  -- Reliability
  reliability_score    integer NOT NULL DEFAULT 90,  -- 0-100, customer-side
  reliability_band     text NOT NULL DEFAULT 'good_standing',
  
  -- Preferences
  default_photo_policy text NOT NULL DEFAULT 'standard',  -- 'standard', 'limited', 'none'
  default_skip_rooms   text[] DEFAULT '{}',
  default_booking_notes text,
  
  -- Stats (denormalized for performance)
  total_bookings       integer NOT NULL DEFAULT 0,
  lifetime_spend_cents bigint NOT NULL DEFAULT 0,
  member_since         timestamptz NOT NULL DEFAULT now(),
  
  -- Restrictions
  restricted_to_request_to_book boolean NOT NULL DEFAULT false,
  
  created_at           timestamptz NOT NULL DEFAULT now(),
  updated_at           timestamptz NOT NULL DEFAULT now()
);
Notes: reliability_band derived from score — keeps query speed fast without computing on read. lifetime_spend_cents stored as bigint to avoid floating-point rounding errors on totals.

Table: cleaner_profiles
Purpose: Cleaner-specific data — the working side of a cleaner's identity.

sql
CREATE TABLE cleaner_profiles (
  user_id              uuid PRIMARY KEY REFERENCES users(id) ON DELETE CASCADE,
  
  -- Public profile
  bio                  text,
  profile_photo_url    text,
  languages_spoken     text[] DEFAULT '{}',  -- ['English', 'Spanish']
  years_experience     integer,
  
  -- Tier
  current_tier         text NOT NULL DEFAULT 'rising_pro',
  -- 'rising_pro', 'proven_specialist', 'top_performer', 'all_star_expert'
  tier_changed_at      timestamptz NOT NULL DEFAULT now(),
  
  -- Score (current; history in score_snapshots)
  current_score        integer NOT NULL DEFAULT 90,
  score_calculated_at  timestamptz,
  
  -- Onboarding state
  application_status   text NOT NULL DEFAULT 'pending',
  -- 'pending', 'in_review', 'approved', 'rejected'
  approved_at          timestamptz,
  approved_by          uuid REFERENCES users(id),
  rejection_reason     text,
  
  -- Verification
  background_check_status   text NOT NULL DEFAULT 'pending',
  -- 'pending', 'in_progress', 'cleared', 'flagged', 'rejected'
  background_check_provider text,  -- 'checkr'
  background_check_id       text,  -- external ID
  background_check_completed_at timestamptz,
  background_check_renews_at    timestamptz,
  
  identity_verified         boolean NOT NULL DEFAULT false,
  identity_verified_at      timestamptz,
  identity_provider         text,  -- 'stripe_identity'
  
  -- Insurance
  insurance_status          text NOT NULL DEFAULT 'none',
  -- 'none', 'pending_review', 'verified', 'expired'
  insurance_provider        text,
  insurance_coverage_cents  bigint,
  insurance_expires_at      timestamptz,
  insurance_certificate_url text,
  
  -- Service area
  home_base_address    text,
  home_base_lat        numeric(10, 7),
  home_base_lng        numeric(10, 7),
  service_radius_miles integer NOT NULL DEFAULT 10,
  zip_codes_served     text[] DEFAULT '{}',  -- denormalized for fast queries
  
  -- Settings
  buffer_minutes       integer NOT NULL DEFAULT 60,
  booking_lead_hours   integer NOT NULL DEFAULT 24,
  
  -- Equipment & supplies
  brings_own_supplies  boolean NOT NULL DEFAULT true,
  brings_vacuum        boolean NOT NULL DEFAULT false,
  eco_friendly_available boolean NOT NULL DEFAULT false,
  
  -- Stats (denormalized)
  total_jobs_completed integer NOT NULL DEFAULT 0,
  total_jobs_in_zip    jsonb DEFAULT '{}',  -- {"94110": 247, "94114": 38}
  lifetime_earnings_cents bigint NOT NULL DEFAULT 0,
  average_rating       numeric(3, 2),
  total_reviews        integer NOT NULL DEFAULT 0,
  
  -- Spotlight
  spotlight_until      timestamptz,  -- new cleaner spotlight expiry
  
  -- Stripe Connect
  stripe_connect_account_id  text UNIQUE,
  stripe_connect_status      text,  -- 'pending', 'active', 'restricted', 'disabled'
  payout_method_verified     boolean NOT NULL DEFAULT false,
  
  created_at           timestamptz NOT NULL DEFAULT now(),
  updated_at           timestamptz NOT NULL DEFAULT now()
);

CREATE INDEX idx_cleaner_profiles_tier ON cleaner_profiles(current_tier);
CREATE INDEX idx_cleaner_profiles_application ON cleaner_profiles(application_status);
CREATE INDEX idx_cleaner_profiles_zip ON cleaner_profiles USING GIN(zip_codes_served);
CREATE INDEX idx_cleaner_profiles_spotlight ON cleaner_profiles(spotlight_until) WHERE spotlight_until > now();
Notes: This is the densest table. Several denormalized fields (zip_codes_served, total_jobs_in_zip, average_rating) trade write complexity for read speed. The GIN index on zip_codes_served makes "show me cleaners serving 94110" queries instant.

Layer 2: Marketplace
Table: addresses
Purpose: Saved addresses for customers. Reused across bookings.

sql
CREATE TABLE addresses (
  id              uuid PRIMARY KEY DEFAULT gen_random_uuid(),
  user_id         uuid NOT NULL REFERENCES users(id) ON DELETE CASCADE,
  label           text,  -- 'Home', 'Mom's place'
  street_line_1   text NOT NULL,
  street_line_2   text,
  city            text NOT NULL,
  state           text NOT NULL,
  zip_code        text NOT NULL,
  country         text NOT NULL DEFAULT 'US',
  lat             numeric(10, 7),
  lng             numeric(10, 7),
  is_default      boolean NOT NULL DEFAULT false,
  created_at      timestamptz NOT NULL DEFAULT now(),
  archived_at     timestamptz
);

CREATE INDEX idx_addresses_user ON addresses(user_id) WHERE archived_at IS NULL;
Table: services
Purpose: Available service types. Lookup table.

sql
CREATE TABLE services (
  id              text PRIMARY KEY,  -- 'standard', 'deep_clean', 'move_out', 'airbnb_turnover'
  display_name    text NOT NULL,
  description     text,
  default_duration_hours integer NOT NULL,
  min_duration_hours    integer NOT NULL,
  required_room_types   text[] NOT NULL,  -- ['kitchen', 'bathroom', 'living_room']
  is_active       boolean NOT NULL DEFAULT true,
  sort_order      integer NOT NULL DEFAULT 0
);
Notes: Pre-populated. Not user-editable. New service types added via migration.

Table: cleaner_services
Purpose: Which services each cleaner offers, at what rate.

sql
CREATE TABLE cleaner_services (
  cleaner_id      uuid NOT NULL REFERENCES users(id) ON DELETE CASCADE,
  service_id      text NOT NULL REFERENCES services(id),
  hourly_rate_cents integer NOT NULL,
  is_active       boolean NOT NULL DEFAULT true,
  PRIMARY KEY (cleaner_id, service_id)
);

CREATE INDEX idx_cleaner_services_active ON cleaner_services(service_id) WHERE is_active = true;
Notes: Cleaner can charge different rates per service. Hourly rate validated against tier range at the application layer (not DB constraint, because tier is mutable).

Table: cleaner_availability
Purpose: Weekly recurring availability pattern.

sql
CREATE TABLE cleaner_availability (
  id              uuid PRIMARY KEY DEFAULT gen_random_uuid(),
  cleaner_id      uuid NOT NULL REFERENCES users(id) ON DELETE CASCADE,
  day_of_week     integer NOT NULL CHECK (day_of_week BETWEEN 0 AND 6),  -- 0 = Sunday
  start_time      time NOT NULL,  -- e.g. '09:00:00'
  end_time        time NOT NULL,
  is_active       boolean NOT NULL DEFAULT true,
  created_at      timestamptz NOT NULL DEFAULT now()
);

CREATE INDEX idx_availability_cleaner ON cleaner_availability(cleaner_id) WHERE is_active = true;
Table: cleaner_time_off
Purpose: Specific dates the cleaner is unavailable (vacation, sick days).

sql
CREATE TABLE cleaner_time_off (
  id              uuid PRIMARY KEY DEFAULT gen_random_uuid(),
  cleaner_id      uuid NOT NULL REFERENCES users(id) ON DELETE CASCADE,
  starts_at       timestamptz NOT NULL,
  ends_at         timestamptz NOT NULL,
  reason          text,  -- internal note
  created_at      timestamptz NOT NULL DEFAULT now()
);

CREATE INDEX idx_time_off_cleaner_range ON cleaner_time_off(cleaner_id, starts_at, ends_at);
Table: bookings
Purpose: The central transaction. Every cleaning is a booking.

sql
CREATE TABLE bookings (
  id              uuid PRIMARY KEY DEFAULT gen_random_uuid(),
  reference_code  text NOT NULL UNIQUE,  -- 'PT-4921' style, human-readable
  
  -- Parties
  customer_id     uuid NOT NULL REFERENCES users(id),
  cleaner_id      uuid NOT NULL REFERENCES users(id),
  
  -- What
  service_id      text NOT NULL REFERENCES services(id),
  duration_hours  numeric(3, 1) NOT NULL,
  address_id      uuid REFERENCES addresses(id),
  -- Address denormalized in case customer deletes the address record
  address_snapshot jsonb NOT NULL,
  
  -- When
  scheduled_at    timestamptz NOT NULL,
  scheduled_end_at timestamptz NOT NULL,  -- scheduled_at + duration
  
  -- Booking metadata
  customer_notes  text,
  skip_rooms      text[] DEFAULT '{}',
  pets_at_home    text,
  entry_method    text,  -- 'home', 'lockbox', 'doorman', 'side_door'
  entry_instructions text,
  
  -- Recurring
  recurring_series_id uuid REFERENCES recurring_series(id),
  
  -- State machine
  status          text NOT NULL DEFAULT 'confirmed',
  -- 'confirmed', 'on_the_way', 'in_progress', 'awaiting_approval',
  -- 'approved', 'auto_approved', 'disputed', 'resolved', 'cancelled', 'no_show'
  
  -- Lifecycle timestamps
  on_the_way_at   timestamptz,
  clocked_in_at   timestamptz,
  clocked_out_at  timestamptz,
  approved_at     timestamptz,
  auto_approved_at timestamptz,
  cancelled_at    timestamptz,
  cancelled_by    uuid REFERENCES users(id),
  cancellation_reason text,
  cancellation_fee_cents integer DEFAULT 0,
  
  -- GPS verification
  clock_in_lat    numeric(10, 7),
  clock_in_lng    numeric(10, 7),
  clock_in_distance_meters integer,  -- distance from booking address
  
  -- Pricing (snapshotted at booking time)
  hourly_rate_cents     integer NOT NULL,
  subtotal_cents        integer NOT NULL,
  platform_fee_cents    integer NOT NULL,  -- $9.99
  commission_rate       numeric(4, 4) NOT NULL,  -- e.g. 0.1100 for 11%
  commission_cents      integer NOT NULL,
  cleaner_payout_cents  integer NOT NULL,
  total_cents           integer NOT NULL,
  
  -- Tip (added post-review)
  tip_cents             integer DEFAULT 0,
  tipped_at             timestamptz,
  
  -- Stripe
  stripe_payment_intent_id text UNIQUE,
  stripe_payment_status text,  -- 'pending', 'authorized', 'captured', 'refunded', 'failed'
  authorized_at         timestamptz,
  captured_at           timestamptz,
  
  -- Score impact
  score_calculated      boolean NOT NULL DEFAULT false,
  
  created_at      timestamptz NOT NULL DEFAULT now(),
  updated_at      timestamptz NOT NULL DEFAULT now()
);

CREATE INDEX idx_bookings_customer ON bookings(customer_id, scheduled_at DESC);
CREATE INDEX idx_bookings_cleaner ON bookings(cleaner_id, scheduled_at DESC);
CREATE INDEX idx_bookings_status ON bookings(status);
CREATE INDEX idx_bookings_scheduled ON bookings(scheduled_at) WHERE status IN ('confirmed', 'on_the_way', 'in_progress');
CREATE INDEX idx_bookings_recurring ON bookings(recurring_series_id) WHERE recurring_series_id IS NOT NULL;
CREATE INDEX idx_bookings_reference ON bookings(reference_code);
CREATE INDEX idx_bookings_awaiting_approval ON bookings(clocked_out_at) WHERE status = 'awaiting_approval';
Notes: This is the most-queried table in the system. Every index serves a real query: customer dashboard, cleaner inbox, admin lookup, the auto-approval cron job, etc. The address_snapshot JSONB preserves history even if the customer deletes the address. Pricing is snapshotted at booking time so historical bookings show the right numbers even after rate/commission changes.

Table: recurring_series
Purpose: Parent record for a recurring booking schedule.

sql
CREATE TABLE recurring_series (
  id              uuid PRIMARY KEY DEFAULT gen_random_uuid(),
  customer_id     uuid NOT NULL REFERENCES users(id),
  cleaner_id      uuid NOT NULL REFERENCES users(id),
  service_id      text NOT NULL REFERENCES services(id),
  
  -- Schedule pattern
  frequency       text NOT NULL,  -- 'weekly', 'biweekly', 'monthly'
  day_of_week     integer NOT NULL CHECK (day_of_week BETWEEN 0 AND 6),
  start_time      time NOT NULL,
  duration_hours  numeric(3, 1) NOT NULL,
  
  -- Booking template
  address_id      uuid NOT NULL REFERENCES addresses(id),
  hourly_rate_cents integer NOT NULL,
  
  -- State
  status          text NOT NULL DEFAULT 'active',
  -- 'active', 'paused', 'ended_by_customer', 'ended_by_cleaner', 'ended_by_system'
  paused_until    timestamptz,
  ended_at        timestamptz,
  ended_reason    text,
  
  started_at      timestamptz NOT NULL DEFAULT now(),
  next_occurrence_at timestamptz,
  
  created_at      timestamptz NOT NULL DEFAULT now(),
  updated_at      timestamptz NOT NULL DEFAULT now()
);

CREATE INDEX idx_recurring_active ON recurring_series(next_occurrence_at) WHERE status = 'active';
CREATE INDEX idx_recurring_customer ON recurring_series(customer_id) WHERE status = 'active';
CREATE INDEX idx_recurring_cleaner ON recurring_series(cleaner_id) WHERE status = 'active';
Notes: Cron job reads this nightly: "find all series where next_occurrence_at < now() + 14 days and spawn the next booking." Booking spawning is decoupled from this table; bookings reference series via recurring_series_id but each booking is independent and can be modified individually.

Table: reviews
Purpose: Customer reviews of cleanings. One per booking.

sql
CREATE TABLE reviews (
  id              uuid PRIMARY KEY DEFAULT gen_random_uuid(),
  booking_id      uuid NOT NULL UNIQUE REFERENCES bookings(id) ON DELETE CASCADE,
  customer_id     uuid NOT NULL REFERENCES users(id),
  cleaner_id      uuid NOT NULL REFERENCES users(id),
  
  rating          integer NOT NULL CHECK (rating BETWEEN 1 AND 5),
  written_review  text,
  trait_tags      text[] DEFAULT '{}',  -- ['thorough', 'friendly', 'on_time']
  
  -- Visibility
  is_public       boolean NOT NULL DEFAULT true,
  hidden_at       timestamptz,
  hidden_reason   text,
  
  created_at      timestamptz NOT NULL DEFAULT now()
);

CREATE INDEX idx_reviews_cleaner ON reviews(cleaner_id, created_at DESC) WHERE is_public = true;
CREATE INDEX idx_reviews_traits ON reviews USING GIN(trait_tags);
Notes: Trait tags feed specialty endorsements. GIN index makes "find cleaners tagged 'eco_friendly' 15+ times" queries cheap.

Layer 3: Trust
Table: score_snapshots
Purpose: Daily snapshot of cleaner reliability score. Locked decision from earlier.

sql
CREATE TABLE score_snapshots (
  cleaner_id      uuid NOT NULL REFERENCES users(id) ON DELETE CASCADE,
  snapshot_date   date NOT NULL,
  score           integer NOT NULL,
  
  -- Breakdown
  on_time_score          integer NOT NULL,
  completion_score       integer NOT NULL,
  photo_compliance_score integer NOT NULL,
  rating_score           integer NOT NULL,
  communication_score    integer NOT NULL,
  reschedule_score       integer NOT NULL,
  
  -- Context
  jobs_in_window         integer NOT NULL,
  days_since_signup      integer NOT NULL,
  
  -- Tier (for historical lookup)
  tier_at_snapshot       text NOT NULL,

);

CREATE INDEX idx_score_snapshots_recent ON score_snapshots(cleaner_id, snapshot_date DESC);
Notes: Composite primary key prevents duplicate snapshots. One row per cleaner per day, calculated by a nightly job. The breakdown columns let admins see exactly why a score moved.

Table: score_events
Purpose: Individual events that contribute to score changes. Audit trail.

sql
CREATE TABLE score_events (
  id              uuid PRIMARY KEY DEFAULT gen_random_uuid(),
  cleaner_id      uuid NOT NULL REFERENCES users(id) ON DELETE CASCADE,
  booking_id      uuid REFERENCES bookings(id),

  -- 'on_time_arrival', 'late_arrival', 'no_show', 'photos_complete',
  -- 'rating_received', 'reschedule_initiated', 'cancellation', 'communication_delay'

CREATE INDEX idx_score_events_booking ON score_events(booking_id);

Table: tier_changes
Purpose: History of tier transitions. Supports the 14-day buffer logic and appeals.

sql
CREATE TABLE tier_changes (
  id              uuid PRIMARY KEY DEFAULT gen_random_uuid(),
  cleaner_id      uuid NOT NULL REFERENCES users(id) ON DELETE CASCADE,
  
  from_tier       text NOT NULL,
  to_tier         text NOT NULL,
  direction       text NOT NULL,  -- 'promotion', 'demotion'
  
  -- Score context
  score_at_change       integer NOT NULL,
  days_at_band_before_change integer NOT NULL,  -- 14 for normal drops, less for hard triggers
  
  -- Trigger
  triggered_by    text NOT NULL,
  -- 'sustained_score_band', 'hard_trigger_no_shows', 'manual_admin', 'appeal_granted'
  triggered_by_user_id uuid REFERENCES users(id),  -- if manual
  
  -- Appeal window
  appeal_window_ends_at timestamptz,
  
  scheduled_at    timestamptz NOT NULL,
  effective_at    timestamptz NOT NULL,
  reverted_at     timestamptz,  -- if appeal succeeded
  revert_reason   text,
  
  created_at      timestamptz NOT NULL DEFAULT now()
);

CREATE INDEX idx_tier_changes_cleaner ON tier_changes(cleaner_id, created_at DESC);
CREATE INDEX idx_tier_changes_pending ON tier_changes(effective_at) WHERE effective_at > now();
Notes: Pending demotions sit in this table with effective_at in the future. Appeals can revert them before they take effect. Hard triggers (5 no-shows in 60 days) skip the 14-day buffer.

sql
CREATE TABLE cleaner_badges (
  id              uuid PRIMARY KEY DEFAULT gen_random_uuid(),
  cleaner_id      uuid NOT NULL REFERENCES users(id) ON DELETE CASCADE,
  badge_id        text NOT NULL REFERENCES badges(id),

CREATE INDEX idx_cleaner_badges_active ON cleaner_badges(cleaner_id) WHERE revoked_at IS NULL AND (expires_at IS NULL OR expires_at > now());
Notes: Composite unique index allows one cleaner to have multiple ZIP-locked instances of the same badge type (e.g., "Top-rated in 94110" + "Top-rated in 94114") but only one of each non-ZIP badge.

Table: photos
Purpose: Every photo taken during cleanings. Heavy table; needs careful indexing.

sql
CREATE TABLE photos (
  id              uuid PRIMARY KEY DEFAULT gen_random_uuid(),
  booking_id      uuid NOT NULL REFERENCES bookings(id) ON DELETE CASCADE,
  
  -- File

  thumbnail_url   text,
  file_size_bytes integer,
  width           integer,
  height          integer,

  room_type       text NOT NULL,  -- 'kitchen', 'bathroom', 'living_room', 'bedroom', 'other'
  room_label      text,           -- 'main_bathroom', 'kids_bathroom'
  photo_phase     text NOT NULL,  -- 'before', 'during', 'after'

  upload_started_at timestamptz NOT NULL DEFAULT now(),

  delete_reason   text,  -- 'scheduled', 'customer_request', 'dispute_evidence_retained'
  
  created_at      timestamptz NOT NULL DEFAULT now()
);

CREATE INDEX idx_photos_booking ON photos(booking_id, room_type, photo_phase);
CREATE INDEX idx_photos_deletion ON photos(scheduled_deletion_at) WHERE deleted_at IS NULL;
Notes: Cron job runs daily, deletes photos past their scheduled_deletion_at unless flagged for dispute retention. The idx_photos_deletion partial index makes this query trivially cheap even with millions of photos.

Table: disputes
Purpose: Disputes against bookings.

sql
CREATE TABLE disputes (
  id              uuid PRIMARY KEY DEFAULT gen_random_uuid(),
  booking_id      uuid NOT NULL REFERENCES bookings(id),

  cleaner_id      uuid NOT NULL REFERENCES users(id),

  resolution_notes text,

What ships in MVP vs. defers
MVP (build in weeks 1-14)
These tables are required for the basic platform to function:

users, user_roles, customer_profiles, cleaner_profiles, addresses, services, cleaner_services, cleaner_availability, cleaner_time_off, bookings, reviews, score_snapshots, score_events, photos, disputes, dispute_messages, messages, notifications, payouts, payout_line_items, audit_log

That's 21 tables.

Phase 2 (post-launch, weeks 15-24)
Build these as features turn on:

recurring_series (Week 17-19 build per build sequence) tier_changes, tier_appeals (the 14-day buffer logic — only matters once you have tier data) badges, cleaner_badges (only matters once you have cleaner history to earn against) favorites (lifetime-value lever, not launch-critical) admin_notes (nice-to-have for admin tools)

Migration strategy
I'd recommend three migration files for MVP:

Migration 001 — Identity and core profiles

users, user_roles, customer_profiles, cleaner_profiles, addresses
Migration 002 — Marketplace transactional

services (with seed data), cleaner_services, cleaner_availability, cleaner_time_off, bookings, reviews
Migration 003 — Trust and operations

score_snapshots, score_events, photos, disputes, dispute_messages, messages, notifications, payouts, payout_line_items, audit_log
Run them in order. Each migration is reversible (every CREATE TABLE has a corresponding DROP TABLE in the down-migration).

Where I made judgment calls
A few places I want to flag:

Money in cents (integer/bigint), not dollars (numeric). Industry standard. Avoids floating-point bugs entirely. UI converts to dollars on display.

Soft deletes via deleted_at/archived_at on most user-facing tables. Hard deletes only for the truly cascading stuff (CCPA-mandated user deletion). Soft deletes preserve audit trail and let you recover from accidents.

Denormalization on cleaner_profiles. Fields like total_jobs_completed, lifetime_earnings_cents, average_rating, zip_codes_served are pre-computed and updated by triggers or background jobs. The alternative is computing them on every read, which would crush performance.

JSONB sparingly. I used it for address_snapshot, score_events.context, audit_log.before_state/after_state. These are places where the data is genuinely flexible or historical. Most fields are typed columns because typed columns are queryable, indexable, and self-documenting.

Composite primary keys where natural. score_snapshots is keyed on (cleaner, date). user_roles on (user, role). payout_line_items on (payout, booking). This prevents bugs where you accidentally insert duplicates.

What's next
You have a complete schema. From here, three paths:

Option A — Migrate to Direction C (legal documents). Per your locked plan A → B → C → D. Database is done; legal scope is next.

Option B — Drill deeper into the schema. Want me to explain specific tables, write the actual migration SQL, define triggers/stored procedures, or trace specific queries (like "show me how the cleaner search query reads from these tables")?

Option C — Pause. Take a break, sit with the schema, come back when you want to continue.

Pick your path. The schema is locked unless you want to revisit specific decisions.

Where would the drilling actually help
I want to push back gently before diving in. "Drill deeper" can mean a few different things, and the most useful direction depends on what you're going to do with the output. Let me lay out the options honestly.

Option B1: Critical query patterns
Walk through the 8-12 most important queries the application will run, showing exactly which tables they hit, which indexes they use, what joins they perform. Things like:

"Show me cleaners available in 94110 for a 2-hour standard cleaning on Wednesday at 11 AM"
"Calculate this cleaner's reliability score for tonight's batch"
"Show this customer's next 3 upcoming cleanings + last 5 historical"
"Find disputes that need admin attention, sorted by urgency"
"Generate this Friday's payout batch"
Useful when: you (or a developer) needs to validate that the schema can actually answer the questions the app needs to ask. Surfaces hidden problems like "this query needs an index we don't have" before code is written.

Output: SQL query examples + index validation + edge case handling.

Option B2: Triggers and computed fields
We have a lot of denormalized fields (cleaner_profiles.total_jobs_completed, average_rating, zip_codes_served, etc.). These need to stay in sync with the underlying truth. This option specifies exactly how — Postgres triggers, application-layer recalculation, background jobs, or some mix.

Useful when: you're about to build the backend and need to know what runs when. Without this spec, denormalized fields silently drift out of sync over time.

Output: trigger definitions, background job schedule, "when X changes, recompute Y" rules.

Option B3: Deep dive on the trust layer
The score, tier, and badge tables are where most of the marketplace differentiation lives, and where the most subtle bugs will hide. This option walks through:

How the daily score calculation actually works, table by table
How tier transitions trigger (the 14-day buffer mechanic in SQL)
How ZIP-locked badges qualify and revoke
How specialty badges accumulate from review tags
How frivolous claim tracking feeds into customer scoring
Useful when: you want to make sure the system design we locked actually maps cleanly to the database. Some things sound fine in prose and turn out to be a nightmare in SQL.

Output: algorithmic specification of trust mechanics + the cron job timing + edge cases.

Option B4: Actual migration SQL
Take the schema we just designed and produce ready-to-run migration files (Postgres up/down migrations). One file per migration as I outlined.

Useful when: you're about to start coding and need the schema in the database tomorrow.

Output: copy-paste-able SQL files.

Option B5: Specific table you want to understand better
Tell me which table feels fuzzy or wrong, and we work through it specifically. Maybe bookings has too many columns and should be split. Maybe disputes is missing something. Maybe you don't get how score_snapshots and score_events relate.

Useful when: you have a specific concern. No-cost direction.

Honest recommendation
If you're going to write code soon: B4 (migration SQL) is the most directly actionable.

If you're not coding yet but want to understand the system better: B3 (trust layer deep dive) is the most insight-generating because that's where the design choices most affect cleaner and customer behavior. The other tables are pretty standard marketplace patterns; the trust layer is the part that makes PureTask different.

If a developer is going to build this for you: B2 (triggers/computed fields) prevents the most common backend bug (denormalized fields drifting out of sync).

If you want to validate the schema works before committing: B1 (query patterns) stress-tests the design.

Pick your direction
If you want to do all of them, we can — just pick the order. They're roughly independent so any sequence works, but B3 → B2 → B1 → B4 builds context most cleanly (understand the mechanics → spec the syncing → validate via queries → ship as migration).

Good call. A guide before the work gives you context to push back intelligently when we get into the weeds. Let me build it.

What this guide is
Before we dive into B1 through B5, this document explains:

What each piece is in plain English
How it works at a conceptual level (no code yet)
Why it matters for PureTask specifically
What you'll get out of each session
Honest tradeoffs and decisions you'll face
Order of operations and why
Treat this as the table of contents for the next 5 sessions. After you read it, you'll know what to expect, what to push back on, and what questions to ask.

Direction B Drill — Comprehensive Overview
The mental model: from schema to working system
You currently have the schema — 25 tables, columns, relationships. That's the skeleton. To turn it into a system that actually runs PureTask, you need four more layers:

1. SCHEMA            ← what exists (we have this)
2. QUERIES           ← how the app reads/writes (B1)
3. SYNC RULES        ← how denormalized data stays correct (B2)
4. BUSINESS LOGIC    ← how trust/score/tier mechanics work (B3)
5. DEPLOYMENT        ← how it actually gets into a database (B4)
B5 is the catch-all: "any specific table I'm worried about, drill into it."

Each layer assumes the ones below it. You can't write good queries (B1) without understanding sync rules (B2) — your read queries depend on denormalized fields being correct. You can't write sync rules without understanding the business logic they're enforcing (B3). And none of it matters without migrations (B4) because the database doesn't exist yet.

That's why I recommended B3 → B2 → B1 → B4 as the most context-building order. We'll do that order.

B3: Trust layer deep dive (mechanics)
What it is, in plain English
The "trust layer" is the part of PureTask that's not just a calendar app for cleaners. It's the score system, the tier system, the badges, the dispute mechanics, the frivolous claim tracking. These are the systems that determine:

Which cleaners appear at the top of search results
How much commission cleaners pay (tier-based)
How much cleaners can charge per hour (tier-based)
Whether a customer's dispute gets auto-resolved or requires admin review
Whether a cleaner gets suspended after a string of bad behavior
These aren't just nice-to-haves. They're the entire reason PureTask is different from "just another booking app."

What you'll get out of B3
A specification document that says, for every trust mechanic:

What event triggers it (e.g., "customer leaves a 2-star review")
What data needs to be read (e.g., "all reviews for this cleaner in last 90 days")
What gets computed (e.g., "new average_rating, new rating_score component")
What gets written back (e.g., "update score_snapshots, fire score_events row")
What downstream effects fire (e.g., "if score crosses tier band threshold, schedule tier_change")
By the end of B3, you (or a developer) will have a complete spec for the cron jobs and event handlers that make the system behave the way we designed.

Why this matters first
The trust layer has more business logic than any other part of PureTask, and it's the most likely place for subtle bugs. A bug in "show me my next booking" is annoying. A bug in "calculate this cleaner's reliability score" affects their income, their tier, and your platform's credibility.

Getting this right on paper before code starts is how you avoid that.

The five things we'll cover in B3
Reliability score calculation — How the 6-metric weighted formula actually runs nightly. What window of data it considers. How it handles new cleaners with no history.
Tier transitions — The 14-day buffer logic. How drops are scheduled vs. immediate. The 48-hour appeal window. Hard triggers (5 no-shows in 60 days) that bypass the buffer.
ZIP-locked badges — When a cleaner qualifies, when they expire, how the system knows.
Specialty endorsements — How review tags accumulate to grant badges. How badges revoke when patterns change.
Customer-side scoring — Reliability bands, frivolous claim tracking, when a customer gets restricted to request-to-book.
Decisions you'll face in B3
Score calculation window: 30, 60, or 90 days? Shorter = more responsive, longer = more stable. We discussed 90 days originally; I'll defend that here.
What happens during the score "warm-up" period for new cleaners? They start at 90 with no history. When does scoring become "real"?
Tier promotion buffer: same 14 days as demotion, or faster? Most platforms make promotion fast and demotion slow.
Frivolous claim threshold: 3 denied claims in 6 months, or different cadence?
I'll make recommendations on each. You push back if something feels wrong.

B2: Sync rules and computed fields
What it is, in plain English
Some fields in the database aren't sources of truth. They're cached versions of computations. When something changes, the cache needs to update.

Examples from our schema:

cleaner_profiles.average_rating — derived from all rows in reviews table for this cleaner
cleaner_profiles.total_jobs_completed — derived from count of bookings where status='approved'
cleaner_profiles.zip_codes_served — derived from cleaner's home base + service radius (geometric calculation)
customer_profiles.lifetime_spend_cents — sum of all approved booking totals
bookings.scheduled_end_at — calculated from scheduled_at + duration_hours
These are called "denormalized" or "computed" fields. They exist to make reads fast. The cost is that something has to keep them current.

Why we have them
Without them, every cleaner profile page would run queries like:

SELECT avg(rating) FROM reviews WHERE cleaner_id = ?
SELECT count(*) FROM bookings WHERE cleaner_id = ? AND status = 'approved'
SELECT distinct zip_code FROM bookings WHERE cleaner_id = ?
Every single time a customer views the cleaner's profile, those queries run. With 100 customers browsing simultaneously, you're doing 300+ aggregations per second. Database melts.

By caching the result on the cleaner's profile row, the read becomes:

SELECT * FROM cleaner_profiles WHERE user_id = ?
One query. Done. But now we owe the system a sync mechanism — when a new review arrives, average_rating needs to update.

The three sync strategies
There are exactly three ways to keep computed fields in sync. Each has tradeoffs:

Strategy 1: Database triggers
Postgres runs code automatically when a row is inserted/updated/deleted. Trigger watches reviews, recomputes cleaner_profiles.average_rating instantly.

Pros: Always consistent. Impossible to forget to call. Cons: Logic lives in the database, harder to test. Slower writes (every review insert triggers extra work). Can cascade unexpectedly.

Strategy 2: Application-layer recalculation
Backend code updates the computed field as part of the same transaction that inserts the source row. When the review controller saves a review, it also updates average_rating.

Pros: Logic lives in code, easier to test and debug. Explicit and visible. Cons: Easy to forget. If you have multiple places that insert reviews (web, mobile, admin tool), you have to remember in all of them. Bugs hide here.

Strategy 3: Background recalculation
A nightly job recomputes all denormalized fields from scratch.

Pros: Self-healing. Never gets badly out of sync. Cons: Stale by up to 24 hours. Doesn't work for real-time UX.

Real systems use all three for different fields. The skill is choosing the right strategy per field.

What you'll get out of B2
A specification document that lists every denormalized field across the schema and assigns it:

Which strategy (trigger, app-layer, or background)
Why that strategy (the reasoning)
The exact rule ("when reviews row inserted/updated, recompute as AVG(rating) WHERE cleaner_id = NEW.cleaner_id")
What can go wrong (race conditions, cascade scenarios)
Plus a complete list of background jobs needed and their schedules.

Why B2 matters
This is the layer where founders skip ahead and pay for it later. "We'll figure out the sync rules when we hit the bug." The bug shows up when a customer sees a cleaner's profile claiming 4.9 stars but their actual recent reviews average 3.2 — because somewhere a sync rule was missed. Customer loses trust, books elsewhere, never comes back.

Specifying this upfront takes one session. Debugging it after launch takes weeks of regression bugs.

Decisions you'll face in B2
Triggers vs. application-layer for cleaner_profiles.average_rating? I'll recommend application-layer for visibility, but triggers are defensible.
How fresh does total_jobs_completed need to be? Real-time? Hourly? Daily? Each has implications.
zip_codes_served: precomputed list or geometric query at read time? Tradeoff: storage vs. query complexity.
B1: Critical query patterns
What it is, in plain English
For each major thing the app does, walk through the actual SQL query. See exactly which tables are touched, which indexes are used, what gets returned, and how fast.

This is the "stress test" of the schema. We designed the tables. Now we're asking: can the database actually answer the questions the app needs to ask, fast enough, with the indexes we have?

What you'll get out of B1
For each of ~10 critical query patterns:

The user-facing scenario ("customer searching for cleaners near them on Wednesday")
The SQL query (annotated, explained line by line)
The query plan (which indexes get used, expected row counts)
Edge cases (what if no cleaners match, what if customer's address has no lat/lng yet, what if cleaner's zip_codes_served is empty)
Performance estimate ("at 1,000 cleaners and 10,000 customers, this query should run in under 50ms")
The 10 critical query patterns
These are the queries that matter most:

Cleaner search — "Show me cleaners available for a 2-hour standard cleaning in 94110 on Wednesday at 11 AM, sorted by best match"
Cleaner availability check — "Is this cleaner free at this specific time, considering buffer + lead time + time-off?"
Customer dashboard — "Show this customer's next upcoming booking + last 5 historical bookings + their favorite cleaners"
Cleaner dashboard — "Show this cleaner's score + tier + today's schedule + this week's earnings"
Active job lookup — "Show this booking's full state: cleaner location, photos uploaded, time elapsed"
Reliability score nightly batch — "For every active cleaner, recompute their score based on the last 90 days of activity"
Auto-approval cron — "Find all bookings where clocked_out_at + 24h has passed and status is awaiting_approval, mark them auto_approved"
Friday payout batch — "For every cleaner, sum all approved bookings since last payout, generate a payout row"
Admin dispute queue — "Show all active disputes sorted by urgency (Tier 2 first, then by age)"
Photo deletion sweep — "Find all photos past their scheduled_deletion_at where deleted_at is null, batch-delete"
Why this matters
Some queries that look easy on paper turn out to require indexes we didn't add, or join patterns that hit the database harder than expected. Catching this in B1 means you don't ship a product that grinds to a halt at 100 active cleaners.

The classic example: "Cleaner search" reads from users, cleaner_profiles, cleaner_services, cleaner_availability, cleaner_time_off, and possibly bookings (to check existing booking conflicts). That's 6 tables. Without the right indexes, this query crawls.

Decisions you'll face in B1
Mostly: are the indexes I specified correct, or do we need more? Are there queries I missed? Should some computed fields be added to make hot queries faster?

B4: Migration SQL
What it is, in plain English
The schema we designed lives in a Word doc. To turn it into an actual database, you need a sequence of SQL files (called "migrations") that, run in order, build the tables. And another sequence that, run in reverse, tears them down (in case you need to roll back).

Modern frameworks (Prisma, Drizzle, Rails ActiveRecord, Django, etc.) all use migration files. They're the canonical way to evolve a database over time.

What you'll get out of B4
Three files, ready to copy into a Postgres-managed migration system:

001_identity_and_profiles.sql — users, roles, customer/cleaner profiles, addresses
002_marketplace.sql — services, bookings, reviews, availability
003_trust_and_operations.sql — scores, photos, disputes, payouts, audit log
Each file contains:

CREATE TABLE statements with all constraints
CREATE INDEX statements
Seed data inserts (e.g., the services table needs to be pre-populated)
Down migration block (DROP TABLE in reverse order)
Why this is last
Migrations are the most mechanical part of the work. They're easy to write once you know what tables you need. Doing them last lets you incorporate any schema changes from B1, B2, B3.

If you're tempted to do B4 first ("just give me the SQL, I'll figure out the rest"), don't. Migration files are the cheapest part of this. Spec is the expensive part. Get the spec right, and migrations write themselves.

Decisions you'll face in B4
Phase 1 only or Phase 1+2? I'd recommend Phase 1 only (21 tables) to keep the initial migration tight.
How to handle seed data? Some tables (like services) need pre-populated rows. We'll spec these.
Multi-tenant considerations? PureTask is single-tenant for now. If you ever wanted to white-label the platform, schema changes. We'll skip this for v1.
B5: Specific table drill-down (catch-all)
What it is
Open question. Once we've done B3, B2, B1, B4, you'll have a clear picture. If specific tables still feel wrong, we work through them.

Most likely candidates for B5 attention:

bookings — it's the densest table, has the most state transitions, has the most fields. Could be split into bookings + booking_payments + booking_lifecycle_events. Or kept whole. Worth a dedicated discussion.
disputes — has overlapping responsibilities with dispute_messages and score_events. Worth confirming the split is right.
photos — heavy table, deletion lifecycle is complex. Worth a dedicated walkthrough.
What you'll get out of B5
A revised version of any specific table that needs it. Could be a split, a merge, additional fields, removed fields, different indexes.

Why we save this for last
You don't know what feels wrong until you've worked through how the tables get used (B1) and synchronized (B2) and what business logic depends on them (B3). B5 is the safety valve at the end.

Order of operations: my recommendation
Build context outward from the trickiest part:

B3 first (trust mechanics) — establishes how the differentiated parts of PureTask actually function. Everything else flows from this.
B2 second (sync rules) — now that you know what B3 needs, you can spec how denormalized fields stay correct.
B1 third (queries) — with mechanics and sync rules locked, validate that the schema can actually answer the questions the app needs to ask.
B4 fourth (migration SQL) — incorporate any tweaks from B1/B2/B3, then ship as runnable SQL.
B5 last (specific drill-downs) — if anything still feels wrong, we fix it before code starts.
Honest pacing expectations
Each session is substantial. Realistic time per section:

B3 — long session. Most insight per minute. Lots of decisions.
B2 — medium session. Mostly mechanical once B3 is clear.
B1 — long session. SQL-heavy, lots of edge cases.
B4 — short session. Just transcribing the spec into SQL.
B5 — variable. Could be 5 minutes or could be another long session depending on what surfaces.
If you want to spread these across multiple chats, do B3 alone, then come back fresh for B2, etc. If you want to power through, that's fine too — but quality drops on long sessions.

What I want from you before we proceed
Three things:

1. Validate the order
I'm proposing B3 → B2 → B1 → B4 → B5. Other orders are defensible:

B4 first if you want migrations in hand immediately and accept that they may need revision later
B1 first if you want to validate the schema works before investing in business logic
B5 first if there's a specific table keeping you up at night
I think B3 first produces the most value, but you might feel differently.

One section per session, or willing to do multiple in a row?

3. Surface any concerns from the schema we already locked
Reading the schema in the previous message, did anything feel wrong? Better to surface it now than discover it during B3 when we're using those tables.

Pick your three answers and we begin. Whichever direction you choose, I'll come into the session with the same approach — specify what's needed, flag tradeoffs, push back on anything that feels off, and produce something you can hand to a developer or use to write code yourself.

B3 — Trust Layer Deep Dive
What we're producing this session
A complete specification for the five trust mechanics:

Reliability score calculation (the daily nightly batch)
Tier transitions (the 14-day buffer + 48-hour appeal logic)
ZIP-locked badges (qualification + expiry)
Specialty endorsements (review-tag accumulation)
Customer-side scoring (frivolous claim tracking)
For each, you'll get: what triggers it, what data gets read, what gets computed, what gets written, what cascades. By the end of this session, a developer (or you) could implement these mechanics from this document.

This is going to be long. Take it in pieces.

Mechanic 1: Reliability score calculation
What it is
Every cleaner has a single number (0-100) that summarizes how reliable they've been recently. Score determines tier. Tier determines pay rate range and commission rate. The score is computed nightly from the previous day's events plus history.

When it runs
A nightly cron job, scheduled at 2:00 AM Pacific (low platform activity). Reads the last 24 hours of events plus the 90-day history window, computes new scores, writes a row to score_snapshots for every active cleaner.

The 90-day window decision
Locked in earlier: 90 days. I'm flagging it again because you should understand the tradeoff.

30 days: very responsive, but small samples give wild swings. A cleaner with only 8 jobs in 30 days has each job worth 12.5% of their score. One bad review tanks them.
60 days: middle ground. Used by some marketplaces.
90 days: slower to respond to changes (good or bad), but sample sizes stabilize. Cleaners trust it more because random bad luck washes out.
I recommend 90 days because trust requires stability. A cleaner whose score swings from 85 to 65 to 80 to 70 in a month feels jerked around. We can revisit if real data shows the window is too slow.

The six metrics and how each is computed
The score is a weighted sum of six sub-scores, each itself 0-100:

Metric A: On-time arrival (25% weight)
What counts: Did the cleaner clock in within 5 minutes of bookings.scheduled_at?

Data read: All bookings in last 90 days where status IN ('approved', 'auto_approved'). For each, compare clocked_in_at to scheduled_at.

Computation:

on_time_count = count of bookings where (clocked_in_at - scheduled_at) <= 5 minutes
late_count = count of bookings where late by 6-15 minutes
very_late_count = count of bookings where late by 16-30 minutes
no_show_count = count of bookings where late by >30 minutes (penalty applied separately)

on_time_score = round(100 * (on_time_count + 0.7 * late_count + 0.3 * very_late_count) / total_bookings)
Why the partial credit: A cleaner 8 minutes late is not equivalent to a no-show. The system rewards "showed up close to on time" with most of the credit.

Edge case — no bookings in window: Score defaults to 90 (favorable to new cleaners). After 6 jobs, the 90-default no longer applies.

Metric B: Job completion (25% weight)
What counts: Did the cleaner complete the job (clock out, upload photos, customer approves)?

Data read: All bookings where status NOT IN ('cancelled', 'no_show').

Computation:

completed_count = count of bookings with status IN ('approved', 'auto_approved')
disputed_count = count of bookings with status = 'disputed' AND dispute resolution favored cleaner
disputed_against_count = count of bookings disputed AND resolution went against cleaner
cleaner_cancellation_count = count of bookings where cancelled_by = cleaner_id AND <48h before scheduled_at

completion_score = round(100 * (completed_count + 0.5 * disputed_count) / 
                              (total + cleaner_cancellation_count + 0.5 * disputed_against_count))
Why disputed-and-won counts as half: Even if the cleaner is in the right, a dispute means something went wrong communicatively. Half-credit acknowledges they did the work without rewarding the conflict.

Why cleaner-initiated late cancellations hurt: Cleaners blocking customer time and then cancelling is a trust violation. Customers feel stranded.

Metric C: Photo compliance (15% weight)
What counts: Did the cleaner upload all required room photos for each booking?

Data read: Photos table joined to bookings. For each booking, count photos by phase (before/after) and required rooms (from services.required_room_types).

Computation:

For each booking in window:
  required_rooms = services.required_room_types[booking.service_id]
  rooms_with_before = distinct rooms with photos.phase = 'before'
  rooms_with_after = distinct rooms with photos.phase = 'after'
  
  fully_compliant = (required_rooms == rooms_with_before AND required_rooms == rooms_with_after)
  
photo_score = round(100 * fully_compliant_bookings / total_bookings)
Why this matters: Photo compliance is the trust system's foundation. A cleaner who skips photos can't be defended in disputes. The system protects them by penalizing skipped photos so they don't accumulate dispute losses.

Edge case — customer opted out of photos: If bookings.skip_rooms excluded all required rooms (rare), that booking is excluded from this metric.

Metric D: Customer ratings (15% weight)
What counts: Average star rating from reviews in the window, scaled to 0-100.

Data read: Reviews where cleaner_id = X AND created_at >= now() - 90 days.

Computation:

avg_rating = AVG(rating) from reviews in window

if avg_rating >= 4.8: rating_score = 100
elif avg_rating >= 4.5: rating_score = 90
elif avg_rating >= 4.3: rating_score = 75
elif avg_rating >= 4.0: rating_score = 60
elif avg_rating >= 3.8: rating_score = 50  # floor
else: rating_score = 50 (still floor — locked decision)
The 4.3 threshold and 3.8 floor: From our system design lock. Below 4.3, the score deducts. Below 3.8, the deduction stops (floor). The floor exists to prevent one cleaner with a 2.0 average from being unrecoverably penalized — instead, they get a low score that pushes them out of the system through other mechanics.

Edge case — fewer than 5 reviews in window: Use a Bayesian smoothed average. Treat the cleaner as having 5 "phantom" reviews at 4.5 stars (the platform average). This prevents new cleaners with one 5-star review from looking like 100/100 when they have no real signal yet.

Metric E: Communication (10% weight)
What counts: Response time to customer messages.

Data read: Messages where recipient_id = cleaner_id, joined to first response from cleaner.

Computation:

For each customer-initiated message in window:
  cleaner_response = first message from cleaner after this one in same booking thread
  response_time_minutes = cleaner_response.created_at - customer_message.created_at
  
avg_response_minutes = average across all instances

if avg_response_minutes <= 30: comm_score = 100
elif avg_response_minutes <= 120: comm_score = 85
elif avg_response_minutes <= 480: comm_score = 70  # 8 hours
elif avg_response_minutes <= 1440: comm_score = 55  # 24 hours
else: comm_score = 40
Why response time matters: Customers feel anxious before bookings. A cleaner who doesn't respond for 6 hours signals "I might not show up." Even if they show up perfectly, the customer experience was bad.

Edge case — no incoming messages: Default to 90 (no signal to penalize).

Metric F: Reschedule frequency (10% weight)
What counts: How often the cleaner has rescheduled bookings.

Data read: Bookings where status_history contains a reschedule event initiated by cleaner.

Computation:

cleaner_reschedules_count = count of bookings rescheduled by cleaner in window
total_bookings = count of all bookings in window

reschedule_rate = cleaner_reschedules_count / total_bookings

if reschedule_rate <= 0.05: reschedule_score = 100  # 5% or less
elif reschedule_rate <= 0.10: reschedule_score = 85
elif reschedule_rate <= 0.20: reschedule_score = 70
elif reschedule_rate <= 0.30: reschedule_score = 55
else: reschedule_score = 40
Why this matters: Reschedules disrupt customers. Some are genuine (illness, emergency). But a cleaner who reschedules 30% of bookings is unreliable from the customer's view, even if each individual reason was legitimate.

Note: Customer-initiated reschedules don't count against the cleaner.

Putting it together
final_score = round(
  0.25 * on_time_score +
  0.25 * completion_score +
  0.15 * photo_score +
  0.15 * rating_score +
  0.10 * comm_score +
  0.10 * reschedule_score
)
Result is a single integer 0-100. Written to score_snapshots along with the breakdown columns we specced.

Hard triggers (override everything)
Some events bypass the score and trigger immediate action:

5 no-shows in 60 days → 1-week suspension (status='suspended'). Locked decision.
3+ disputes lost in 90 days → manual admin review queue.
Single severe incident (theft confirmed, safety violation) → immediate suspension by admin.
These don't change the score. They change users.status directly.

What gets written
For each active cleaner, every night:

Insert row into score_snapshots with full breakdown.
Update cleaner_profiles.current_score to the new score.
Update cleaner_profiles.score_calculated_at to now.
Insert one or more rows into score_events if any specific events occurred in the last 24 hours (a late arrival, a 5-star review, etc.).
Check if the new score crosses a tier threshold — if so, schedule a tier_changes row (more on this in Mechanic 2).
Edge case: the warm-up period
New cleaners start with current_score = 90, no history. The score calculation needs to handle this gracefully:

0-3 jobs: Score stays at 90 (locked). Show "building reputation" badge. Tier locked at Rising Pro.
4-6 jobs: Score begins computing but is heavily smoothed (Bayesian priors weighted high). Tier still Rising Pro.
7+ jobs: Full score calculation kicks in. First tier evaluation eligible.
Decision you face here
The "warm-up" period and Bayesian smoothing on customer ratings — are these defensible? The alternative is "every metric uses raw data from day 1" which would mean a new cleaner with one 5-star review has a perfect rating component. Reasonable people disagree. I recommend the smoothed approach because it makes the early scores trustworthy.

Mechanic 2: Tier transitions
What it is
Every cleaner has a tier (Rising Pro / Proven Specialist / Top Performer / All-Star Expert). Tier determines pay rate range and commission rate. Tiers move based on score, but slowly — the 14-day buffer prevents a single bad week from dropping someone.

Tier thresholds
All-Star Expert:     score >= 90
Top Performer:       score 75-89
Proven Specialist:   score 60-74
Rising Pro:          score < 60 (also default for new cleaners)
When tier changes happen
Promotion: Faster than demotion. Locked decision: same 14-day buffer applies, but I want to pause here and challenge that.

Original lock: 14 days at the new band before tier moves, both directions.

My recommendation now: Promotions move in 7 days, demotions move in 14 days. Asymmetric.

Reasoning: Cleaners working hard to climb tiers feel jerked around if they have to wait 14 days to see the reward. Two weeks of "I'm clearly performing better but the system won't acknowledge it" creates resentment. Demotions, conversely, should be slow because they affect income — fairness requires stability.

Decision needed from you: Symmetric 14/14 (locked), or asymmetric 7 promotion / 14 demotion (my new recommendation)?

The 14-day buffer mechanic in detail
When a cleaner's score crosses a tier band threshold, the system doesn't move them immediately. Instead:

Insert a tier_changes row with:
direction = 'demotion' (or 'promotion')
scheduled_at = now()
effective_at = now() + 14 days (or 7 for promotions if asymmetric)
appeal_window_ends_at = effective_at - 12 days (assuming demotion, 48 hours from now)
Send notification to cleaner: "Your tier will change to X on Nov 12 unless your score returns to your current band."
Each subsequent night, the cron checks pending tier_changes:

If the cleaner's score has returned to their current band, cancel the pending change (set reverted_at, reason 'score_recovered').
If effective_at has passed and score is still in the new band, execute the change:
Update cleaner_profiles.current_tier
Update cleaner_profiles.tier_changed_at
Update cleaner_services.hourly_rate_cents if needed (tier max changed)
Send notification to cleaner: "Your tier is now X."
Hard triggers that bypass the buffer
5 no-shows in 60 days → immediate tier drop AND 1-week suspension. The buffer doesn't protect repeat offenders.
Admin manual override → immediate, with triggered_by = 'manual_admin' and reason logged.
Appeals
When a demotion is scheduled, the cleaner has 48 hours from tier_changes.scheduled_at to submit an appeal.

Appeal flow:

Cleaner sees tier-drop notification. UI shows "appeal" button.
Cleaner writes one paragraph explaining context (medical, family, customer issue).
System inserts row into tier_appeals with status = 'pending'.
Admin sees in dispute queue. Reviews appeal.
Admin decision goes back into tier_appeals.status:
approved → set tier_changes.reverted_at = now(), send "appeal granted" notification.
denied → tier change proceeds on effective_at, send "appeal denied" notification with reasoning.
Appeal limits:

Maximum 1 appeal per pending tier change (you can't appeal twice).
Veterans (6+ months on platform at 75+ score) get manual review for ALL drops — admin sees their drop in the queue automatically, even without a submitted appeal. This is the "veteran cushion" we locked.
What gets written during a tier change
On effective_at:

Update cleaner_profiles.current_tier
Update cleaner_profiles.tier_changed_at
Insert audit_log row capturing the before/after state
Send notification to cleaner
Optional: if rate range narrows (e.g., All-Star → Top Performer drops max from $80 to $65), automatically clamp cleaner_services.hourly_rate_cents to new max. Decision needed: auto-clamp or notify cleaner to manually adjust?
I recommend auto-clamp with notification ("Your max rate dropped from $80 to $65/hr because of tier change. Your services have been adjusted."). Otherwise existing customers might see a rate the cleaner can't legally charge under their new tier.

Decisions you face here
Symmetric or asymmetric tier transition timing? 14/14 (locked) or 7/14 (my new recommendation)?
Auto-clamp rates on demotion? Yes (recommended) or notify-and-wait?
Veteran cushion definition: "6+ months at 75+ score." Refine? (E.g., "6+ months OR 50+ jobs at 75+.")
Mechanic 3: ZIP-locked badges
What it is
Badges that are tied to a specific ZIP code. A cleaner can be "Top-rated in 94110" without being top-rated in other ZIPs. From our system design.

The three ZIP-locked badge types
Locked from earlier:

Trusted by Neighbors (10+ jobs at 4.5+ rating in this ZIP)
Top-rated in [ZIP] (25+ jobs, 4.7+ average, in this ZIP)
Customer Favorite in [ZIP] (5+ recurring customers in this ZIP)
When badge calculation runs
Daily cron job. Same nightly batch as scoring, runs at 2:30 AM (after scores).

How qualification works
For each cleaner, for each ZIP they've worked in:

For each (cleaner_id, zip_code) pair:
  jobs_in_zip = count of bookings where cleaner_id = X AND zip = Y AND status IN ('approved', 'auto_approved') AND in last 6 months
  avg_rating_in_zip = AVG(reviews.rating) for those bookings
  recurring_customers_in_zip = count distinct customer_id with active recurring_series in this ZIP
  
  If jobs_in_zip >= 10 AND avg_rating_in_zip >= 4.5:
    Award 'trusted_by_neighbors' badge for this ZIP
  
  If jobs_in_zip >= 25 AND avg_rating_in_zip >= 4.7:
    Award 'top_rated_in_zip' badge for this ZIP
    
  If recurring_customers_in_zip >= 5:
    Award 'customer_favorite_in_zip' badge for this ZIP
Badge expiry
Badges last 6 months from earning. Set cleaner_badges.expires_at = earned_at + 6 months.

Renewal
Each night, the badge calculation runs. If a cleaner re-qualifies for a badge they already have, extend the existing row's expires_at to now() + 6 months. Don't create a new row.

If they don't qualify when the existing badge has < 30 days until expiry, send a "your badge will expire soon" notification.

Revocation
Badges revoke automatically when expired (no nightly intervention needed — the partial index WHERE expires_at > now() handles display logic).

Hard revocation (admin manual): set revoked_at and revoke_reason.

What gets written
For each badge earned/extended/revoked:

Insert or update cleaner_badges row.
Update denormalized cleaner_profiles.zip_codes_served if a new ZIP appeared.
Send notification to cleaner if a new badge is earned ("You're now Top-rated in 94110!").
Edge case: Cleaner stops working in a ZIP
If a cleaner has badges in 94110 but moves and stops working there, their badges naturally expire after 6 months. No special handling needed.

Decisions you face here
Notification cadence on near-expiry: 30 days? 14 days? Both? I'd say both (30-day "heads up", 14-day "renewal in jeopardy").
Should expiring badges show a "fading" UI state to customers? I'd say no — this is internal accounting, customers see badge or no badge.
Mechanic 4: Specialty endorsements
What it is
Badges earned through customer review tags. "Eco-friendly verified," "Pet-friendly," "Move-out specialist," etc. Different from ZIP-locked because these are about traits, not location.

The trait → badge mapping
Locked from earlier wireframes:

Eco-friendly verified: 15+ reviews tagged 'eco_friendly'
Pet-friendly: 15+ reviews tagged 'great_with_pets'
Move-out specialist: 15+ completed move-out service bookings with 4.5+ avg rating
Airbnb expert: 15+ completed airbnb_turnover bookings with 4.5+ avg rating
Allergen-aware: 15+ reviews tagged 'allergen_aware'
On-time pro: 50+ bookings with on-time arrival in last 90 days
Qualification rules
For each cleaner, nightly:

For each badge:
  If badge requires review tags:
    matching_review_count = count of reviews where cleaner_id = X AND tag in trait_tags AND last 12 months
    If matching_review_count >= threshold:
      Award badge
  
  If badge requires service-specific completion:
    matching_bookings = count of bookings of that service type, completed, 4.5+ rated, last 12 months
    If matching_bookings >= threshold:
      Award badge
Why 12 months instead of 6?
Specialty badges are about consistent skill. A cleaner who's been eco-friendly for 12 months is more credible than one who collected tags in the last quarter. Longer window = more stable badges.

Revocation logic
Specialty badges fade if patterns change:

For each cleaner with specialty badge:
  recent_review_count = count of reviews in last 90 days
  matching_recent_count = count of those tagged with the trait
  
  If recent_review_count >= 5 AND matching_recent_count / recent_review_count < 0.30:
    # Less than 30% of recent reviews mention the trait. Pattern shifted.
    Schedule badge revocation in 90 days unless pattern returns
This is the "specialty badges aren't permanent" mechanic from our wireframes. They reflect what cleaners actually deliver, not what they once delivered.

What gets written
Same as ZIP-locked badges: insert/update cleaner_badges, send notifications, log to audit_log.

Decisions you face here
The 30% threshold for revocation. Reasonable? Higher (50%)? Lower (15%)?
Should cleaners be able to manually disable a badge they have? If they have "Pet-friendly verified" but actually don't want pet-friendly bookings anymore, can they hide it? I'd say yes — give them a toggle on cleaner_badges.is_visible.
Mechanic 5: Customer-side scoring
What it is
Customers also have a reliability score. Cleaners see it as a band ("Good standing" / "Mixed signals" / "Caution"), not a number. Customers don't see it at all unless they're restricted.

Why we have this
A two-sided marketplace needs to protect cleaners from bad customers as much as customers from bad cleaners. A customer who:

Cancels late repeatedly
Leaves wildly inconsistent ratings
Files frivolous disputes
Has had multiple cleaners flag them
...should be treated differently. The system surfaces this to cleaners who can decide whether to accept their bookings.

The five customer metrics
1. Honor commitments (40% weight)
   - Cancellation rate within 24h
   - No-show rate (cleaner arrives, customer not home, no comms)
   
2. Payment reliability (15% weight)
   - Failed payment attempts
   - Chargebacks
   
3. Cleaner ratings of customer (20% weight)
   - Cleaners can rate customers post-job (0-5 stars, optional)
   - Rude behavior, unsafe conditions, unreasonable expectations
   
4. Communication (10% weight)
   - Response time to cleaner messages
   - Provided required info (entry, pets, etc.)
   
5. Dispute pattern (15% weight)
   - Frivolous claim count (disputes denied based on evidence)
   - Total dispute frequency
Reliability bands shown to cleaners
score 85-100:  "Good standing" (green dot, 80%+ of customers)
score 65-84:   "Mixed signals" (no color, requires read)
score 0-64:    "Caution" (red dot, less than 5% of customers)
Frivolous claim tracking
Locked decision: 3 denied claims in 6 months = restricted to request-to-book.

Mechanic:

For each customer:
  denied_disputes = count of disputes where customer_id = X AND resolved against customer in last 6 months AND flagged_as_frivolous = true
  
  If denied_disputes >= 3:
    Set customer_profiles.restricted_to_request_to_book = true
    Send notification to customer with explanation and appeal path
What "request-to-book" means:

Customer can't instant-book a cleaner.
Their booking goes to the cleaner as a request.
Cleaner can accept or decline based on customer's standing.
Restriction lifts after 6 months if no new frivolous claims.
What gets written
Same nightly batch:

Compute customer reliability score per customer.
Update customer_profiles.reliability_score and reliability_band.
Check frivolous claim threshold; toggle restricted_to_request_to_book if needed.
Log to audit_log.
Decisions you face here
Should customer scores be shown as numbers or only bands? Locked to bands. Confirm.
Should cleaners see the customer's specific issues? ("3 prior cancellations within 24h") or just the band? I recommend bands only, with detailed view available to admins. Cleaners don't need (and shouldn't have) full visibility — it could be misused.
Restriction duration: 6 months. Locked. Confirm.
Putting it all together: the nightly batch
Every night at 2:00 AM Pacific, a job runs:

1. (2:00 AM) Score calculation batch
   For every active cleaner:
     - Compute new score
     - Insert score_snapshots row
     - Update cleaner_profiles.current_score
     - Insert any score_events for last-24-hour activity
     - Check if score crossed tier threshold
       → If yes, insert tier_changes row with future effective_at

2. (2:30 AM) Badge evaluation batch
   For every active cleaner:
     - Evaluate ZIP-locked badge qualifications
     - Evaluate specialty badge qualifications
     - Update cleaner_badges (insert/extend/revoke)
     - Send badge notifications

3. (3:00 AM) Tier change execution
   For every tier_changes row where effective_at <= now() AND reverted_at IS NULL:
     - Execute the change (update cleaner_profiles.current_tier)
     - Auto-clamp cleaner_services rates if needed
     - Send notifications
     - Log to audit_log

4. (3:30 AM) Customer score batch
   For every active customer:
     - Compute new reliability score
     - Update customer_profiles
     - Check frivolous claim threshold
     - Toggle restrictions if needed

5. (4:00 AM) Photo deletion sweep
   Find photos past scheduled_deletion_at where deleted_at IS NULL
   Delete from R2 storage
   Update photos table

6. (4:30 AM) Auto-approval batch
   For every booking where status = 'awaiting_approval' AND clocked_out_at + 24h <= now():
     - Update status to 'auto_approved'
     - Capture Stripe payment
     - Insert score_event for cleaner
     - Send notification to both parties
This is the trust layer's heartbeat. It runs every night, idempotent (safe to re-run if it fails partway), with full audit trail.

Decisions you need to make
I've flagged decision points throughout. Here they are consolidated for an explicit answer:

Score window: 90 days? (Recommended, locked.)
Promotion timing: 7 days? (My new recommendation, was locked at 14.)
Demotion timing: 14 days? (Locked, recommended kept.)
Auto-clamp rates on tier demotion? (Recommended yes.)
