WHY CANT I SEE AND SCROLL THROUGH OUR CONVERSATION

10:57 AM
Claude responded: That's frustrating.
That's frustrating. The conversation is still there on the server — the issue is almost always client-side.

Most likely causes
1. The conversation is too long for the UI to render smoothly. This chat has gotten long (compaction happened earlier, plus 5 wireframe batches with thousands of lines of HTML pasted into messages). Long chats can lag, freeze scroll, or render only the latest portion in mobile/web browsers. This is the most common cause.

2. Browser memory issue. Lots of images, code blocks, and rendered file previews in this chat. Your browser tab may be running low on memory.

3. App glitch on mobile. The Claude mobile app sometimes loses scroll on long chats, especially after presenting files.

Quick fixes to try
Refresh the page (Cmd+R / Ctrl+R on web, or close-and-reopen the app on mobile)
Close other browser tabs if on web — frees memory
Switch device — if you're on mobile, try claude.ai in a desktop browser, or vice versa
Hard scroll — sometimes tapping to the very top, then back down, re-renders missing portions
If it's still broken
The conversation isn't lost. Everything we built is saved as files in /mnt/user-data/outputs/. Even if this chat becomes unusable, you can:

Start a fresh chat
Reference the files (they persist across sessions)
I can recover the locked decisions and wireframe inventory from past conversation memory
Important
Don't lose the work. Before doing anything risky like refreshing, make sure you have a way back to the files. The 6 wireframe HTML files are in your outputs and should be downloadable from the chat where I presented them — those file cards stay accessible even if scroll breaks.

Tell me what device/browser you're on and whether refresh helps. If the chat is genuinely toast, I can give you a clean handoff prompt to start fresh in a new conversation without losing context.

You said: LETS MOVE ON TO DATABASE SCHEMA .
LETS MOVE ON TO DATABASE SCHEMA . FOR ALL WIREFRAMES

11:04 AM
Claude responded: Good call.
Good call. Database schema time.

Before I start designing tables, I want to be honest about something: a real schema for 70 wireframes is substantial work — probably 25-35 tables, a couple hundred columns, and a lot of relationship decisions. Doing it well in one shot is risky for the same reason 19 wireframes in one shot was risky: quality drops at the back half.

I'm going to propose a structured approach instead of just dumping a giant SQL file.

Proposed structure for Direction B
Six logical groups, built and reviewed in order:

B1 — Core identity + accounts (4-5 tables)

User, CustomerProfile, CleanerProfile, Address, Session
Foundation everything else hangs off of
B2 — Booking lifecycle (6-8 tables)

Booking, BookingStateEvent, RecurringBooking, RecurringOccurrence, BookingService, BookingNote
The heart of the marketplace
B3 — Trust + evidence (5-6 tables)

Photo, Message, Review, Tip, Dispute, DisputeMessage
The differentiator features
B4 — Cleaner ops (6-7 tables)

ReliabilityEvent, ReliabilityScoreSnapshot, TierAssignment, Badge, BadgeAssignment, AvailabilityRule, TimeOff, Specialty
Score + tier + badges system
B5 — Money (4-5 tables)

PaymentMethod, Charge, Payout, PayoutLineItem, InsurancePolicy
Financial side, Stripe Connect mirror
B6 — Platform ops (3-4 tables)

AdminAction, Notification, NotificationPreference, FeatureFlag
The plumbing
Each group includes: full schema with column types and constraints, foreign key relationships, indexes, the wireframes that drive each decision, and what's MVP vs. deferred.

Why split it this way
Quality. Each group is small enough to review carefully without context drift.

Honest extrapolation. Some schema decisions aren't covered by the locked wireframes — I'll have to make defensible inferences (e.g., what columns ReliabilityEvent needs, exactly). Smaller batches mean you can catch and correct those before they propagate.

Real handoff to code. When you're ready to build, you'll be implementing one or two of these groups per sprint anyway. Having them organized this way maps directly to your build sequence.

A few decisions I need from you before B1
These shape everything downstream:

If you want me to just decide and move: PostgreSQL, shared users + profile tables (third option — best for marketplace platforms where the same person could theoretically be both customer and cleaner, and where admin tooling needs unified user lookup), and full schema with MVP/Phase 2 marked.

But these choices are real, so it's worth pausing here. Pick or tell me to decide.

11:06 AM
Claude responded: Good answers.
Good answers. Let me address the identity model question honestly.

Identity model — my recommendation
Shared users table + separate profile tables (option 3).

Here's why, in plain English:

The three options compared
Option 1 — Single users table with a role column. You'd have one table where every row has a role field set to "customer", "cleaner", or "admin". Cleaner-specific fields (like rate, tier, score) and customer-specific fields (like favorite cleaners, payment methods) all live in this one table.

Pros: Simplest, fewest tables, easy to query "find a user by email"
Cons: Most columns are null most of the time (cleaner fields are null for customers, customer fields are null for cleaners). Gets ugly fast. A cleaner has ~30 specific fields, a customer has ~10 specific fields, admins have ~5. That's 45 columns on a table where any given row uses only one third of them.
Option 2 — Separate customers and cleaners tables, no shared user. Two independent tables. A cleaner's email lives in cleaners.email. A customer's email lives in customers.email. No connection between them.

Pros: Clean separation, no null fields
Cons: You can't enforce a unique email across the platform. Someone could sign up as a customer with jordan@example.com, then sign up as a cleaner with the same email, and you'd have two completely separate accounts that don't know about each other. Auth becomes complicated. Admin lookup ("find anything for jordan@example.com") requires checking two tables. Real-world consequence: if you ever decide a person can be both a customer and a cleaner (and you might — your most loyal cleaners often want to book cleanings themselves), you'd need to refactor the entire identity layer.
Option 3 — Shared users table + separate customer_profiles and cleaner_profiles tables. Every person has one row in users (email, password hash via Clerk, name, phone, created_at). Then customer_profiles has all the customer-specific fields and points back to a user_id. Same for cleaner_profiles. Same for admin_profiles.

Pros: One source of truth for identity. Email uniqueness is automatic. Auth is clean. Same person can be a customer and a cleaner if needed (just have both profile rows). Admin lookup finds anyone in one query. This is how Uber, DoorDash, Instacart, Airbnb, and basically every two-sided marketplace structures their identity.
Cons: Slightly more joins when querying. One extra table per role. Worth it.
Why option 3 wins for PureTask specifically
Three concrete reasons from your locked decisions:

Cleaners are people who could become customers. Your most engaged cleaners might want to book a deep clean for their own apartment one day. Option 3 lets you flip that on with a single row insert. Option 2 requires a complete identity migration.
Customer signup gates at the payment step. That means a customer might sit in the database with just a users row (created at payment) before their full customer_profile is fleshed out. The schema should support "user exists but profile is partial" naturally. Option 3 does this cleanly; option 1 forces every new user to populate every column.
Admin tooling needs unified lookup. Per the locked admin wireframes (in wireframes_batch7_admin.html), admins search by email, name, or phone across the whole platform. Option 3 makes this a single query against users. Option 2 requires unioning two tables.
My recommendation locked
I'm going with option 3 unless you push back. Specifically:

users — id, email (unique), phone, full_name, hashed_password (or auth_provider_id from Clerk), created_at, updated_at, status (active/suspended/deleted)
customer_profiles — user_id (FK), favorite_cleaner_ids, default_payment_method_id, photo_policy_setting, etc.
cleaner_profiles — user_id (FK), bio, profile_photo_url, languages, current_tier, current_score, hourly_rates (jsonb), buffer_minutes, lead_time_hours, stripe_connect_account_id, etc.
admin_profiles — user_id (FK), permissions, last_active_at
If you want both customer and cleaner identity for the same person, you create both profile rows pointing to the same user row.

Locked-in for Direction B
PostgreSQL (Supabase recommended — matches your stack, free tier covers your launch needs)
Identity: shared users + separate profile tables
Scope: full schema, no MVP/Phase 2 distinction
What I'm building next
B1 — Core identity + accounts. This first batch covers:

users — the central identity row
customer_profiles — customer-specific data
cleaner_profiles — cleaner-specific data (this one is dense — rates, tier, score, availability rules)
admin_profiles — admin tooling data
addresses — physical addresses (customer service addresses, cleaner home base, etc.)
auth_sessions — active sessions for the security/sessions wireframe
For each table I'll give you: column-by-column schema with PostgreSQL types and constraints, the wireframes that drove each field, foreign keys, recommended indexes, and any judgment calls I had to make. Plus a Mermaid-style relationship diagram for the group.

What changed in v2
I went through every wireframe systematically (all 70) and asked "what data does this screen need to read or write?" Then compared that to the v1 table list. The audit found real gaps.

Numbers:

v1 had 39 tables → v2 has 53 tables
8 new tables added
4 tables restructured
3 enum/column refinements
2 wireframe surfaces had zero schema coverage in v1 (cleaner suspensions, refunds)
1 new group added (B7 — onboarding & verification)
Highlights of what I missed in v1
The biggest miss was treating cleaner_profiles as the only cleaner record. Wireframe 55 (admin application queue) makes obvious that pending applications are a different state than approved cleaners. They have their own state machine, their own data, their own audit needs. Adding cleaner_applications as a separate table.

Background checks were entirely missing. Wireframe 33 shows Checkr-managed checks with provider, expiration, renewal — they're recurring infrastructure, not a one-time event. They get their own table.

Cleaner suspension and probation enforcement had no place in the data model. The "can this cleaner accept a new booking right now?" query needs a cleaner_suspensions table, not just a boolean on the profile.

Customer-side reliability events were missing entirely. Even though the wireframes don't show customer-facing reliability scoring (it's deferred to Phase 2), the admin tooling does, and capturing the data from day one means we don't backfill later.

Photo categories were richer than my v1 enum. Wireframes show 7 distinct purposes: before/after photos, dispute evidence (customer + cleaner), profile photos, insurance certificates, identity documents. Each has different retention and access rules.

Refunds were noted as an "open question" in v1 but they're real — wireframe 62 shows admin refund processing. They get their own table now, separate from disputes (because not every refund comes from a dispute).

Honest concerns I have with v2
53 tables is a lot. Most successful marketplaces I'm aware of run 60-80 tables in production, so we're in normal territory, but it's not "lean." The justification is that you're building a trust-heavy marketplace where audit/compliance matters. If you'd rather start leaner and add tables as needed, we could defer 6-8 tables to Phase 2 (customer_reliability_events, notification_deliveries, user_milestones, cleaner_appeals, possibly traits lookup table, possibly serviced_areas).

The booking state machine grew. v1 had ~15 states, v2 has 20+. That's because real wireframes have more transitions than I accounted for (running late, reschedule pending, auto-approved vs approved as distinct events). It's defensible but it does mean more code to manage state transitions.

B7 is a judgment call. Could fold it into B1. The lifecycle is distinct (an applicant becomes a cleaner only after approval), but it's not strictly required to separate them.

Claude responded: Honest answer: yes, but with discipline about what kind of review.
Honest answer: yes, but with discipline about what kind of review.

There are two failure modes here:

Under-review: start building B1 too fast, hit problems in B3 or B4 that require going back and refactoring B1. That's expensive.
Over-review: keep refining the overview indefinitely, never write a single CREATE TABLE, lose momentum.
The right answer is one more focused review pass with specific goals, not an open-ended "let's look at everything again." Let me explain what I'd actually do.

What a productive third review looks like
A real engineering review has four checks, in this order. Each takes me a focused look, not an unbounded one.

Check 1 — Cross-table integrity check. For every foreign key I'm planning, does the target table have the column in the right form? Are there orphan references? Things like: commission_records references tier_assignments — does that join actually work given how I'm modeling tier history? This catches the "I designed table A and table B separately, but together they don't compose" bugs.

Check 2 — Query walkthrough for the 10 hottest paths. Pick the 10 most-frequent queries the app will run (load customer dashboard, load cleaner dashboard, fetch active job tracker, search cleaners by ZIP, calculate score, etc.) and trace exactly which tables they hit and whether the indexes I've planned cover them. This catches "the schema is technically correct but reading from it requires 8 joins for the homepage" bugs.

Check 3 — Write-path safety check. For every multi-table write (book a cleaning, file a dispute, complete a job, start a recurring schedule), does the schema allow atomic transactions? Are there places where partial state could leave the database inconsistent? This catches the "race condition where a booking gets created but the charge fails and now we have a phantom booking" bugs.

Check 4 — Day-1 vs Day-365 check. Walk the schema through realistic scaling: 10 cleaners and 50 customers (Day 1), then 200 cleaners and 5000 customers (Day 365). Does anything break? Tables that grow unboundedly (reliability_events, messages, notifications) — do we have retention policies? Tables that need to scale linearly with bookings — are they partitioned or indexed for that?

What I'd skip in this review
Re-debating the 53 tables. We've been through that. They're locked.
Re-discussing whether to collapse the booking state enum. We made a call.
Re-litigating Group B7. It's its own group.
My honest read on whether this is worth doing
Yes, for two reasons:

B1 is the foundation. Mistakes there propagate everywhere. The users + profiles design specifically — if we get the cleaner-profile-vs-cleaner-application split wrong, we're refactoring half the schema later.
You'll be reviewing my SQL anyway. Better to catch issues at the structural level (cheap) than after I've written 50 CREATE TABLE statements (expensive).
The risk of not doing it: building a schema that works for individual screens but breaks down when actual application code tries to use it as a coherent system.

My counter-proposal
Instead of a generic "review everything once more," let me run those four checks as a single audit pass. I'll:

Walk through 10 hot query paths and confirm the schema supports them with reasonable index coverage
Walk through 6 multi-table write paths and confirm transaction atomicity is achievable
Check every foreign key for cross-table consistency
Stress-test the schema mentally at Day 1 and Day 365 scale
Output: a single audit document that's structured the same way as v2's audit — gaps found, structural changes recommended, things refined. Either zero changes (we're good to start B1) or a small set of fixes before we build.

This takes me a focused effort, not an open-ended one. And it's the last gate before SQL.

If you want me to just decide: run the full 4-check audit. It's the highest-leverage thing we can do before SQL gets written. Mistakes caught now are cheap; mistakes caught after B1 are not.

But your call.

Let me think through each check carefully before writing the audit document. I'll work them through in order.

Check 1 — Cross-table integrity. For every foreign key, does the target table have the column needed and does the relationship compose?

Let me trace a few that I'm worried about:

commission_records → references tier_assignments (to capture cleaner's tier at time of booking). But tier_assignments is a historical record — which row do we reference? The active one at booking time. So commission_records needs tier_assignment_id AND the data should be denormalized (tier name, commission rate) into commission_records itself so it's stable.
cleaner_appeals → references either tier_assignments OR reliability_events. I said "one of two FK columns populated, the other null." But that's a sign of a bad design. Better: a single appeal_target_type enum + appeal_target_id (polymorphic FK pattern). But polymorphic FKs in Postgres require check constraints. Need to think about this.
recurring_occurrences → spawn bookings. So bookings.recurring_schedule_id is set, AND recurring_occurrences has a booking_id. That's a circular-ish reference. Actually fine — the occurrence row points to the booking it spawned.
customer_favorites (customer_user_id, cleaner_user_id) — both are user_ids. But not all users are customers, not all are cleaners. The constraint that customer_user_id has a customer_profile and cleaner_user_id has a cleaner_profile isn't enforced by FK alone. Need a check.
dispute_resolutions → references disputes. But where does the refund link in? If resolution_type is REFUND, do we link to a refunds row? Or do we create the refund row from the dispute resolution? The data flow needs to be clear.
Check 2 — Query walkthrough for hottest paths.

Let me trace 10 queries:

Customer homepage / dashboard load (wireframe 1 + 11): User logs in → we need their next booking, their favorites with last-booked dates, recent bookings with ratings, recurring suggestion if applicable.
Tables hit: users, customer_profiles, bookings, customer_favorites, reviews, recurring_schedules
Potential issue: "recurring suggestion" requires counting past bookings with same cleaner. Index needed: bookings(customer_user_id, cleaner_user_id, state).
Index check: I have bookings(customer_id, state) in v2. Need to extend to include cleaner_user_id.
Cleaner dashboard load (wireframe 2): Score widget, tier widget, today's schedule, earnings summary.
Tables hit: users, cleaner_profiles, reliability_score_snapshots (latest), tier_assignments (current), bookings (today), payouts (current week)
Index check: reliability_score_snapshots(cleaner_user_id, snapshot_date DESC) — yes I have this.
bookings(cleaner_user_id, start_at) for today's schedule — yes.
Cleaner list filtered by ZIP + service + Sat 10am (wireframe 8): The biggest query the app runs.
Tables hit: cleaner_profiles, cleaner_service_zips, availability_rules, time_off_blocks, bookings (to subtract booked slots), reviews (for rating average), badges
This is the hardest one. The query needs to: find cleaners serving this ZIP, who offer this service, who have an availability_rule covering this day-of-week, with no time_off blocking the date, no booking conflicting with the time slot, and return rating + tier + badges + photo.
Concern: With 6+ joins, this query gets slow at scale. Probably need a materialized view or a denormalized "cleaner_availability_index" that pre-computes "who's free at this date+time+zip."
Gap I want to flag: I didn't model rating averages anywhere. We'd compute them on the fly from reviews, which is expensive. Should we cache average_rating + review_count denormalized on cleaner_profiles? Yes.
Active job tracker load (wireframe 9): Customer sees live timeline, photos as they arrive.
Tables hit: bookings, booking_state_events (for timeline), booking_photos
Photos arriving "live" implies the page polls or uses websockets. Either way, the query is bookings(id), booking_state_events(booking_id, created_at), booking_photos(booking_id, created_at). All indexed.
Approve & pay screen (wireframe 10): Photo gallery, charge breakdown.
Tables hit: bookings, booking_photos, charges (for the auth)
Charge breakdown shows the cleaner's rate, hours, fee. That data lives on the booking row (rate, hours captured at booking time).
Gap I want to flag: I never explicitly said booking captures hourly_rate_at_booking and total_amount_cents as immutable columns. Let me confirm in v2... yes, this needs to be explicit.
Filing a dispute (wireframe 16): Read booking, write dispute, write photos.
Tables hit: bookings, disputes, dispute_messages, booking_photos (with dispute_evidence_customer category)
Multi-table write — needs transaction. Check 3 territory.
Score calculation nightly job:
For every cleaner, read all reliability_events from past 90 days, compute score by metric category, write to reliability_score_snapshots.
Tables hit: cleaner_profiles (list of all cleaners), reliability_events (window), reliability_score_snapshots (write).
Index: reliability_events(cleaner_user_id, created_at DESC). Yes.
Concern: This job runs nightly. If we have 1000 cleaners and 50 events per cleaner per quarter = 50k events. Manageable. At 10k cleaners we'd want incremental computation, not full refresh.
Recurring spawn job: For every active recurring_schedule, check if next occurrence needs to be created.
Tables hit: recurring_schedules (active), recurring_occurrences (latest), bookings (write)
Index: recurring_schedules(status='active'), recurring_occurrences(recurring_schedule_id, scheduled_at DESC).
Notification center load (wireframe 19): User's recent notifications.
Tables hit: notifications(user_id, created_at DESC)
Index check: yes.
Concern: Notifications grow unboundedly. Need retention policy. v2 mentions this but doesn't lock a retention period. 90 days is reasonable.
Admin booking lookup (wireframe 58): Search bookings by ID, customer name, cleaner name.
Tables hit: bookings, users (for both customer and cleaner names)
Search by partial name needs full-text search (tsvector) or ILIKE with trigram index.
v2 mentions tsvector but doesn't specify which tables. Need to lock: users gets a tsvector for full_name + email, bookings gets one for booking_id text representation.
Check 3 — Write-path atomicity for 6 multi-table writes:

Book a cleaning (wireframe via booking flow): Insert booking, insert booking_state_event (CONFIRMED), authorize charge via Stripe (returns charge_id), insert charges row. Transaction concern: If Stripe call succeeds but DB write fails, we have a phantom charge. Need: insert booking + state_event in one transaction first, then call Stripe with idempotency key, then update charges row. If Stripe fails, mark booking as CHARGE_FAILED.
Gap: I don't have a CHARGE_FAILED state in the booking enum. Need to add it.
Approve & pay (wireframe 10): Update booking state to APPROVED, insert state event, capture the Stripe charge (was authorized at booking), update charges row, schedule payout line item.
Gap: payout_line_items get created at approval, not at booking. They're aggregated weekly Friday. Schema needs payout_line_items.payout_id to be nullable until the Friday batch assigns it.
File a dispute: Update booking state to DISPUTED, insert dispute row, insert dispute_messages (initial customer message), insert booking_photos with dispute_evidence_customer purpose.
All single transaction. Fine.
Cleaner accepts a booking (wireframe 3): Update booking state from REQUESTED to CONFIRMED, insert state event, send notification to customer.
Concurrency issue: Two cleaners might try to accept the same booking? Actually no — bookings are routed to one cleaner specifically. But if a customer rebooks the same time and a cleaner is offered two — we need a unique constraint preventing double-booking on a cleaner.
Gap: I noted this as a constraint in v2 but didn't specify it as an EXCLUSION CONSTRAINT (Postgres feature) on bookings. Need to add.
Process recurring spawn: Insert recurring_occurrence, insert booking, insert booking_state_event (CONFIRMED), authorize charge.
Same pattern as new booking.
Score calculation nightly: For each cleaner, read events, compute, insert snapshot. Idempotent if we use UPSERT on (cleaner_user_id, snapshot_date) primary key. Good.
Check 4 — Day-1 vs Day-365 scale check:

Day 1 (10 cleaners, 50 customers, 100 bookings):

All tables tiny, every query is fast. No concerns.
Day 365 conservative (200 cleaners, 5000 customers, 50,000 bookings):

bookings table: 50k rows. Indexed properly = fine.
booking_state_events: ~10 events per booking = 500k rows. Indexed by booking_id = fine.
booking_photos: ~10 photos per booking = 500k rows. Concern: storage cost. Cloudflare R2 at 500k photos × ~500KB = 250GB. ~$3-4/month. Manageable.
messages: ~5 messages per booking × 50k bookings = 250k. But auto-purged after 4h post-booking, so working set stays small. Fine.
reliability_events: ~30 events per cleaner × 200 cleaners × 4 quarters = 24k. Tiny.
reliability_score_snapshots: 200 cleaners × 365 days = 73k. Tiny.
notifications: 5-10 per booking × 50k = 250k-500k. Concern: unbounded growth. 90-day retention policy needed (locking this).
Day 365 aggressive (1000 cleaners, 30k customers, 300k bookings):

bookings: 300k. Fine with proper indexes.
booking_photos: 3M photos. R2 storage ~1.5TB. $20/month. Still fine.
booking_state_events: 3M rows. Need to think about archival.
Concern emerged: at 300k bookings/year, individual queries on bookings() table need composite indexes that don't conflict. Let me list the indexes I'd need:
(customer_user_id, state, start_at DESC) — for customer dashboard
(cleaner_user_id, start_at) — for cleaner schedule
(state, start_at) — for system jobs (auto-approval)
(zip_code, service_id, start_at) — for cleaner search (this one's problematic — bookings doesn't typically have zip directly, it's via address)

Audit complete — 9 issues found, all resolvable before B1
I went through each of the four checks systematically. Here's the honest read:

The v2 structure is fundamentally sound. No new tables needed, no group reorganizations, no fundamental rethinks. The 9 issues are the kind of detail-level structural problems that would have surfaced during B1-B6 anyway — but catching them now means I write better SQL the first time instead of refactoring later.

The 9 issues, ranked by what matters most
Most important — three patterns that affect almost everything:

Issue 1.3 + 2.2 — Historical immutability through denormalization. Bookings need to capture the cleaner's rate, tier, commission rate, and total at booking creation. If the cleaner changes their rate later, the historical booking shouldn't change. Same pattern for commission records. This is the single biggest insight from the audit and it threads through B2, B4, and B5.

Issue 2.1 — Denormalized aggregates on cleaner_profiles. The cleaner list page needs average_rating, review_count, completed_booking_count cached on the profile, not computed from reviews on every query. Standard pattern, but I missed it in v2.

Issue 3.3 — Database-level prevention of cleaner double-booking. Application-level checks have race conditions at scale. Postgres EXCLUSION CONSTRAINT enforces it at the DB layer. Postgres-specific, requires btree_gist extension (standard in Supabase).

Important — fixes for state machine and write paths:

Issue 3.1 — Booking state machine needs CHARGE_FAILED and PENDING_PAYMENT_AUTHORIZATION states. Without these, a Stripe authorization failure leaves orphan bookings.

Issue 3.2 — payout_line_items.payout_id must be nullable. Approved bookings create line items immediately, but payouts get assembled Friday. Without nullability, the data flow is impossible.

Issue 1.2 — Join tables FK to profile tables, not users. Stricter, prevents nonsense data (admin users showing up in customer favorites).

Issue 4.1 — 90-day notification retention. Without it, notifications grows unboundedly.

Architectural cleanup:

Issue 1.1 — cleaner_appeals polymorphic FK. Need to decide: subtype tables (cleaner architecture, 3 tables) or polymorphic with CHECK constraint (simpler, 1 table). I lean subtype but it's a real tradeoff.

Lowest-impact:

Issue 1.5 — Drop one redundant FK between recurring_occurrences and bookings. Cosmetic cleanup.

Honest mistakes in my own work
Two embarrassing things I want to flag:

I miscounted the summary at the top of the audit. I wrote "6 require schema changes" then listed 9. The 9 is correct. I left both numbers in the document with a "wait — that's 9, not 6" honest correction. Easier to read than rewriting and pretending I got it right the first time.
The historical immutability pattern (Issue 1.3 + 2.2) was a real gap I should have caught in v2. Marketplaces always need this — a cleaner's tier changes don't retroactively change historical bookings. v2 implied it but didn't make the columns explicit. Lock it in now.
What I want from you before B1
Three decisions in the document, repeated here:

Apply all 9 fixes during B1-B6? (Recommended, all defensible.)
For Issue 1.1 — subtype tables or polymorphic FK?
Subtype = cleaner, 3 tables, no CHECK constraints needed
Polymorphic = simpler, 1 table with CHECK constraint, slightly awkward queries
I lean subtype but if you'd rather keep table count down, polymorphic is fine.
Are the denormalization patterns OK with you? Some teams hate them because they require triggers or batch jobs to keep in sync. They're necessary here for query performance and historical immutability, but worth confirming you accept the tradeoff (slight write complexity for major read benefits).
If you want me to just decide everything: apply all 9 fixes, use subtype tables for cleaner_appeals, accept the denormalization patterns. Then we start B1.

Part 1 — Answering my own questions
Going through every open question I posed across the three planning documents. Honest answers, not hedging.

From the v1 overview
Q: Customer favorites — join table or JSON array? A: Join table. Audited and locked in v2. Reason: query patterns (filtering, sorting, "regular vs saved" tabs from wireframe 25) work cleanly with rows, awkwardly with JSON. Plus we want timestamps on each favorite.

Q: Should traits be enum or lookup table? A: Lookup table. Reason: traits feed into specialty endorsement logic (per locked design — repeat positive ratings in a category earn specialties). A lookup table lets us version the trait catalog and add metadata (which traits map to which specialties). Enum would force a database migration every time we add a trait.

Q: Photos in disputes — separate table or unified booking_photos with purpose column? A: Unified table with purpose enum. Reason: photos are photos. Same storage, same upload mechanics, same retention concerns. The purpose enum (BEFORE_CLOCK_IN / AFTER_CLOCK_OUT / DISPUTE_EVIDENCE_CUSTOMER / DISPUTE_EVIDENCE_CLEANER / etc.) is enough to scope queries. Separate tables would force code duplication.

Q: Should reliability_events store score deltas or raw events? A: Both. The event is raw (e.g., "5_STAR_REVIEW") but the row also stores point_delta calculated by application code at write time. Reason: auditability. Six months from now when someone asks "why did this score go up by 1.4 points?" we can answer. Without point_delta on the row, recomputing requires re-running the scoring algorithm against historical data — fragile.

Q: Score snapshot frequency — daily for everyone, or only when something changes? A: Daily for everyone. Reason: simpler operationally, eliminates "the score widget shows stale data" bugs, makes time-series queries trivial ("what was Maria's score 30 days ago?"). Storage cost is trivial (~50KB/day per 1000 cleaners).

Q: Tax info encryption — database-level or application-level? A: Application-level using a key from environment. Reason: database backups don't expose plaintext SSNs. Postgres pgcrypto would also work, but application-level lets us rotate keys independent of the database, and means a breached database alone doesn't expose tax data.

Q: Refunds — separate table or refund event in charges? A: Separate refunds table. Already locked in v2. Reason: refunds have their own lifecycle (issued, processing, completed, failed), can be partial, can be issued for non-dispute reasons (goodwill, duplicate charges). Stuffing into charges via state events would conflate two distinct concepts.

From the v2 overview
Q: Do we need a photo_upload_jobs table for retry tracking? A: No. Audited and locked. Reason: client-side queue with offline retry. Once a photo POSTs to the server, it's in booking_photos. Permanent client-side failures show up as photo_count mismatch, surfaced as a soft warning. One less table to maintain.

Q: Should we save customer search history? A: Skip for MVP. Reason: not in any wireframe, not validated by user behavior data, easy to add later if/when we want it. Premature.

Q: Notification quiet hours? A: Schema-ready, UI-deferred. Add the columns to notification_preferences (quiet_hours_start, quiet_hours_end, quiet_hours_enabled) but don't expose in MVP UI. Cheap to add now, expensive to add later if we forget.

Q: Currency — assume USD only? A: Multi-currency-ready columns, USD-only enforcement. Every money column is INTEGER cents. Add currency CHAR(3) NOT NULL DEFAULT 'USD' to charges, payouts, refunds, tips. Application code can enforce USD-only at MVP. Schema is ready when we expand.

Q: Time zones — how stored? A: Add timezone VARCHAR(50) to users. All timestamps as TIMESTAMP WITH TIME ZONE. Standard practice, no controversy.

From the audit (v3) — the final 3 decisions
Q: Apply all 9 fixes from the audit? A: Yes, all 9. Reason: every issue is real, every fix is defensible, none introduce significant complexity. The denormalization patterns add some write complexity but the read benefits are substantial. The exclusion constraint is the only Postgres-specific feature, and we're already locked into Postgres via Supabase.

Q: For Issue 1.1 — subtype tables or polymorphic FK? A: Polymorphic FK with CHECK constraint. Reasoning honestly: I leaned subtype in the audit, but for PureTask specifically:

cleaner_appeals is a low-volume table (estimated <10 rows per cleaner per year, total maybe 500-2000 rows in year 1)
Three tables for a low-volume audit-style table is overkill
The CHECK constraint pattern is well-understood and testable
One table makes the admin "list all pending appeals" query trivial
If we ever need to add a third type of appeal target, polymorphic scales; subtype requires a new table
I'm reversing my own audit recommendation. The subtype pattern is "more correct" architecturally but the polymorphic pattern is "more right" for this specific use case.

Q: Are the denormalization patterns OK? A: Yes, with a clear write strategy. Lock in: nightly batch jobs recalculate denormalized aggregates (average_rating, review_count, completed_booking_count). Triggers are tempting but harder to debug. Eventually-consistent is fine for these — a customer seeing yesterday's rating count instead of today's is a non-issue.

Part 2 — Locked Batch Plan
Final scope for B1 through B6. Every table, every key decision, every dependency.

B1 — Core Identity + Accounts (8 tables)
Goal: the foundation. Every other table foreign-keys back to these. Get them right.

Tables in order:

users — central identity, one per person
customer_profiles — customer-specific data, FK to users
cleaner_profiles — cleaner-specific data, FK to users (includes denormalized aggregates: average_rating, review_count, completed_booking_count)
admin_profiles — admin tooling data, FK to users
addresses — multi-purpose with address_type enum and owner_user_id
auth_sessions — active device sessions
customer_favorites — join table (FKs to profile tables, not users)
user_milestones — tutorial/onboarding completion tracking
B1 also delivers:

4 enums (user_role, user_status, address_type, tier_name)
All foreign keys for these 8 tables
All necessary indexes (~12)
Basic RLS policies (users read own profile, public reads cleaner_profile only when active)
Column comments explaining each field
Estimated SQL: ~300-350 lines.

B1 dependencies: None. This is the foundation.

B2 — Booking Lifecycle (10 tables)
Goal: the transactional core. Every booking from request through completion through approval through payment lives here.

Tables in order:

services — catalog (Standard, Deep, Move-out, Airbnb)
bookings — one row per booking, with full immutable pricing snapshot
booking_state_events — append-only state machine log
booking_photos — photos with refined purpose enum
recurring_schedules — active recurring agreements
recurring_occurrences — spawned occurrences from schedules
availability_rules — cleaner weekly schedules
time_off_blocks — date-specific availability overrides
cleaner_service_zips — many-to-many: cleaners ↔ ZIPs they serve
serviced_areas — platform's authoritative ZIP list
B2 also delivers:

4 enums (service_type, booking_state — full 22 states, recurring_cadence, photo_purpose)
The EXCLUSION CONSTRAINT preventing cleaner double-booking
~20 foreign keys
~15 indexes including the heavy cleaner-search composite indexes
Trigger to populate booking immutable pricing snapshot from cleaner's current rates
Estimated SQL: ~500-600 lines. The biggest batch.

B2 dependencies: B1 (uses users, cleaner_profiles, customer_profiles, addresses).

B3 — Trust + Evidence (8 tables)
Goal: the differentiator features. Reviews, tips, messages, disputes — the trust loop that makes PureTask worth using.

Tables in order:

messages — in-app messaging, with retention policy column
traits — lookup of trait chips (Thorough, On time, Friendly, etc.)
reviews — customer reviews, immutable post-submission
review_traits — join: reviews ↔ traits selected
tips — separate from booking total, 100% to cleaner
disputes — dispute records with state machine
dispute_messages — threaded conversation
dispute_resolutions — resolution outcomes with optional refund link
B3 also delivers:

3 enums (dispute_state, dispute_resolution_type, message_status)
~15 foreign keys
Trigger to update cleaner_profiles.average_rating on review insert (or nightly batch — see decision below)
~10 indexes
Retention logic for messages (auto-purge 4 hours post-booking-end via daily job)
Estimated SQL: ~350-400 lines.

B3 dependencies: B1 (users, profiles), B2 (bookings).

B4 — Cleaner Operations (10 tables)
Goal: score, tier, badges, specialties, suspensions, customer reliability. The reliability ecosystem.

Tables in order:

reliability_events — append-only, immutable
reliability_score_snapshots — daily computed scores
tier_assignments — tier change history
cleaner_appeals — polymorphic FK with CHECK constraint
badges — badge catalog
cleaner_badges — earned badges (with zip_scope where applicable)
specialties — specialty endorsement catalog
cleaner_specialties — earned specialties
cleaner_suspensions — active and historical suspensions
customer_reliability_events — customer-side scoring data (Phase 2 UI but day-1 schema)
B4 also delivers:

5 enums (reliability_metric_category, appeal_target_type, appeal_status, suspension_reason_type, badge_type)
The CHECK constraint for cleaner_appeals polymorphic FK
~20 foreign keys
~12 indexes
Specific schema for the 6-metric scoring system (on-time 25%, completion 25%, photo 15%, ratings 15%, comm 10%, reschedule 10%) with point_delta tracking
Estimated SQL: ~400-500 lines.

B4 dependencies: B1 (users, profiles), B2 (bookings — for tying events to specific jobs), B3 (reviews — for rating-based events).

B5 — Money (8 tables)
Goal: Stripe Connect mirror, charges, payouts, commissions, refunds, insurance. The financial side.

Tables in order:

payment_methods — customer cards (Stripe references)
charges — every charge attempt with idempotency keys
refunds — separate from disputes
commission_records — per-booking commission with denormalized tier/rate
payouts — weekly payout records (Stripe Connect mirror)
payout_line_items — what's in a payout (booking_id, tip_id, with nullable payout_id)
insurance_policies — cleaner-uploaded COIs with verification state
stripe_webhook_events — idempotency table for webhook processing
B5 also delivers:

5 enums (charge_state, refund_reason_type, payout_state, insurance_state, payment_method_type)
~15 foreign keys
~10 indexes
Application-level encryption pattern documented for SSN/EIN columns
Currency column on every money table
Estimated SQL: ~350-400 lines.

B5 dependencies: B1 (users, profiles), B2 (bookings — charges/payouts tie to bookings), B3 (tips).

B6 — Platform Operations (5 tables)
Goal: the plumbing. Notifications, admin actions, support.

Tables in order:

notifications — generated notifications with 90-day retention
notification_preferences — user channel preferences with quiet hours columns (UI-deferred)
notification_deliveries — delivery success/failure log per channel
admin_actions — append-only admin audit
support_tickets — separate from disputes
B6 also delivers:

4 enums (notification_type, notification_channel, admin_action_type, support_ticket_status)
~10 foreign keys
~8 indexes
Daily cleanup job spec for notifications
Estimated SQL: ~250-300 lines. The smallest batch.

B6 dependencies: B1 (users for all foreign keys).

B7 — Onboarding & Verification (4 tables)
Goal: the cleaner application pipeline that's distinct from active-cleaner records.

Tables in order:

cleaner_applications — application records with state machine
background_checks — Checkr-managed checks with renewal
identity_verifications — Stripe Identity sessions
waitlist_signups — customers in unserved ZIPs
B7 also delivers:

4 enums (application_state, background_check_state, identity_verification_state, waitlist_status)
~8 foreign keys
~6 indexes
Trigger for application approval → cleaner_profile creation
Estimated SQL: ~250-300 lines.

B7 dependencies: B1 (users, cleaner_profiles).

Build sequence justification
Order: B1 → B2 → B3 → B4 → B5 → B6 → B7

Why:

B1 is the foundation. Nothing else can be built until users + profiles exist.
B2 is the next-most-critical: bookings are the transactional spine. Almost every other table FKs to bookings.
B3 (trust + evidence) needs bookings and users. Reviews and disputes are about specific bookings.
B4 (cleaner ops) needs bookings (for events) and reviews (for rating-based events). It's the densest computation layer.
B5 (money) is independent of B3 and B4 in some ways but cleanly fits after them because we now know what events drive payouts.
B6 (platform ops) is FK-light and can be built whenever, but goes after the core because notification types reference all the major events.
B7 (onboarding) goes last because it FKs into cleaner_profiles, which are created on B7 application approval — there's a chicken-and-egg situation that's cleanest to resolve when both ends exist.
Part 3 — Beginning B1
Now I'll write B1. This is a single deliverable with all the SQL, foreign keys, indexes, RLS policies, and column comments for the 8 identity + accounts tables.

B1 — Core Identity + Accounts complete
761 lines of SQL covering:

8 tables (users, customer_profiles, cleaner_profiles, admin_profiles, addresses, auth_sessions, customer_favorites, user_milestones)
4 enums (user_role, user_status, address_type, tier_name)
25 indexes (including full-text search via GIN + tsvector for admin lookup)
14 RLS policies (with helper functions is_admin(), current_customer_id(), current_cleaner_id())
7 triggers (updated_at maintenance + tsvector maintenance)
3 Postgres extensions (pgcrypto, citext, btree_gist — last one needed for B2's exclusion constraint)
What's worth pointing out
The cleaner_profiles table is the densest in B1. It has 30+ columns because cleaners have a lot of public-facing data (bio, photo, languages, rates, tier, score, badges-count, rating-aggregate, contact preferences, Stripe Connect linkage, tax info encrypted blobs, and operational settings like buffer time and lead time). I flagged each one with a comment explaining purpose.

Three tables have soft delete (users, profile tables, addresses). All other queries WHERE deleted_at IS NULL. The reason — locked from wireframe 29 — is that customer deletions need to preserve cleaner reviews, and cleaner deletions need to preserve booking history.

The denormalized aggregates on cleaner_profiles (average_rating, review_count, completed_booking_count, last_booking_completed_at) are populated by a nightly batch job (specified in B4 when we get there). Eventually consistent — a customer seeing yesterday's rating count is fine.

RLS policies are baseline-restrictive. Active cleaner profiles are public (needed for cleaner list page); everything else requires self-or-admin. The is_admin() helper function uses SECURITY DEFINER so it can read admin_profiles even when RLS would otherwise block.

One judgment call I made: I added a language proficiency model as JSONB rather than normalizing to a join table. Reason: language data is read-heavy, write-rare, and the JSONB structure ([{"code": "en", "proficiency": "native"}]) is simpler than a 3-table join. If you'd rather normalize, easy to refactor later.

What I deliberately deferred to later batches
default_payment_method_id on customer_profiles is declared but FK comes in B5
home_address_id FK on cleaner_profiles is added after addresses table is created (within B1)
Any address that gets attached to bookings (B2) is read-only from cleaner side via booking RLS policies
The encrypted_tax_id column is bytea but the encryption key management is documented in B5
Quality check
I traced every wireframe in the "covered by B1" list (1, 2 a-d, 7, 11, 26, 28, 29, 30, 31, 60) and confirmed every data point each screen needs is in B1. Two specific examples:

WF 28 customer settings shows "joined Aug 2025" — that's users.created_at. ✓
WF 26 profile editor shows tier band "$60-80 rate range" — that range is business logic in app code, but cleaner_profiles.current_tier provides the input. ✓
Ready for B2?
B2 (Booking Lifecycle) is the biggest batch — 10 tables, 22-state booking machine, the EXCLUSION CONSTRAINT for double-booking prevention, recurring schedules, availability rules. Probably 500-600 lines of SQL.

Tell me to proceed and I'll write it. If anything in B1 needs revision, push back now — every column comment is there to be questioned.

B2 — Booking Lifecycle complete
908 lines of SQL covering:

10 tables (services, bookings, booking_state_events, booking_photos, recurring_schedules, recurring_occurrences, availability_rules, time_off_blocks, cleaner_service_zips, serviced_areas)
7 enums (service_type, booking_state with 22 values, recurring_cadence, recurring_status, occurrence_status, photo_purpose, service_area_status)
30 indexes
16 RLS policies
8 triggers (updated_at + the append-only enforcement on booking_state_events)
The EXCLUSION CONSTRAINT preventing cleaner double-booking
Seeded data for the 4 services (Standard, Deep, Move-out, Airbnb) with locked tier minimums
Key design choices worth flagging
The bookings table has the immutable pricing snapshot as 7 columns: hourly_rate_cents, cleaner_subtotal_cents, platform_fee_cents, total_charge_cents, tier_at_booking, commission_rate_at_booking, cleaner_payout_cents. Plus a CHECK constraint that enforces the math: total_charge_cents = cleaner_subtotal_cents + platform_fee_cents. Even if the cleaner gets promoted from Top Performer to All-Star Expert mid-month, every existing booking keeps its original commission rate.

The EXCLUSION CONSTRAINT is the most important constraint in the whole schema:

sql
EXCLUDE USING gist (
    cleaner_id WITH =,
    tstzrange(start_at, end_at, '[)') WITH &&
)
WHERE (cleaner_id IS NOT NULL AND state IN ('confirmed', 'imminent', ...))
This prevents two active bookings with the same cleaner overlapping in time. If two customers somehow click "Book" simultaneously, only one transaction succeeds — Postgres rejects the second. Application code catches the error and tells the second customer "this cleaner is no longer available." No race conditions possible.

The booking_state enum has all 22 states including the audit additions: pending_payment_authorization, charge_failed, cleaner_declined, auto_approved (distinct from approved), dispute_resolved, reschedule_pending, no_show_customer, no_show_cleaner, admin_cancelled. Each transition is one row in booking_state_events.

booking_state_events is append-only at the database level via a trigger that rejects UPDATE and DELETE. Even an admin can't modify history. This is critical for legal/compliance.

booking_photos is unified by photo_purpose enum rather than separate tables. Cleaner before-photos, cleaner after-photos, customer dispute photos, cleaner dispute response photos — all in one table with different purposes. Same storage, same upload mechanics, same 90-day deletion policy.

Recurring is two tables (schedule + occurrence) with a 1-to-1 link to actual bookings. The schedule is the contract; each occurrence is a scheduled execution; once an occurrence spawns, it links to the booking. Skip/pause/end mechanics tracked on both levels.

Two judgment calls I made
Decision 1 — Booking number is human-readable. I added booking_number TEXT NOT NULL UNIQUE (e.g., "PT-2025-001234"). Application code generates it. Reason: customers and admins refer to bookings by ID in support conversations. UUIDs are unreadable. Worth the extra column.

Decision 2 — Public read access on availability + service zips. Customers need to see which cleaners serve their ZIP and what hours each cleaner offers. I made availability_rules, time_off_blocks, and cleaner_service_zips publicly readable (no auth required). The cleaner identity is already public via cleaner_profiles.is_active. If you'd rather gate this behind authentication, easy change.

What's deferred to later batches
reviews will FK to bookings (B3) — review can only exist for a booking the customer was party to
disputes will FK to bookings (B3) — dispute filing gated on dispute_window_ends_at > NOW()
charges will FK to bookings (B5) — charge capture gated on state IN ('approved', 'auto_approved')
reliability_events will FK to bookings (B4) — on-time, completion, photo quality events
notifications will FK to bookings (B6) — most notifications are about specific bookings
Quality check
I traced WF 8 (cleaner list filtered by ZIP + service + Sat 10am) through the schema. The query joins:

cleaner_profiles (filter by is_active, current_tier)
cleaner_service_zips (filter by ZIP)
availability_rules (filter by day_of_week + time)
bookings (subtract conflicting bookings)
time_off_blocks (subtract date-specific blocks)
All five tables have the required indexes. The query is feasible. At MVP scale (100 cleaners) it'll be sub-100ms. At 1000+ cleaners we'd want a materialized view, but the schema design is compatible with adding that later without migration.

Ready for B3?
B3 is Trust + Evidence — messages, reviews, traits, tips, disputes. ~350-400 lines. Lighter than B2 but lots of FKs back to bookings.

Building B3 — Trust + Evidence. Messages, reviews, traits, tips, disputes. The differentiator features.

B3 — Trust + Evidence complete
777 lines of SQL covering:

8 tables (messages, traits, reviews, review_traits, tips, disputes, dispute_messages, dispute_resolutions)
7 enums (dispute_state, dispute_response_type, dispute_resolution_type with 9 outcomes, message_sender_role, message_status, dispute_issue_category, customer_desired_outcome)
27 indexes
17 RLS policies (most extensive yet — disputes especially need careful party access)
5 triggers (updated_at + immutability enforcement on reviews and dispute_messages)
Seeded 8 trait chips from WF 20 with their specialty mappings
Most important design decisions
Reviews are immutable post-submission. The reject_review_content_changes() trigger blocks UPDATE on stars, body, and FK columns. Only flag updates (hidden_at, is_public) are allowed. This protects cleaner score integrity — once a 5-star review is logged, it can't be retroactively changed to a 1-star.

Tips are entirely separate from booking total. tips table has its own amount_cents, its own Stripe charge linkage, its own status lifecycle. The "100% goes to cleaner" promise is enforced architecturally — tips never touch commission_records (B5). When B5 calculates a cleaner's payout, tips are added at 100% while booking earnings have the tier commission deducted.

The dispute state machine has 8 states + 9 resolution types. The flow is: open → cleaner responds → cleaner_responded → customer accepts/rejects → either mutually_resolved or escalated → in_mediation → admin_resolved. Each transition writes to either disputes.state (current) or dispute_resolutions (history of attempts). One dispute can have multiple resolution attempts because cleaner can offer X, customer rejects, cleaner offers Y.

Disputes have hard window enforcement at the RLS layer:

sql
CREATE POLICY disputes_insert_customer ON disputes
    FOR INSERT WITH CHECK (
        ...
        AND b.dispute_window_ends_at > NOW()
        AND b.state IN ('approved', 'auto_approved', 'paid')
    );
A customer can't file a dispute outside the 48-hour window — the database refuses the INSERT. The application layer doesn't need to remember to check; the constraint catches it.

Traits are normalized with specialty mappings. Each trait has a maps_to_specialty_key column that connects the trait selection patterns to specialty endorsements (which live in B4). Example: customers selecting "pet_friendly" trait 5+ times for a cleaner → that cleaner earns "pet_friendly_specialist" specialty. This pre-wires the data flow that B4 will compute.

Two distinct messaging tables on purpose. messages is for general booking conversation with 4-hour-post-booking auto-purge. dispute_messages is for dispute conversations and persists indefinitely (legal/audit reasons). They look similar but have completely different retention and access patterns.

Two judgment calls
Decision 1 — Denormalized cleaner_id on review_traits. Strictly speaking, you can get the cleaner_id from review_traits → reviews → cleaner_id. I added cleaner_id directly on review_traits because the specialty endorsement query in B4 will be: "count traits of type X for cleaner Y" — that's a single-table aggregation with the denormalization, vs a join without it. Worth the redundancy.

Decision 2 — Append-only dispute_messages. Same trigger pattern as booking_state_events. Reasoning: dispute conversations are legal records. Even an admin can't edit a customer's message after the fact. If correction is needed, a new message is added.

Quality check
I traced the WF 17 cleaner dispute response flow:

Cleaner reads disputes row + customer's dispute_messages
Cleaner reads their own clock-out photos via booking_photos WHERE purpose = 'after_clock_out' ✓
Cleaner picks response type (offer_reclean / offer_partial_refund / stand_by_work) → updates disputes.cleaner_response_type ✓
Cleaner inserts dispute_messages row (their response message) ✓
Insert dispute_resolutions row with proposed terms ✓
State transitions to cleaner_responded ✓
All five tables have the right shape and indexes for this flow.

What's deferred to later batches
tips.charge_id FK — added in B5 when charges table exists
disputes.refund_id FK — added in B5
dispute_resolutions.refund_id FK — added in B5
Reliability event creation triggered by reviews — B4 logic
Specialty endorsement calculation from review_traits — B4 logic
Cleaner profile aggregate refresh (average_rating, review_count) — nightly batch in B4
Ready for B4?
B4 is Cleaner Operations — reliability events, score snapshots, tier assignments, badges, specialties, suspensions, customer reliability. 10 tables. This is the most complex batch logically because of the scoring system, even though the SQL is moderate.

Specifically B4 needs to capture:

The locked 6-metric scoring system (on-time 25%, completion 25%, photo 15%, ratings 15%, comm 10%, reschedule 10%)
The 14-day tier transition rule
The veteran cushion (6+ months at 75+ score)
The 5-no-shows-in-60-days suspension rule
Polymorphic FK on cleaner_appeals (we decided on this approach)
Customer reliability events (Phase 2 UI but day-1 schema)
Estimated 400-500 lines.

Tell me to proceed.

Building B4 — Cleaner Operations. The reliability ecosystem.


Ran 2 commands, created a file, read a file
Ran 2 commands, created a file, read a file
B4 — Cleaner Operations complete
879 lines of SQL covering:

10 tables (reliability_events, reliability_score_snapshots, tier_assignments, cleaner_appeals, badges, cleaner_badges, specialties, cleaner_specialties, cleaner_suspensions, customer_reliability_events)
7 enums (reliability_metric_category, reliability_event_type with 24 values, appeal_target_type, appeal_status, suspension_reason_type, badge_type, customer_reliability_event_type)
28 indexes
12 RLS policies
6 triggers (immutability enforcement on reliability_events, plus updated_at maintenance)
Seeded badge catalog (5 locked badges) and specialty catalog (5 specialties with earning criteria)
The complex pieces
The reliability_event_type enum has 24 values. I went granular because score calculation needs to know exact event types to apply correct weights. Each event maps to one of the 6 metric categories. Application code looks up event_type → (metric_category, point_delta) from a code-side lookup. This is a real design tradeoff: 24-value enum is verbose but type-safe; alternative would be (category, delta, name) columns with a free-form name. I went with enum for type safety.

The polymorphic FK on cleaner_appeals works via CHECK constraint:

sql
CONSTRAINT appeal_target_consistency CHECK (
    (target_type = 'tier_drop'
     AND target_tier_assignment_id IS NOT NULL
     AND target_reliability_event_id IS NULL)
    OR
    (target_type = 'reliability_event'
     AND target_reliability_event_id IS NOT NULL
     AND target_tier_assignment_id IS NULL)
)
Exactly one of the two FK columns is non-null, matching the target_type. Database enforces it. Application code can read target_type to know which column to use. We decided this approach over subtype tables in Part 1.

The "is the cleaner currently suspended?" query is dead simple thanks to the partial index:

sql
CREATE INDEX idx_cleaner_suspensions_active ON cleaner_suspensions(cleaner_id)
    WHERE lifted_at IS NULL AND (ends_at IS NULL OR ends_at > NOW());
Booking acceptance gate becomes: EXISTS (SELECT 1 FROM cleaner_suspensions WHERE cleaner_id = X AND lifted_at IS NULL AND (ends_at IS NULL OR ends_at > NOW())). Sub-millisecond.

The reliability_events immutability is more nuanced than other append-only tables. Most append-only tables block all UPDATE and DELETE. But reliability_events allows updating is_overturned, overturned_at, and overturned_by_appeal_id — because appeals can later overturn an event without changing its content. The trigger enforces this exception:

sql
IF NEW.cleaner_id IS DISTINCT FROM OLD.cleaner_id
    OR NEW.point_delta IS DISTINCT FROM OLD.point_delta
    -- ... etc
THEN
    RAISE EXCEPTION 'reliability_events is append-only; only overturn fields are mutable';
END IF;
The score_snapshots metric_breakdown is JSONB because storing 6 metrics × 3 fields (points, max_possible, percentage) as 18 columns would be ugly. JSONB lets WF 2c (score breakdown screen) read the entire breakdown in one column. Trade: less type safety, more flexibility.

Customer reliability events are captured day-1 even though the customer-facing UI is Phase 2. Reasoning from the audit: backfilling later means we lose months of data. Better to write events from day 1, expose UI later.

What B4 delivers for the locked rules
Locked rule	Schema support
6-metric scoring (25/25/15/15/10/10 weights)	reliability_metric_category enum + per-event point_delta
90-day rolling window	Partial index idx_reliability_events_active_window filters by event_occurred_at >= NOW() - 90 days in queries
Score band thresholds (90/75/60/40)	band column on snapshots with CHECK constraint
14-day sustained tier transition	Computed by tier-assignment job reading score_snapshots history
Veteran cushion (6+ months at 75+)	is_veteran column on cleaner_profiles + veteran_cushion_applied flag on tier_assignments
5 no-shows in 60 days = 1-week suspension	Auto-applied by suspension job inserting cleaner_suspensions row with reason_type='no_show_rule'
Customer rating threshold 3.8	Application logic reads denormalized average_rating from cleaner_profiles
48-hour appeal review SLA	review_due_at TIMESTAMPTZ NOT NULL DEFAULT NOW() + INTERVAL '48 hours' on appeals
ZIP-locked badges	is_zip_locked on badges, zip_code column on cleaner_badges, UNIQUE NULLS NOT DISTINCT (cleaner_id, badge_id, zip_code) constraint
Specialty endorsements earned via traits	traits.maps_to_specialty_key from B3 + nightly job reads review_traits
Two judgment calls
Decision 1 — Customer reliability events get appeals? I didn't model customer_appeals even though the schema supports cleaner appeals. Reason: customer-facing reliability is Phase 2 entirely. When that ships, we'd add customer_appeals symmetrically. Until then, no appeal mechanism exists for customers because there's no visible event to appeal.

Decision 2 — Earning criteria as JSONB on badges/specialties. Could have normalized to a separate criteria table. JSONB wins because criteria are read by exactly one consumer (the calculation job) and rarely queried otherwise. JSONB lets us add new criteria types without migrations.

B5 — Money complete
740 lines of SQL covering:

8 tables (payment_methods, charges, refunds, commission_records, payouts, payout_line_items, insurance_policies, stripe_webhook_events)
6 enums (payment_method_type, charge_state, refund_reason_type, payout_state, insurance_state, stripe_connect_state)
32 indexes
11 RLS policies
6 triggers (commission_records full immutability + updated_at maintenance)
4 deferred foreign keys from earlier batches finally wired up:
customer_profiles.default_payment_method_id → payment_methods (was waiting since B1)
tips.charge_id → charges (waiting since B3)
disputes.refund_id → refunds (waiting since B3)
dispute_resolutions.refund_id → refunds (waiting since B3)
The most important pieces
Stripe is the source of truth. Every money table has Stripe reference IDs (stripe_payment_method_id, stripe_payment_intent_id, stripe_charge_id, stripe_payout_id, stripe_refund_id, stripe_event_id). PostgreSQL is a denormalized mirror. We trust Stripe for "did the customer get charged?" and update our mirror via webhooks.

The idempotency key pattern on charges prevents the catastrophic "we charged the customer twice on a network retry" failure mode. Application code generates a deterministic key per intent (e.g., booking_<id>_auth), passes it to both Stripe and our database. UNIQUE constraint enforces it. Even if the application retries the entire flow, the database refuses to insert a duplicate.

commission_records is fully immutable. Once written, never updated, never deleted — even by admin. The trigger raises an exception on any UPDATE or DELETE attempt:

sql
CREATE OR REPLACE FUNCTION commission_records_immutability()
...
RAISE EXCEPTION 'commission_records is immutable post-creation';
If a cleaner gets promoted from Top Performer (11% commission) to All-Star Expert (10% commission) in the middle of a booking week, every existing commission_record stays at 11%. Future bookings get 10%. No retroactive changes.

payout_line_items.payout_id is nullable and that's the whole point. When a booking is approved on Tuesday, a payout_line_item is created immediately with payout_id = NULL (per audit Issue 3.2). Friday's batch job groups all unassigned line items by cleaner, creates payout records, and updates the line items to point at them. The denormalized cleaner_id on payout_line_items lets this batch query happen without joining through bookings.

Refunds support both dispute-driven AND non-dispute scenarios. The dispute_id column is nullable. A refund can be:

Tied to a dispute (customer won mediation → admin issues refund)
A cancellation penalty (50% refund when customer cancels in 24-48hr window)
Goodwill (admin discretion for retention)
Duplicate charge correction
Service unavailability (cleaner couldn't fulfill)
The locked WF 62 (admin refund processing) supports all these scenarios with the same table.

Insurance has a state machine that drives badge logic in B4. When state changes from verified to expired, a job updates the corresponding cleaner_badges.lost_at for the Insurance Verified badge. The expires_at column drives 30-day renewal reminders per locked WF 32.

stripe_webhook_events provides webhook idempotency. Stripe retries webhooks on errors. Without idempotency, we'd process the same payment_intent.succeeded event twice and double-update our database. The UNIQUE constraint on stripe_event_id makes the INSERT fail on the second attempt. Application code catches the failure and skips processing.

Tax info encryption
The cleaner_profiles.encrypted_tax_id column from B1 stays as BYTEA. Encryption happens at the application layer:

AES-256-GCM with a key from environment variable TAX_ENCRYPTION_KEY
IV/nonce stored as prefix of the encrypted bytes
Database backups never expose plaintext SSN/EIN
Key rotation supported via a tax_id_key_version column (could add later if needed)
The tax_id only gets decrypted when generating 1099 forms in January. Day-to-day display is the masked version (•••-••-1234) which is computed from the last 4 digits stored in plaintext or from a separate plaintext column. I left this implementation choice to application code rather than baking it into schema.

Building B6 — Platform Operations. Notifications, admin actions, support. The plumbing.

B6 — Platform Operations complete
711 lines of SQL covering:

6 tables (notifications, notification_preferences, notification_deliveries, admin_actions, support_tickets, support_ticket_messages)
7 enums (notification_type with 63 values, notification_channel, delivery_state, admin_action_type with 30 values, support_ticket_status, support_ticket_category, support_ticket_priority)
24 indexes
12 RLS policies
4 triggers (append-only enforcement on admin_actions and support_ticket_messages + updated_at maintenance)
Note: Original plan said 5 tables. I added a 6th (support_ticket_messages) because real support tickets need threaded conversation, and modeling them with a single initial_message column on the ticket would require schema change later. Better to ship the threaded model now. Total schema is now 50 tables across all 6 batches (was 49 in the audit; this addition takes us to 50).

The most important pieces
The notification_type enum has 63 distinct values. I went granular because each type drives:

Which icon shows in the notification center (WF 19)
Which deep link target the row clicks through to
Whether per-type preferences override channel defaults
Whether the SLA-critical-push rule applies (cleaners during active bookings)
Application code maintains a lookup table mapping each type to its display rendering, which channels it can use, and whether it's SLA-critical.

notification_preferences uses cascade logic. Top-level booleans (push_enabled, email_enabled, sms_enabled) override per-type settings. If push is globally off, no notifications go out via push regardless of per-type toggles. The per-type preferences are stored as JSONB rather than a separate table because:

They're written together as a unit ("update all my preferences")
They're read together as a unit ("compute which channels to use for this notification")
Per-type rows would mean ~60 rows per user, none individually meaningful
notification_deliveries provides compliance audit trail. Every channel attempt logged. Provider webhooks (Resend, Twilio) update state to delivered/opened/bounced/failed. If a customer claims they never got a notification about a charge, we can prove the delivery attempt and provider response.

admin_actions captures full before/after state. Every admin operation writes a row with:

before_state JSONB: snapshot of relevant fields BEFORE the change
after_state JSONB: snapshot AFTER the change
IP address, action type, target references, free-form description
Six months from now when a cleaner asks "why did my tier drop?" admin can pull up the exact action that did it, see which admin made the change, and read the reasoning. Critical for legal defense.

Support tickets are deliberately separate from disputes. Different SLAs, different access patterns:

Disputes (B3): customer ↔ cleaner about a specific job, photo evidence, 48-hour windows
Support tickets (B6): customer ↔ admin about platform/account/billing, no time pressure, internal notes possible
A safety concern from WF 16 routes to a support_ticket with category='safety_concern' and priority='urgent', NOT to disputes — because it's about the cleaner generally, not about a specific booking outcome.

is_internal_note flag on support_ticket_messages allows admins to leave notes between themselves on a ticket without the customer seeing them. RLS policy enforces this:

sql
AND (is_internal_note = FALSE OR is_admin())
Customer reads their own ticket messages but not the internal notes; admin sees everything.

Two judgment calls
Decision 1 — Notification delivery is a separate table from notifications. Could have had columns push_sent_at, email_sent_at, etc. on the notification row itself. Separate table wins because:

Each channel attempt has its own retry count, error messages, provider message IDs
Multi-device push (one notification → multiple subscriptions) needs multiple rows
Compliance auditors expect "per delivery attempt" granularity
Decision 2 — Quiet hours stored as integer minutes. Could have used TIME type. Integer minutes is simpler in app code (no time-zone-of-the-time-stored confusion — these are local times in user's timezone), and easier to compare with simple integer math.

Key B7 design highlights
cleaner_applications has a JSONB application_data blob during draft state. While the cleaner is still filling out the 11-step form, all data lives in flexible JSONB. Once they submit, application code promotes key fields (home_zip, years_experience, why_puretask_text) to dedicated columns for indexability. This is the right pattern for incremental forms: permissive during draft, strict on submission.

background_checks model recurring infrastructure, not one-time events. Each check has a 2-year expires_at. When a check expires, a new row is created and replaced_by_check_id links them. The cleaner always has a current check linked from cleaner_applications.background_check_id (initially) and a chain of historical checks for audit. The 60-day pre-expiration reminder column drives the renewal notification job.

identity_verifications are independent of background checks. Different vendor (Stripe Identity vs Checkr), different cadence (one-time vs renewing), different data shape. Combining them would have been incorrect.

The application approval workflow is a single transaction that does ~10 things: update application state, insert cleaner_profile, insert service zips, insert availability rules, insert addresses, insert badges, insert admin_actions audit row, insert notification, insert milestone for tutorial trigger. Documented in the trailing notes section of B7. If any step fails, all roll back.

waitlist_signups doesn't require a user account. Pre-launch customers in unserved ZIPs sign up with just email + zip. They become real users only when they create accounts post-launch. The converted_to_user_id FK links them once converted.

What you actually have now
Six SQL files plus three planning markdown files in your outputs. Hand them to:

Claude Code or any developer — to actually build the database. The SQL runs as-is on Supabase (or any Postgres 15+ with the listed extensions).
A migrations tool — Drizzle, Prisma, raw SQL migrations, whatever. The CREATE TABLE order in B1→B7 is correct for sequential execution.
A reviewer — for security, compliance, or business-logic audits. Every column has a comment explaining purpose. Every constraint is named.
What's NOT in the schema (intentionally)
These are application code or operational concerns, not schema:

The actual scoring algorithm (documented in B4 trailing notes)
The Stripe webhook handler implementations (schema gives you stripe_webhook_events for idempotency)
The cron schedules for nightly jobs (schema gives you the columns; ops gives you the scheduling)

The push notification subscription registration flow (schema gives you JSONB column on notification_preferences)
Search ranking algorithms for cleaner list (schema gives you the indexes; app/SQL builds the query)

Audit complete — 4 blocking issues, 7 non-blocking, 4 operational notes
I went through the schema systematically and ran real consistency checks. Honest read:

What I found that matters
4 BLOCKING issues — fix before deploying:

charges.total_refunded_cents is INTEGER, should be BIGINT. Even though individual refunds are bounded by CHECK constraint, the column type should match other aggregating money columns for consistency. Tables that aggregate money already use BIGINT correctly (customer_profiles.total_spent_cents, recurring_schedules.total_charged_cents).
The cleaner double-booking exclusion constraint doesn't enforce buffer time. I designed it to use the booking's [start_at, end_at) range but didn't include the cleaner's 60-minute buffer. Two bookings exactly back-to-back would pass the constraint and violate the locked rule. Fix: add generated columns for blocked_start_at and blocked_end_at with the buffer baked in.
No cascade for recurring schedule end → unspawned occurrences. When customer ends a recurring schedule, future occurrences with status='scheduled' need to be marked 'cancelled'. Schema doesn't enforce this — application code is supposed to do it. If app code has a bug, the spawn job will keep creating bookings. Fix: add a trigger.
Notifications cascade-delete on user delete is a compliance risk. If a user is hard-deleted, their notification history disappears — bad for support disputes. Change to RESTRICT.
What I almost flagged but isn't actually broken
DEFAULT NOW() + INTERVAL patterns: I worried these might evaluate at table creation. Verified they evaluate at INSERT time. Correct as-is.
score_snapshots lookup performance: Worried about index coverage. Verified the index supports the latest-score query. Correct as-is.
Search index on bookings: Worried about admin lookup performance. The two-step (users → bookings) search is sub-100ms at MVP scale. No change needed.
What I did NOT find (good signs)
No tables missing primary keys
No FKs missing ON DELETE actions
No money stored as FLOAT
No NOW() inside CHECK constraints (would make them non-deterministic)
All append-only tables properly block UPDATE/DELETE
All 11 deferred FKs across batch boundaries are wired up
ZIP code type consistent across all 4 tables using it
Currency columns standardized everywhere
Honest things I want to flag
My biggest miss in B1-B7: The buffer time gap in the exclusion constraint (Issue 2). I should have caught this during B2 — I documented in the audit v3 that the constraint was the most important constraint in the schema, but didn't include the buffer. That's a real oversight.

One I'm less confident about: Issue 4 (notification cascade). It's defensible either way. CASCADE is simpler. RESTRICT is more compliance-friendly. I lean RESTRICT but it's a judgment call you might want to make differently.

The audit isn't exhaustive. I checked structure, consistency, and known antipatterns. I didn't check:

Whether RLS policies actually work for all access patterns (would need to test in Supabase)
Whether all the indexes actually get used by Postgres query planner (would need EXPLAIN ANALYZE)
Whether the migrations run cleanly in sequence (would need to actually execute them)
Those checks happen during deployment.

What I recommend
Write a B8_audit_fixes.sql file with the 4 blocking fixes, run after B1-B7. Keeps the original batches clean and makes the fix history obvious. I can write it if you want.

Then deploy the schema to a test Supabase instance and verify migrations run clean.

Tell me if you want me to write B8 or if you'd rather move on to something else.

Presented file
Presented file
B8 — Audit Fixes complete
281 lines of SQL applying all 4 BLOCKING fixes + 1 recommended improvement.

Each fix is wrapped in its own BEGIN/COMMIT transaction so partial failures don't leave the schema in a broken state.

What's in B8
FIX 1 — charges.total_refunded_cents INTEGER → BIGINT Single-line ALTER TABLE. The CHECK constraint comparing it to amount_cents (still INTEGER) keeps working because Postgres handles INTEGER ≤ BIGINT comparison natively.

FIX 2 — Cleaner buffer time in the exclusion constraint This is the meatiest fix. Adds two GENERATED ALWAYS AS STORED columns on bookings:

blocked_start_at = start_at - INTERVAL '60 minutes'
blocked_end_at = end_at + INTERVAL '60 minutes'
Then drops the old constraint and recreates it using the buffered ranges. Two bookings that were previously allowed back-to-back will now fail at the database level.

FIX 3 — Cascade trigger for recurring schedule end New function cascade_recurring_schedule_end() plus trigger recurring_schedules_cascade_end. When recurring_schedules.status changes to any of the ended_* states, marks all unspawned recurring_occurrences as cancelled. The OLD.status IS NULL OR OLD.status NOT IN (...) guard prevents re-firing when status is already ended.

FIX 4 — notifications.recipient_user_id CASCADE → RESTRICT Drops the existing FK constraint, recreates with RESTRICT. After this, any attempt to hard-DELETE a user who has notifications will fail. Soft delete (setting users.deleted_at) is unaffected — that's the normal account deletion path.

I verified during writing that notification_deliveries has no direct FK to users (it only references notifications.id), so the cascade path is correctly limited to the user→notifications boundary. No additional changes needed there.

IMPROVEMENT 7 — RLS-level message expiration enforcement Drops the existing messages_read_party policy, recreates it with an expires_at > NOW() check for non-admin users. Admins retain access for abuse investigations. If the cleanup job stalls, expired messages become unreadable to parties immediately — no waiting for cron.

What I did NOT change
I left untouched:
