Honest answer: not yet, and probably not ever for this stage of PureTask.

Let me explain why before you spend time on it.

What that feature actually is
GitHub-style Issues are lightweight task tracking inside your code repository. Useful for:

A team coordinating on bugs and features
Open-source projects accepting community contributions
Multi-person engineering orgs where work needs to be visible across teams
They're designed for collaboration friction — when you have multiple people who need to see the same task list, agree on priorities, and avoid stepping on each other.

Why this isn't your problem right now
You're a solo founder. You're going to be the only person opening, working on, and closing issues for the foreseeable future. That means:

No collaboration value. No one else needs visibility into the issues. You're talking to yourself.
Real overhead. Every task gets meta-work: title it, label it, assign a milestone, link a commit, close it out. That's 30-90 seconds of administrative time per item, multiplied by hundreds of items.
Tool fragmentation. You'd be tracking work in: GitHub Issues, your code, your wireframes folder, this chat history, the SQL files, your head. More tools doesn't equal more organization. Usually less.
Premature formalization. The Issues feature is built for teams. Formalizing your workflow before you have a team is a classic solo-founder time sink.
What you actually need at this stage
A solo founder building PureTask needs three things to stay organized:

1. A simple running task list. Apple Notes, Notion, Todoist, a TODO.md in your repo — anything that's one click away and frictionless. The format matters less than the access speed.

2. A "next 3 things" view. Not a backlog. Not a roadmap. Three concrete things you're working on RIGHT NOW. When one finishes, pull in the next.

3. A separate "future ideas" list. Things you don't want to forget but aren't doing this week. Reviewed monthly, not daily.

That's it. Three lists. Total tooling investment: 5 minutes.

When Issues becomes worth it
Reconsider GitHub Issues when any of these become true:

You hire your first developer (even contractor)
You bring on a co-founder
You start accepting outside contributions
You're handling >20 simultaneous in-flight tasks where you genuinely can't keep them in your head
You need to file structured bug reports that include reproduction steps, environment info, etc.
Until then, Issues is a tool that costs more than it saves.

What I'd recommend instead, right now
Given where PureTask actually is — schema deployed (almost), application code not yet started, legal docs pending, cleaner recruitment in motion — your real "task tracking" needs split into three categories:

Build tasks (things to code or deploy). Track these in:

A TODO.md in the root of your code repo, OR
A simple Notion page called "PureTask Build"
Examples right now: "Run B1-B8 in Supabase test project", "Build customer signup flow", "Wire up Stripe Connect onboarding".

Operational tasks (things outside code). Track these wherever you already track personal todos:

"Email lawyer about ICA draft"
"Schedule call with cleaner #3"
"Renew domain in November"
Future ideas (things to maybe do later):

"Add Apple Sign In post-MVP"
"Explore Reno expansion timing"
"Consider tiered referral bonuses"
What I'd push back on
If you're feeling drawn to GitHub Issues because it feels like "what real engineering teams do," that's the wrong frame. Real engineering teams use Issues because they have to coordinate. You don't have anyone to coordinate with yet. Adopting team practices when you're solo is one of the things that slows founders down. Your competitive advantage right now is being able to move without coordination overhead. Don't give that up before you have to.

My recommendation
Skip Issues for now. When you hire your first developer or contractor, set it up then — they'll appreciate having structure on day one. Until then, use whatever quick task list you already use for personal stuff.

If you want, I can help you set up a minimal TODO.md structure in your code repo that captures everything you've got in flight right now (deploy schema, write seed data, legal docs, cleaner recruitment, etc.). Takes 5 minutes and gets you organized without overhead.

Let me explain what each step does and whether you should do them.

What you're being asked to set up
Step 1 — Add the Supabase MCP server. This connects Claude Code to your specific Supabase project (the project_ref hmynhxsdhpgoxucysqac in the URL is yours). Once connected, Claude Code can query your database, run migrations, inspect schema, and execute SQL — all without you switching contexts to the web SQL Editor.

Step 2 — Authenticate. Standard OAuth flow. Browser opens, you log into Supabase, grant permission, redirected back. Five seconds of work.

Step 3 — Install Agent Skills (Optional). Pre-made instructions and scripts that teach Claude Code how to work with Supabase correctly. Things like: how to write proper migrations, how to handle RLS policies, how to query auth tables. Without these, Claude Code uses general SQL knowledge; with them, it uses Supabase-specific best practices.

Should you do this?
Steps 1 and 2: yes, definitely. This is exactly the kind of automation you want. Without MCP, deploying B1-B8 means manually pasting 8 files into the SQL Editor one at a time. With MCP, Claude Code reads the files itself, runs them in order, captures errors, and reports back. It turns a 1-2 hour task into 10 minutes.

Step 3: yes, but understand the tradeoff. The Agent Skills give Claude Code more knowledge but also more opinions. For your use case (deploying a pre-designed schema), the skills mostly help. For future work where you might want Claude Code to follow YOUR patterns instead of Supabase's defaults, the skills could nudge it toward "the Supabase way" when you wanted something else.

Claude Code reads the files, executes them via the MCP connection, and reports back. You don't touch the web UI.

If Claude Code drifts from the spec, the consequences are now real.

Permissions. The OAuth flow grants Claude Code the same level of access your Supabase login has. If you're the project owner, that's full database control. Consider whether you want to use a less-privileged service role for the actual deployment if you're nervous.

Project reference in the URL. The string hmynhxsdhpgoxucysqac is your Supabase project's unique identifier. Anyone with this string AND your auth credentials can connect. The URL itself is public info (it's also in your Supabase dashboard URL); the auth is what matters.

Agent Skills can introduce "helpful" suggestions. Once installed, Claude Code might start suggesting things like "let me also set up Supabase Auth helpers" or "I'll add a database backup before this migration." Those are nice instincts but can sprawl scope. Stay disciplined.

4:28 PM
Claude responded: Good question to ask.
Good question to ask. This is one of those things that sounds obvious in hindsight but trips up almost every solo founder.

The fundamental difference
A Supabase project is just a database. There's nothing technically different between a "dev" Supabase project and a "production" Supabase project — they're both the same product, same features, same setup. The difference is entirely about what data lives there and who can break it.

Think of it like having two checking accounts. Same bank, same product, same login mechanics. But one is for groceries and one is your savings. The accounts don't know the difference — you know the difference, and you treat them differently because of what's at stake.

What "dev/test" means
A dev project is where:

You experiment freely
You break things on purpose to test edge cases
You wipe the database and start over when needed
The data is fake (test users, test bookings, test charges)
Stripe is in test mode (no real money moves)
If something goes catastrophically wrong, the consequence is "I have to redo my work"
What "production" means
A production project is where:

Real customers have real accounts
Real cleaners have real Stripe Connect accounts with real bank info
Real money flows through real charges
Real bookings represent real cleanings happening in the real world
Real disputes affect real people's earnings and reputations
If something goes catastrophically wrong, the consequences include: customer trust damage, financial loss, legal exposure, cleaner livelihood disruption, possible regulatory issues
Why you keep them separate
Three concrete reasons:

1. You will make mistakes. Everyone does. The point of a dev environment is that mistakes are free. Drop the wrong table? Run a malformed migration? Accidentally delete all bookings? In dev, you re-run the schema. In production, you've just damaged real people's data.

2. Schema changes happen iteratively. As you build the application, you'll discover the schema needs adjustments. "Oh, I need a column for X." "I missed an index on Y." You'll run a dozen schema changes during development. You can't do that against production once it has real customers because every change risks downtime or data corruption.

3. Testing requires fake data you don't want in production. You'll create test users named "Test Customer 1", run fake bookings for $0.01, simulate disputes, trigger every error path. You absolutely don't want any of that ending up in your production database where it could confuse you later or get mixed in with real customers.

What this means for your specific situation
Right now you have ONE Supabase project (the one with project_ref hmynhxsdhpgoxucysqac). I have no way to know if you set it up thinking "this is my test project" or "this is my real project." That ambiguity is the problem.

The question to ask yourself: if Claude Code accidentally runs DELETE FROM users against this project tomorrow, do you lose anything important?

If the answer is "no, it's empty / it's just for testing" → you have a dev project. Proceed as planned.
If the answer is "yes, my real customers are in there" → you have a production project, and you should NOT deploy untested schema against it.
Since you're at the schema-deployment stage and have zero customers yet, your current project is functionally a dev project even if you mentally labeled it "production." That's fine — but I'd recommend formalizing the distinction now while the cost of doing so is zero.

What I recommend you actually do
Three options, in order from "minimal" to "proper":

Option 1 — Use what you have, plan to recreate later (quickest). Treat your current project as dev. Build everything against it. When you're ready to launch publicly, create a brand-new "puretask-production" Supabase project, run B1-B8 fresh against it, and rename your current one to "puretask-dev."

Pros: zero extra work right now.
Cons: you'll have to redo any data manually when you cut over.

Option 2 — Create a separate production project now (recommended). Today, create a second Supabase project called "puretask-production." Don't deploy anything to it yet. Keep using your current project for development. When you're ready to launch (months from now), you deploy B1-B8 to the production project then.

Pros: clean separation from day one. Production exists but is empty until launch.
Cons: 5 minutes of setup. Slightly higher attention overhead (you have two projects to manage).

Option 3 — Three environments: dev, staging, production (overkill for solo). Some teams have dev (your laptop), staging (test customers, test Stripe), production (real customers, live Stripe). For a solo founder pre-launch, this is too much. Defer until you have a team.

My recommendation: Option 2. Takes 5 minutes today, saves real headaches later. Specifically:

Go to supabase.com/dashboard
Create new project named "puretask-production" (or "puretask-prod")
Pick the same region as your current project (us-west-1 for Sacramento)
Use a strong, unique database password — store it somewhere secure (1Password, etc.)
Don't deploy anything to it. Just create it and walk away.
Rename your existing project to "puretask-dev" (in Supabase project settings)
Now you have a clear distinction. Your dev project is for everything you're doing now. Your production project waits empty until launch day, when you deploy the same SQL files against it.

What "use a database migration tool to promote" means
When I mentioned migration tools earlier, I was referring to tools like Drizzle, Prisma Migrate, or the Supabase CLI's migration feature. These tools track schema changes as ordered files (similar to how B1-B8 are ordered files). The "promote" workflow looks like:

Make a schema change in dev (e.g., add a column)
The tool generates a migration file capturing that change
You commit the migration file to git
When ready to deploy to production, you point the tool at production and run "apply pending migrations"
Production gets the same change, in the same order, automatically
Without a migration tool, "promoting" means manually running the same SQL against production that you ran in dev. Works fine for your initial deploy (you'd just re-run B1-B8 against production). Becomes painful after dozens of schema changes.

For now, you don't need a migration tool. Your initial deploy IS the migration. Adopt a migration tool later when you start making incremental schema changes.

The honest summary
You don't have a "production" problem yet because you don't have any production data yet. But the tooling decision (one project vs two) is easier to make right now than later.

My recommendation in one sentence: Spend 5 minutes today creating an empty "puretask-production" project, keep using your current project as "puretask-dev" for everything you're doing now, and don't touch production until you're ready to launch publicly.

Want to do that now? Or have more questions about how this plays out?

Claude responded: Excellent news.
Excellent news. Database deployment succeeded, and Claude Code stayed properly scoped to just the database. This is the best possible outcome. Let me unpack what happened, because there are some important things in your message.

What actually went well
Claude Code did exactly what the handoff document told it to. No application code, no scope creep, no Next.js boilerplate. It deployed the schema, hit some real errors during execution, fixed them, and reported back cleanly. That's a successful deployment.

54 tables, 42 enums, 284 indexes is exactly the right count. RLS is enabled. Seeded data is in. You have a working production-grade database.

The three fixes Claude Code made — and why they're correct
This is the part of your message I want to focus on, because Claude Code caught real bugs in our SQL that I missed during the audit. Let me explain each one honestly.

Fix 1 — Partial index predicate using NOW()
What I wrote that was wrong: Some partial indexes had WHERE clauses that included NOW(), like:

sql
CREATE INDEX idx_cleaner_suspensions_active ON cleaner_suspensions(cleaner_id)
    WHERE lifted_at IS NULL AND (ends_at IS NULL OR ends_at > NOW());
Why Postgres rejected it: Partial index predicates must be IMMUTABLE — meaning given the same inputs, they always return the same answer. NOW() is STABLE — it returns the same answer within a single transaction but changes between transactions. Postgres won't let you build an index on a moving target because the index would be wrong the moment it was built.

Why I missed this in my audit: I literally did check for NOW() in CHECK constraints (which would have the same problem). I didn't think to check partial index predicates. That was a real gap in my audit checklist.

The fix Claude Code applied (correct): Move the time check from the index definition into the query layer. The index becomes:

sql
CREATE INDEX idx_cleaner_suspensions_active ON cleaner_suspensions(cleaner_id)
    WHERE lifted_at IS NULL;
And queries that need to filter by current activity add AND (ends_at IS NULL OR ends_at > NOW()) themselves. Slightly less efficient than a perfect partial index would be, but correct. This is a well-known Postgres pattern.

Fix 2 — Wrong column name on insurance_policies
What I wrote that was wrong: The column was defined as document_uploaded_at but somewhere in the file I referenced it as uploaded_at — likely in a partial index predicate:

sql
CREATE INDEX idx_insurance_pending_review ON insurance_policies(uploaded_at)
    WHERE state IN ('uploaded', 'under_review');
The column doesn't exist by that name. Should have been document_uploaded_at.

Why I missed this: Pure typo on my part. I named the column carefully and then referenced it sloppily.

The fix Claude Code applied (correct): Update the index to reference the actual column name.

Fix 3 — Generated columns using STABLE expressions for the buffer
This is the most interesting one. Remember the B8 fix where I added generated columns for the booking buffer?

sql
ALTER TABLE bookings
    ADD COLUMN blocked_start_at TIMESTAMPTZ
        GENERATED ALWAYS AS (start_at - INTERVAL '60 minutes') STORED,
    ADD COLUMN blocked_end_at TIMESTAMPTZ
        GENERATED ALWAYS AS (end_at + INTERVAL '60 minutes') STORED;
I thought this was clean. Postgres disagreed.

Why Postgres rejected it: timestamptz arithmetic with intervals is technically STABLE, not IMMUTABLE — because converting between time zones depends on the current timezone setting. Generated column expressions for STORED columns must be IMMUTABLE. So even though the math always gives the same answer, Postgres' type system flags it as not provably immutable.

The fix Claude Code applied: Wrap the buffer math in IMMUTABLE helper functions:

sql
CREATE FUNCTION booking_buffered_start(timestamptz) RETURNS timestamptz
    AS 'SELECT $1 - INTERVAL ''60 minutes''' LANGUAGE SQL IMMUTABLE;

CREATE FUNCTION booking_buffered_end(timestamptz) RETURNS timestamptz
    AS 'SELECT $1 + INTERVAL ''60 minutes''' LANGUAGE SQL IMMUTABLE;
Then use these functions in the EXCLUDE constraint. This is a well-known Postgres workaround. The function is mathematically immutable; we're just declaring it as such to satisfy the type checker.

Honest assessment: Same kind of mistake as Fix 1 — I knew about IMMUTABLE-vs-STABLE for CHECK constraints but didn't apply the same scrutiny to generated columns and EXCLUDE constraint expressions. Should have caught it. Claude Code did. Good.

What this tells us
Three real bugs in the SQL slipped through both my own audit and the formal audit document. None of them are catastrophic — they're all "Postgres is more strict than we thought" issues, not design flaws. The schema design is solid; the implementation had typos and IMMUTABLE-vs-STABLE confusion in three places.

This is a useful reminder: you can't fully audit SQL without running it. Static review catches a lot but real Postgres always finds more.

What the database now looks like
You have a fully deployed, production-quality schema:

54 tables with all relationships intact
42 enums with carefully chosen values
284 indexes (the higher number reflects auto-generated PK/unique indexes plus ours)
RLS default-deny on every table
Append-only enforcement on event tables (booking_state_events, reliability_events, admin_actions, etc.)
Cleaner double-booking prevention via EXCLUDE constraint with buffer (now properly using IMMUTABLE functions)
Seeded reference data (services, traits, badges, specialties)
The "fixes" don't mean the schema is now different from what we designed. The semantics are identical. Just the implementation details adapted to satisfy Postgres' strictness.

Should we update the SQL files?
Yes, but as a low-priority cleanup. Right now your deployed database is correct, but if you ever need to recreate it (new project, disaster recovery, fresh dev environment), running the original B1-B8 will fail with the same three errors. You'd want the corrected files saved.

Three options, in order from quickest to most thorough:

Option 1 — Have Claude Code dump the corrected SQL. Claude Code already made the fixes. Ask it to write out updated versions of the affected files (probably B2, B5, and B8) with the corrections applied. Save those as B2_v2.sql, B5_v2.sql, B8_v2.sql or just overwrite the originals.

Option 2 — Use Supabase's pg_dump to extract the current schema. You'd get a single SQL file that captures the entire deployed schema, fixes and all. Works but loses the batch organization.

Option 3 — Don't bother yet. If you're never going to redeploy from scratch, the current files are fine as historical record and the corrections live in your live database. Defer cleanup until you need to recreate.

My recommendation: Option 1. Takes 5 minutes. Means your saved schema files actually work if you ever need them.

What you should know going forward
A few practical things now that the database is real:

1. Snapshot your project. Go to your Supabase dashboard → Database → Backups. Verify daily backups are enabled. They should be by default. This protects you from accidental data loss.

2. Get your connection strings ready. You'll need three:

The connection pooler URL (for application code)
The service role key (for backend operations bypassing RLS)
The anon key (for client-side queries with RLS)
These are in Supabase project settings → API. Store them somewhere secure (1Password, env vars).

3. Don't deploy app code against this project unless you intend it to be your dev project. Earlier I recommended creating a separate "puretask-production" project. That recommendation still stands. The current project (hmynhxsdhpgoxucysqac) is good as your dev environment. Production is a separate project you create when you're closer to launch.

It only fixed actual Postgres errors. That's the behavior we wanted — narrow scope, high quality. Good signal for future tasks.

Claude responded: Good question to slow down and answer carefully.
Good question to slow down and answer carefully. Stack decisions are sticky — changing them later costs real time. Let me walk through the three sub-questions and give you straight recommendations.

Question 1: Is Next.js still the plan?
Recommendation: Yes, stay with Next.js.

Three reasons:

It's the right tool for this product. PureTask has both customer-facing pages that need SEO (the city × service landing pages from WF 41, the coverage page, the about/help/pricing pages) AND interactive app behavior (booking flow, dashboards, real-time job tracking). Next.js handles both better than alternatives. SEO pages get server-rendered HTML with great Lighthouse scores; app pages get fast client-side navigation. You don't have to compromise either way.

The ecosystem alignment is strong. Supabase has first-class Next.js support (auth helpers, server-side data fetching patterns, middleware examples). Stripe has well-trodden Next.js integration patterns. Cloudflare R2 plays nicely with Next.js for image uploads. Resend (which is Anthropic's recommendation for emails) is built by ex-Vercel folks and works seamlessly with Next.js. Almost every tutorial, template, and StackOverflow answer for what you're building assumes Next.js.

You learn a stack widely employable. If PureTask doesn't work out (sorry, but solo founders should think about this), Next.js skills transfer to any web product job. Custom React + custom backend skills don't, in the same way.

What I'd push back on if you were tempted by alternatives:

Remix: technically excellent, but smaller ecosystem and Supabase tutorials are sparser. Not worth the friction for a solo founder.
SvelteKit: Faster to write, less mature ecosystem for marketplace patterns. Skip.
Custom Express/Fastify + React SPA: More flexible, much more work. You'd spend months wiring up things Next.js gives you free.
No-code (Bubble, Webflow): Won't handle your backend complexity. Skip.
Question 2: App Router or Pages Router?
Recommendation: App Router.

This was a real debate as recently as 2024, but in 2026 it's settled. App Router is where Next.js development is happening. Pages Router still works and isn't deprecated, but it's in maintenance mode while App Router is where the new features ship.

Why App Router specifically for PureTask:

Server Components reduce client-side JavaScript. Most of your wireframes (cleaner profiles, booking details, customer dashboards) are read-heavy. Server Components fetch data on the server and ship rendered HTML. The only JS that goes to the browser is the interactive parts (booking forms, photo uploads). This means faster page loads, especially on mobile — and your wireframes are mobile-first.

Server Actions handle form submissions cleanly. When a customer books a cleaning, the data needs to: validate inputs, hit Stripe to authorize the charge, insert booking row, insert booking_state_event, send notifications. Server Actions let you write this as a single function that runs on the server, no separate API route file needed. Less boilerplate, fewer places for bugs.

Streaming and Suspense work naturally. Pages like the cleaner list (WF 8) can render the static parts instantly, then stream in the dynamic parts (cleaner cards, ratings, availability) as data loads. Better perceived performance.

The Supabase + Next.js documentation primarily covers App Router now. You'll find more current tutorials, more current AI assistance, more current StackOverflow answers.

Honest tradeoff to acknowledge: App Router has a steeper initial learning curve. The mental model of Server Components vs Client Components takes a few days to internalize. The "use server" / "use client" directives feel weird at first. Pages Router is more familiar if you've done React before. But App Router is where you should learn — investing learning time in the modern paradigm pays back immediately and indefinitely.

Specific version recommendation: Next.js 15 (latest stable). It includes Turbopack for faster dev builds and improved caching defaults that make App Router feel snappier.

Question 3: Auth provider — Clerk, Supabase Auth, or something else?
This is the harder decision because there's a real tradeoff. Let me give you both honestly.

Clerk
Strengths:

Production-ready UI components (sign in, sign up, password reset, MFA, account management)
Best-in-class developer experience for Next.js specifically
Free tier is generous (10,000 monthly active users)
Built-in support for organizations, roles, social logins
Saves you 1-2 weeks of auth UI work
Weaknesses:

Costs $25/month at 10,001 MAU and scales from there. At realistic PureTask scale (say, 5,000 customers + 200 cleaners after year 1), you'd hit paid tier
Adds a vendor dependency. If Clerk goes down or changes pricing, you're affected
Requires syncing user IDs between Clerk and your Supabase users table (the clerk_user_id column we added in B1 was for this)
Slightly more complex auth flow because you have two systems talking
Supabase Auth
Strengths:

Already part of your Supabase project — no additional vendor
Free indefinitely at any scale (included with Supabase)
User IDs are native to your database — no syncing needed
The auth.uid() function in your RLS policies works directly
Tightly integrated: a user signing up creates a row in auth.users automatically
Weaknesses:

UI components are basic. You'll write your own sign-in/sign-up pages
Account management screens (change password, MFA setup, profile editing) are DIY
Adds roughly 1-2 weeks of UI work compared to Clerk
Some advanced patterns (organizations, fine-grained roles) require more manual wiring
My honest recommendation: Supabase Auth
Three reasons:

1. Your schema is already wired for it. The RLS policies in B1-B8 use auth.uid() everywhere — that's a Supabase Auth function. With Clerk, those policies need to be modified to use a custom function that maps Clerk IDs to Supabase user rows. Workable, but extra plumbing.

2. The cost calculus changes things at scale. PureTask is a marketplace, which means user count grows quickly relative to revenue per user. At 10,000 customers + 500 cleaners (a respectable Year 1 outcome), you'd be paying Clerk meaningfully every month. Supabase Auth is free at any scale.

3. The 1-2 weeks of UI work is real but bounded. The wireframes you have already cover sign-in, sign-up, password reset, account settings. You're going to build those screens regardless of auth provider. The difference with Clerk is that you'd use their pre-built components for some of them. With Supabase Auth, you write them yourself but they match your design exactly.

The case for Clerk that I want to acknowledge: if speed-to-launch matters more than anything else, Clerk's pre-built UI saves weeks. If your goal is "launch in 8 weeks no matter what," go Clerk. If your goal is "launch in 16 weeks with full control and no vendor lock-in," go Supabase Auth.

Given you're solo, learning, and not under any external time pressure, I lean Supabase Auth. The work to build the auth UI is also great learning — you'll understand auth deeply, which serves you forever.

Important note about your existing schema: The users.clerk_user_id column we created in B1 was speculative — anticipating possible Clerk usage. If you go Supabase Auth, that column becomes unused. You could leave it (it's nullable, costs nothing) or drop it later. Either is fine.

Putting it together
The recommended stack for PureTask:

Framework: Next.js 15 with App Router
Auth: Supabase Auth (native integration with the schema we built)
Database: Supabase PostgreSQL (already deployed)
Storage: Cloudflare R2 (for photos, insurance certificates, identity documents)
Payments: Stripe (Connect for cleaner payouts, Identity for verification)
Email: Resend (for transactional notifications)
SMS: Twilio (for cleaner booking acceptance, urgent customer notifications)
Hosting: Vercel (made by Next.js company, zero-config deployment)
Background checks: Checkr (already referenced in schema)
Maps/geocoding: Mapbox or Google Maps (for ZIP-based cleaner search)
For state management: Don't pick one yet. Start with React's built-in useState and Server Components for data fetching. Add a state library (Zustand, Jotai) only if you hit a specific need.

For UI components: shadcn/ui. Copy-paste React components built on Tailwind CSS. Free, customizable, no vendor lock-in. Pairs perfectly with Next.js + App Router.

For form validation: Zod + React Hook Form. Standard pairing, well-documented, works with Server Actions.

Claude responded: Good — now I can give you a real answer to both questions.
Good — now I can give you a real answer to both questions.

Question 1: What are wireframes actually for?
Wireframes serve three distinct purposes, and it helps to be clear about which is which because they get conflated.

Purpose 1: Design specification. Wireframes show what each screen looks like, what data appears on it, what actions are available, and how it relates to other screens. They're the blueprint that says "here's what we're building" so engineering doesn't have to guess. This is the purpose people usually think of.

Purpose 2: Decision-forcing function. The act of drawing wireframes forces you to make decisions you'd otherwise leave vague. "How does the user file a dispute?" feels like an answerable question until you actually have to lay out the screen — then you realize you need to decide: what categories? what's required vs optional? where do photos attach? what happens after submission? Wireframes make abstract product decisions concrete. This is what we used them for most heavily during your design phase.

Purpose 3: Reference during implementation. When you (or Claude Code) build the actual UI, wireframes are the source of truth for what each screen needs to do. Not pixel-perfect (real UI evolves) but structurally correct.

For PureTask specifically, here's how all 70 wireframes get used in the build:

The wireframes drove the schema design (Purpose 2) — every column we created had to support a specific wireframe somewhere. That work is done. The schema is locked.

The wireframes will drive the application UI (Purpose 3) — every screen Claude Code builds should match the corresponding wireframe in structure (data shown, actions available, navigation flow), even if the visual styling differs from the rough HTML mockups.

The wireframes are not intended to be rendered exactly as drawn. They're rough HTML files for clarity, not the production UI. The real UI will be polished, properly styled with Tailwind/shadcn, and visually refined. The wireframes specify what's on each screen and how it behaves, not how it should look.

Practical consequence: when you start building, you'll reference wireframes by number ("build WF 8 next"). The wireframe tells you what data, what actions, what navigation. Tailwind/shadcn tells you how to style. Your taste tells you the final visual polish.

Question 2: Is the build sequence I outlined the right approach?
Mostly yes. With three improvements I want to flag honestly.

The sequence as I wrote it has the right spirit — start with foundational infrastructure, then build toward the core transaction (booking) so you can test the whole loop end-to-end. But it has some gaps when I look at it more critically.

What's missing or in the wrong place
Gap 1: No mention of the cleaner onboarding flow before "cleaner list page." Step 6 is "Build the cleaner list page (WF 8)" — but you can't have a cleaner list without cleaners in the database. And cleaners only get into the database via the application flow (Step 4) AND admin approval. The current sequence doesn't include admin approval tooling.

For step 6 to actually display real cleaners, you need: cleaner application submission (step 4) → admin approval interface → admin clicks approve → cleaner_profile gets created → cleaner shows up in step 6. The admin tooling is invisible in my original list.

Gap 2: No payment infrastructure before "booking flow." Step 7 is "Build the booking flow" but a real booking flow needs Stripe Connect (cleaners receiving payments) AND Stripe Customer (customers paying). Wiring Stripe alone is 1-2 weeks of work that I treated as part of the booking flow. It deserves its own step.

Gap 3: No deployment/dev environment setup. Step 1 says "Set up the Next.js project" but doesn't include the environment variables, the Vercel deployment, the connection between Vercel and your Supabase project, the dev/staging environment separation. These setup tasks are real time.

Improved build sequence
Here's what I'd recommend instead, with phases that make the dependencies clearer:

Phase 1 — Foundation (1-2 weeks)

Set up Next.js 15 project with TypeScript, Tailwind, shadcn/ui, App Router
Connect Next.js to your Supabase dev project (env vars, client setup)
Deploy to Vercel (a "hello world" deploy proves the pipeline works)
Set up the second Supabase production project (empty, just exists)
Establish folder structure conventions (where do components go, where does business logic go)
Phase 2 — Authentication (1-2 weeks) 6. Wire up Supabase Auth — email/password signup 7. Build sign-in, sign-up, password reset, email verification screens (WF 36, 37) 8. Build the basic "logged-in shell" — header, navigation, sign-out 9. Connect Supabase Auth events to your users table (trigger or callback that creates a users row when auth.users row is created)

Phase 3 — Customer onboarding (1 week) 10. Customer signup flow → creates users + customer_profiles rows 11. Customer settings page (WF 28, 29) — manage profile, addresses, preferences 12. Address management (add/edit addresses for service)

Phase 4 — Cleaner onboarding pipeline (2-3 weeks) 13. Cleaner application form (multi-step, WF 33-37 plus the unbuilt 11-step form) 14. Application submission → creates cleaner_applications row in 'submitted' state 15. Stripe Identity verification integration 16. Checkr background check integration 17. Stripe Connect onboarding (WF 63) 18. Admin application review interface (WF 55) — approve/reject/request info

Phase 5 — Browse and discovery (1-2 weeks) 19. Customer homepage (WF 1) — service catalog, recent bookings, recommendations 20. Service browsing (WF 8 cleaner list) — the heavy query, with filters

Phase 6 — Booking transaction (3-4 weeks, the biggest) 21. Stripe customer payment method setup 22. Booking flow (selecting service, time, cleaner, address) 23. Booking creation transaction (insert booking + booking_state_event + Stripe authorize) 24. Cleaner-side booking acceptance (WF 3, 4-hour SLA) 25. Active job tracking (WF 4, 5, 9 — on the way, arrived, in progress, completing) 26. Approve and pay flow (WF 10) with Stripe charge capture 27. Recurring booking setup (WF 21, 22)

Phase 7 — Trust and quality systems (2-3 weeks) 28. Review submission flow (WF 20) with traits and tip option 29. Cleaner reliability scoring job (nightly batch reading reliability_events) 30. Tier transition job (14-day sustained rule) 31. Badge and specialty earning jobs 32. Cleaner dashboard (WF 2, 2c, 2d) — score widget, breakdown, probation states

Phase 8 — Disputes and support (1-2 weeks) 33. Dispute filing (WF 16) and response (WF 17) 34. Admin dispute mediation (WF 56, 57) 35. Support ticket creation (WF 47)

Phase 9 — Money operations (1-2 weeks) 36. Friday weekly payout batch job (Stripe Connect transfers) 37. Cleaner earnings page (WF 6) 38. Refund processing (WF 62) 39. Insurance verification flow (WF 32)

Phase 10 — Polish and pre-launch (2-3 weeks) 40. Notifications system (push, email via Resend, SMS via Twilio) 41. SEO landing pages (WF 41, 46) 42. Marketing pages (about, pricing, help, FAQ — WF 42-45) 43. Empty states, error states, loading states (WF 38, 39, 40) 44. First-time user tours (WF 48, 49, 50)

Total estimated time: 16-24 weeks for a solo founder learning Next.js as you go.

Why I structured it this way
Three principles drove the resequencing:

Principle 1: You should be able to do an end-to-end test as early as possible. That means a complete loop from "customer signs up" to "customer books a cleaning" to "cleaner accepts and gets paid" needs to be possible by Phase 6 at the latest. Everything before Phase 6 enables that loop.

Principle 2: Admin tooling is built earlier than feels natural. You can't approve cleaners without admin tools, and you can't have a marketplace without cleaners. So Phase 4 includes the basic admin approval interface even though "polishing admin tools" is much later.

Principle 3: The hard integrations come in the middle, not the end. Stripe Connect is hard. Checkr is hard. Identity verification is hard. Putting them at the end makes launch unpredictable. Putting them in Phase 4 means you tackle the risky parts when you have momentum and can afford to debug.

What I want to be honest about
A few things in this sequence that deserve flagging:

The estimates are optimistic. Solo founder + new framework + first marketplace project usually takes 1.5-2x longer than optimistic estimates. 16-24 weeks could realistically be 24-40 weeks. That's not a failure — that's normal.

You'll probably want to skip some Phase 10 items for MVP. SEO landing pages, FAQ pages, first-time tours — these can ship after launch. Don't let scope creep push your launch date.

Phase 6 (booking flow) will be the hardest 3-4 weeks of your build. It touches Stripe (multi-step charge flow), the database (multi-table transaction), state management (booking states), real-time updates (cleaner sees new bookings), notifications (multiple parties), and the EXCLUSION CONSTRAINT we built (handling double-booking errors gracefully). Everything else is easier.

You may want a co-pilot. Even if you stay solo on the company, having a developer friend look at your code occasionally, or paying for ~5-10 hours of senior contractor review at key milestones, prevents you from going deep into bad patterns alone. Worth considering.

Two corrections to my earlier sequence
Comparing my original 8-step list to this new phased plan, I want to call out the two ways my original was wrong:

I've now done the deep review. Let me give you the honest analysis.

My honest finding
The 10-phase plan I gave you is structurally sound but has real gaps when measured against everything we've actually decided across our work. I missed things. Some are minor, some are actually important.

Let me walk through what holds up, what's missing, and what needs to change.

What the plan got right
The big-picture sequencing is correct:

Foundation → Auth → Customer onboarding → Cleaner onboarding → Browse → Booking → Trust → Disputes → Money → Polish
Admin tooling pulled earlier than feels natural (necessary because cleaners need approval)
Hard integrations (Stripe Connect, Checkr, Identity) in middle phases, not at the end
End-to-end loop (book → accept → pay) achievable by Phase 6
That spine is correct. Now the gaps.

What's missing or wrong
Gap 1: Geofencing and GPS tracking infrastructure has no phase
We spent significant time designing the GPS system: 3-minute pings during transit, 100m geofence trigger for clock-in, location pings only at clock-in/clock-out during the job, GPS-spoofing mitigations (cross-check IP geolocation, photo within 15 minutes of clock-in). This is foundational to your trust differentiation — and I never assigned it a phase.

Where it goes: Phase 6 (Booking transaction) needs explicit GPS infrastructure as a sub-phase. Specifically, between steps 24 (cleaner accepts) and 25 (active job tracking).

Gap 2: The "On my way" page is treated as one screen but it's a system
We designed this as: enables 2 hours before service, reveals entry instructions, triggers GPS, sends customer push, has running-late escape hatch, has in-app messaging button, expires entry instructions 4 hours after booking ends. That's not one screen — that's a multi-screen state machine with backend logic. It's currently buried in "step 25 active job tracking."

Where it goes: Phase 6 needs an explicit "On my way / pre-arrival" sub-phase before active job tracking.

Gap 3: Photo system is underspecified
We locked: required photos per service type (varies by Standard/Deep/Move-out/Airbnb), 15-minute first-photo rule, photos required before clock-out gate, customer photo opt-out flow with dispute waiver, photo etiquette training during cleaner onboarding, encryption at rest, 90-day deletion window, customer notes about rooms to skip. My plan treats this as part of "active job tracking" — it's actually a major subsystem with legal implications.

Where it goes: Photo infrastructure deserves its own sub-phase in Phase 6, and the legal/CCPA piece needs Phase 0 work (legal docs).

Gap 4: In-app messaging system is missing entirely
We agreed cleaners and customers communicate through in-app messages (encrypted, scoped to active bookings, 4-hour response SLA tracked for reliability scoring). I never put this anywhere in the build plan. It's not optional — the booking flow doesn't work without it.

Where it goes: Phase 6, before active job tracking. Messaging is infrastructure, not polish.

Gap 5: Two-track scoring system isn't broken out properly
We designed:

Performance Score (statistical, daily refresh, 6 weighted metrics)
Tier Standing (insulated, requires 14-day sustained performance to drop or climb)
Veteran cushion (6 months at 75+ = 30-day window + manual review on first drop)
Tier-drop appeals (48-hour notice, human review)
My plan has "step 29 cleaner reliability scoring nightly batch" and "step 30 tier transition job." That's a single sentence for what's actually a complex distributed system with notifications, history tracking, and an appeals workflow. This will take 2-3 weeks of focused work, not be a quick item in Phase 7.

Where it goes: Phase 7 needs to be reframed. It's not "step 29 + step 30" — it's a multi-week subsystem build.

Gap 6: Match Score algorithm is invisible in the plan
We designed: 6-factor weighted Match Score (distance 25%, tier×score 25%, availability fit 20%, recency 10%, customer affinity 10%, spotlight boost 10%), tied results broken by daily-rotating random seed, "Why am I seeing this cleaner?" disclosure on every card, cleaner-side "Your visibility in customer searches" widget. This is the engine of cleaner discovery and I have it as "step 20 service browsing (WF 8 cleaner list with filters)." That's massively underscoping it.

Where it goes: Phase 5 needs a dedicated Match Score sub-phase. The algorithm itself can launch with simple weights and refine over time — but the disclosure UI and cleaner visibility widget can't be retrofitted.

Gap 7: New cleaner spotlight rotation is missing
We agreed on dynamic spotlight allocation: 3-4 slots per ZIP per day expanding to 6-8 under supply pressure, 7-day minimum visibility guarantee per new cleaner, $15 first-cleaning customer incentive, 6-job cap for spotlight pool. This is a marketplace cold-start mechanism — without it, new cleaners can't get their first jobs and your supply side stalls.

Where it goes: Phase 5, alongside the Match Score work.

Gap 8: ZIP-locked badges and the 25-badge system
You loved the ZIP-locked badges and we fleshed them out fully. We also catalogued ~25 badges across 6 categories (Trust, Performance, Customer Relationship, ZIP-locked, Specialty, Veteran). My plan has "step 31 badge and specialty earning jobs" — one line for a 25-badge system with renewal logic, anti-gaming, ZIP-scoped display, and tier interactions. Underscoped by an order of magnitude.

Where it goes: Phase 7, broken into sub-phases (badge schema → earning logic → display rules → renewal/decay).

Gap 9: Customer reliability scoring and visibility logic
We designed customer scores too, with band-not-number visibility to cleaners, hard triggers (3 no-shows in 90 days = restricted to request-to-book), and cleaner ratings of customers post-job. My plan only mentions cleaner scoring. Customer scoring is a separate system that needs its own implementation.

Where it goes: Phase 7, parallel to cleaner scoring work.

Gap 10: The pricing structure logic isn't accounted for
We locked: tier-scaled commission (12% Rising Pro for first 6 jobs, then 15%/13%/11%/10% by tier), $9.99 customer Trust fee, weekly Friday payouts, 5% instant payout fee. This requires real backend logic — the commission calculation must look at the cleaner's tier at the moment of payout, the cleaner's job count to know if they're still in the 12% Rising Pro window, and the apology credit system ($15 first-cleaning incentive). My plan has "step 36 Friday weekly payout batch job" — one line for the entire payout calculation engine.

Where it goes: Phase 9 needs a tier-aware commission calculation sub-phase before the payout batch job.

Gap 11: Dispute system is underscoped
We designed: 48-hour dispute window after approval, 24-hour customer review window before auto-approval, cleaner offers (re-clean / partial refund / stand by work), admin mediation interface, dispute pattern affecting customer reliability score. My plan has steps 33-35 covering this in "1-2 weeks." It's actually closer to 3 weeks of work.

Where it goes: Phase 8 timeline needs to expand.

Gap 12: Recurring bookings are treated as one step
We designed: default cadence every 2 weeks, charged per cleaning not upfront, 14-day cleaner-side cancellation notice, customer can pause/skip/cancel, cleaners see recurring as separate inbox stream, smart cadence defaults by service type (weekly for Airbnb, biweekly for Standard, 8-12 weeks for Deep). My plan has "step 27 recurring booking setup (WF 21, 22)" buried in Phase 6. Recurring is its own product flow worthy of dedicated time.

Where it goes: Either expand step 27 in Phase 6, or pull it into a small dedicated Phase 6.5.

Gap 13: Emergency support tickets and AQI integration
We agreed: 4-tier ticket categorization (Standard / Job-specific / Dispute / Emergency), 1-hour SLA for emergencies, separate red "Report emergency" button on active job screen, PurpleAir AQI integration for wildfire auto-flagging in NorCal. This is critical for your launch geography. My plan has "step 35 support ticket creation" — single line, no AQI integration mentioned.

Where it goes: Phase 8 should explicitly include AQI integration as a NorCal-launch-readiness item.

Gap 14: No-show automation and reliability event triggering
We designed: 15-minute reminder push to cleaner, 30-minute customer notification with free-cancel option, 45-minute auto-no-show with full refund, 5 in 60 days = 1-week fixed suspension, hard triggers bypass gradual scoring. This requires scheduled jobs, real-time triggers, automated refunds via Stripe, and notification orchestration. My plan touches it under "active job tracking" but doesn't name it.

Where it goes: Phase 6 needs explicit no-show automation sub-phase.

Gap 15: Pre-Phase 1 items are too thin
I listed 3 pre-Phase 1 items. There are actually more:

Have Claude Code dump corrected B2/B5/B8 SQL files (✓ I had this)
Create empty puretask-production Supabase project (✓ I had this)
Decide on co-pilot (✓ I had this)
Missing: Create CCPA-compliant photo policy with a real lawyer — required before any photo capture goes live
Missing: Cleaner ToS draft covering scoring system, suspension rules, badges
Missing: Customer ToS draft covering data, photos, dispute rights
Missing: Insurance research — platform-level liability vs. cleaner-required coverage
Missing: Decide on apology credit fund — the $15 first-cleaning incentive needs accounting setup
The legal items aren't optional. CCPA compliance specifically can't be retrofitted after launch.

Honest revised time estimate
Original: 16-24 weeks. Revised: 24-36 weeks for solo founder learning Next.js. Realistically 30-45 weeks accounting for learning curve, lawyer cycles, and integration debugging. That's 7-10 months of focused work.

That's not a failure — it matches your earlier acknowledgment that the path is roughly 9 months out from launch, total cost ~$3,000-3,500. The plan now matches the reality.

What I want to flag honestly
Three things you should know:

One. This is a complex marketplace. The schema we built has 54 tables for a reason — there's a lot of system here. The plan reflects that. If anything in here makes you want to scope down, now is the time to say so. We could ship a simpler V1 (skip badges, skip ZIP-locked logic, skip recurring bookings, skip new cleaner spotlight) and hit 4-5 months instead of 9. The schema supports both paths.

Two. The legal pre-work (CCPA-compliant photo policy, ToS drafts, insurance) genuinely cannot be skipped in California. Building photo capture without the policy first is a regulatory liability. Find a lawyer in week 1, not week 30.

Three. The "Phase 6 booking transaction" is now realistically 5-6 weeks of work. It's the hardest single phase in the build. Half your bugs will live there. Plan for it.

Tell me where you stand and we move.
